<content>ï»¿The White Ladies of Worcester

CHAPTER I

THE SUBTERRANEAN WAY

The slanting rays of afternoon sunshine, pouring through stone arches, lay in broad, golden bands, upon the flags of the Convent cloister.</content>
<content>The old lay-sister, Mary Antony, stepped from the cool shade of the cell passage and, blinking at the sunshine, shuffled slowly to her appointed post at the top of the crypt steps, up which would shortly pass the silent procession of nuns returning from Vespers.</content>
<content>Daily they went, and daily they returned, by the underground way, a
passage over a mile in length, leading from the Nunnery of the White
Ladies at Whytstone in Claines, to the Church of St. Mary and St.
Peter, the noble Cathedral within the walls of the city of Worcester.</content>
<content>Entering this passage from the crypt in their own cloisters, they walked in darkness below the sunny meadows, passed beneath the Fore-gate, moving in silent procession under the busy streets, until they reached the crypt of the Cathedral.</content>
<content>From the crypt, a winding stairway in the wall led up to a chamber above the choir, whence, unseeing and unseen, the White Ladies of Worcester daily heard the holy monks below chant Vespers.</content>
<content>To Sister Mary Antony fell the task of counting the five-and-twenty veiled figures, as they passed down the steps and disappeared beneath the ground, and of again counting them as they reappeared, and moved in stately silence along the cloister, each entering her own cell, to spend, in prayer and adoration, the hours until the Refectory bell should call them to the evening meal.</content>
<content>This counting of the White Ladies dated from the day, now more than half a century ago, when Sister Agatha, weakened by prolonged fasting, and chancing to walk last in the procession, fainted and, falling silently, remained behind, unnoticed, in the solitude and darkness.</content>
<content>It was the habit of this saintly lady to abide in her own cell after Vespers, dispensing with the evening meal; thus her absence was not discovered until the following morning when Mary Antony, finding the cell empty, hastened to report that Sister Agatha having long, like Enoch, walked with God, had, even, as Enoch, been translated!</content>
<content>The nuns who flocked to the cell, inclining to Mary Antony's view of the strange happening, kneeled upon the floor before the empty couch, and worshipped.</content>
<content>The Prioress of that time, however, being of a practical turn of mind, ordered the immediate lighting of the lanterns, and herself descended to search the underground way.</content>
<content>She did not need to go far.</content>
<content>The saintly spirit of Sister Agatha had indeed been translated.</content>
<content>They found her frail body lying prone against the door, the hands broken and torn by much wild beating upon its studded panels.</content>
<content>She had run to and fro in the dank darkness, beating first upon the door beneath the Convent cloisters, then upon the door, a mile away, leading into the Cathedral crypt.</content>
<content>But the nuns were shut into their cells, beyond the cloister; the good people of Worcester city slept peacefully, not dreaming of the despairing figure running to and fro beneath them tottering, stumbling, falling, arising to fall again, yet hurrying blindly onwards; and the Cathedral Sacristan, when questioned, confessed that, hearing cries and rappings coming from the crypt at a late hour, he speedily locked the outer gate, said an "Ave," and went home to supper; well knowing that, at such a time, none save spirits of evil would be wandering below, in so great torment.</content>
<content>Thus, through much tribulation, poor Sister Agatha entered into rest; being held in deepest reverence ever after.</content>
<content>More than fifty years had gone by.</content>
<content>The Prioress of that day, and most of those who walked in that procession, had long lain beside Sister Agatha in the Convent burying-ground.</content>
<content>But Mary Antony, now oldest of the lay-sisters, never failed to make careful count, as each veiled figure passed, nor to impart the mournful reason for this necessity to all new-comers.</content>
<content>So that the nun whose turn it was to walk last in the procession, prayed that she might not hear behind her the running feet of Sister Agatha; while none went alone into the cloisters after dark, lest they should hear the poor thin hands of Sister Agatha beating upon the panels of the door.</content>
<content>Thus does the anguish of a tortured brain leave its imperishable impress upon the surroundings in which the mind once suffered, though the freed spirit may have long forgotten, in the peace of Paradise, that slight affliction, which was but for a moment, through which it passed to the eternal weight of glory.</content>
<content>Of late, the old lay-sister, Mary Antony, had grown fearful lest she should make mistake in this solemn office of the counting.</content>
<content>Therefore, in the secret of her own heart, she devised a plan, which she carried out under cover of her scapulary.</content>
<content>Twenty-five dried peas she held ready in her wallet; then, as each veiled figure, having mounted the steps leading from the crypt doorway, moved slowly past her, she dropped a pea with her right hand into her left.</content>
<content>When all the holy Ladies had passed, if all had returned, five-and-twenty peas lay in her left hand, none remained in the wallet.</content>
<content>This secret dropping of peas became a kind of game to Mary Antony.</content>
<content>She kept the peas in a small linen bag, and often took them out and played with them when alone in her cell, placing them all in a row, and settling, to her own satisfaction, which peas should represent the various holy Ladies.</content>
<content>A large white pea, of finer aspect than the rest, stood for the noble Prioress herself; a somewhat shrivelled pea, hard, brown, and wizened, did duty as Mother Sub-Prioress, an elderly nun, not loved by Mary Antony because of her sharp tongue and strict fault-finding ways; while a pale and speckled pea became Sister Mary Rebecca, held in high scorn by the old lay-sister, as a traitress, sneak, and liar, for if ever tale of wrong or shame was whispered in the Convent, it could be traced for place of origin to the slanderous tongue and crooked mind of Sister Mary Rebecca.</content>
<content>When all the peas in line upon the floor of her cell were named, old Mary Antony marked out a distant flagstone, on which the sunlight fell, as heaven; another, partially in shadow, purgatory; a third, in a far corner of exceeding darkness, hell.</content>
<content>She then proceeded, with well-directed fillip of thumb and middle finger, to send the holy Ladies there where, in her judgment, they belonged.</content>
<content>If the game went well, the noble Prioress landed safely in heaven, without even the most transitory visit to purgatory; Mother Sub-Prioress, rolling into purgatory, remained there; while the pale and speckled pea went straight to hell!</content>
<content>When these were safely landed, Mary Antony rubbed her hands and, chuckling gleefully, finished the game at gay hap-hazard, it being of less importance where the rest of the holy Ladies chanced to go.</content>
<content>CHAPTER II

SISTER MARY ANTONY DISCOURSES

As Mary Antony shuffled slowly from the shadow into the sunshine, a gay little flutter of wings preceded her, and a robin perched upon the parapet behind the stone seat upon which it was the lay-sister's custom to await the sound of the turning of the key in the lock of the heavy door beneath the cloisters.</content>
<content>"Thou good-for-nothing imp!"</content>
<content>exclaimed Mary Antony, her old face crinkling with delight.</content>
<content>"Thou little vain man, in thy red jerkin!</content>
<content>Beshrew thine impudence, intruding into a place where women alone do dwell, and no male thing may enter.</content>
<content>I would have thee take warning by the fate of the baker's boy, who dared to climb into a tree, so that he might peep over the wall and spy upon the holy Ladies in their garden.</content>
<content>Boasting afterward of that which he had done, and making merry over that which he pretended to have seen, our great Lord Bishop heard of it, and sent and took that baker's boy, and though he cried for mercy, swearing the whole tale was an empty boast, they put out his bold eyes with heated tongs, and hanged him from the very branches he had climbed.</content>
<content>They'd do the like to thee, thou little vain man, if Mary Antony reported on thy ways.</content>
<content>Wouldst like to hang, in thy red doublet?"</content>
<content>The robin had heard this warning tale many times already, told by old
Mary Antony with infinite variety.</content>
<content>Sometimes the tongue of the baker's boy was cut out at the roots; sometimes he lost his ears, or again, he was tied to a cart-tail, and flogged through the Tything.</content>
<content>Often he became a pieman, and once he was a turnspit in the household of the Lord Bishop himself.</content>
<content>But, whatever the preliminaries, and whether baker, pieman, or turnspit, his final catastrophe was always the same: he was hanged from a bough of the very tree into which, impious and greatly daring, he had climbed.</content>
<content>This was an ancient tale.</content>
<content>All who might vouch for it, saving the old lay-sister, had passed away; and, of late, Mary Antony had been strictly forbidden by the Reverend Mother, to tell it to new-comers, or to speak of it to any of the nuns.</content>
<content>So, daily, she told it to the robin; and he, being neither baker's lad, pieman, nor turnspit, and having a conscience void of offence, would listen, wholly unafraid; then, hopping nearer to Mary Antony, would look up at her, eager inquiry in his bright eyes.</content>
<content>On this particular afternoon he flew up into the very tree climbed by the prying and ill-fated baker's lad, settled on a bough which branched out over the Convent wall, and poured forth a gay trill of song.</content>
<content>"Ha, thou little vain man, in thy brown and red suit!"</content>
<content>chuckled Mary Antony, leaning her gnarled hands on the stone parapet, as she stood framed in one of the cloister arches overlooking the garden.</content>
<content>"Is that thy little 'grace before meat'?</content>
<content>But, I pray thee, Sir Robin, who said there was cheese in my wallet?</content>
<content>Nay, is there like to be cheese in a wallet already containing five-and-twenty holy Ladies on their way back from Vespers?</content>
<content>Out upon thee for a most irreverent little glutton!</content>
<content>I fear me thou hast not only a high look, thou hast also a proud stomach; just the reverse of the great French Cardinal who came, with much pomp, to visit us at Easter time.</content>
<content>He had a proud look and a Come down again, thou little naughty man, and I will tell thee what the Lord Cardinal had under his crimson sash.</content>
<content>'Tis not a thing to shout to the tree-tops.</content>
<content>I might have to recite ten Paternosters, if I let thee tempt me so to do.</content>
<content>For whispering it in thine ear, I should but say one; for having remarked it, none at all.</content>
<content>Facts are facts; and, even in the case of so weighty a fact, the responsibility rests not upon the beholder."</content>
<content>Mary Antony leaned over the parapet, looking upward.</content>
<content>The afternoon sunlight fell full upon the russet parchment of her kind old face, shewing the web of wrinkles spun by ninety years of the gently turning wheel of time.</content>
<content>But the robin, perched upon the bough, trilled and sang, unmoved.</content>
<content>He was weary of tales of bakers and piemen.</content>
<content>He was not at all curious as to what had been beneath the French Cardinal's crimson sash.</content>
<content>He wanted the tasty morsels which he knew lay concealed in Sister Mary Antony's leathern wallet.</content>
<content>So he stayed on the bough and sang.</content>
<content>The old face, peering up from between the pillars, softened into tenderness at the robin's song.</content>
<content>"I cannot let thy little grace return unto thee void," she said, and fumbled at the fastenings of her wallet.</content>
<content>A flick of wings, a flash of red.</content>
<content>The robin had dropped from the bough, and perched beside her.</content>
<content>She doled out crumbs, and fragments of cheese, pushing them toward him along the parapet; leaving her fingers near, to see how close he would adventure to her hand.</content>
<content>She watched him peck a morsel of cheese into five tiny pieces, then fly, with full beak, on eager wing, to the hidden nest, from which five gaping mouths shrieked a shrill and hungry welcome.</content>
<content>Then, back again swift as an arrow from the archer's bow noting, with bright eye, and head turned sidewise, that the hand resting on the coping had moved nearer; yet brave to take all risks for the sake of those yellow beaks, which would gape wide, in expectation, at sound of the beat of his wings.</content>
<content>"Feed thyself, thou little worldling!"</content>
<content>chuckled old Antony, and covered the remaining bits of cheese with her hand.</content>
<content>"Who art thou to come here presuming to teach thy betters lessons of self-sacrifice?</content>
<content>First feed thyself; then give to the hungry, the fragments that remain.</content>
<content>Had I five squealing children here which Heaven forbid I should eat mine own mess, and count myself charitable if I let them lick the dish.</content>
<content>The holy Ladies give to the poor at the Convent gate, that for which they have no further use.</content>
<content>Does thy jaunty fatherhood presume to shame our saintly celibacy?</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress did chide me sharply because, to a poor soul with many hungry mouths to feed, I gave a good piece of venison, and not the piece which was tainted.</content>
<content>Truth to tell, I had already made away with the tainted piece; but Mother Sub-Prioress was pleased to think it was in the pot, seething for the holy Ladies' evening meal; and wherefore should Mother Sub-Prioress not think as she pleased?</content>
<content>"'Woman!'</content>
<content>she cried; 'Woman!'</content>
<content>and when Mother Sub-Prioress says
'Woman!'</content>
<content>the woman she addresses feels her estate would be higher had
God Almighty been pleased to have let her be the Man, or even the
Serpent, so much contempt does Mother Sub-Prioress infuse into the
name 'Woman!'</content>
<content>said Mother Sub-Prioress, 'wouldst thou make all the
Ladies of the Convent ill?'</content>
<content>"'Nay,' said I, 'that would I not.</content>
<content>Yet, if any needs must be ill, 'twere easier to tend the holy Ladies in their cells, than the Poor, in humble homes, outside the Convent walls, tossing on beds of rushes.'</content>
<content>"'Tush, fool!'</content>
<content>snarled Mother Sub-Prioress.</content>
<content>"'The Poor are not easily made ill.'

"Tush indeed!</content>
<content>I tell thee, little bright-eyed man, old Antony, can 'tush' to better purpose!</content>
<content>That night there were strong purging herbs in the broth of Mother Sub-Prioress.</content>
<content>Yet she did but keep her bed for one day.</content>
<content>Like the Poor, she is not easily made ill!</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Well, have thy way; only peck not my fingers, Master Robin, or I will have thee flogged through the Tything at the cart-tail, as was done to a certain pieman, whose history I will now relate.</content>
<content>"Once upon a time, when Sister Mary Antony was young, and fair to look upon Nay, wink not thy naughty eye "

At that moment came the sound of a key turning slowly in the lock of the door at the bottom of the steps leading from the crypt to the cloister.</content>
<content>CHAPTER III

THE PRIORESS PASSES

A key turned slowly in the lock of the oaken door at the entrance to the underground way.</content>
<content>The old lay-sister seized her wallet and pulled out the bag of peas.</content>
<content>Below, the heavy door swung back upon its hinges.</content>
<content>Mary Antony dropped upon her knees to the right of the steps, her hands hidden beneath her scapulary, her eyes bent in lowly reverence upon the sunlit flagstones, her lips mumbling chance sentences from the Psalter.</content>
<content>The measured sound of softly moving feet drew near, slightly shuffling as they reached the steps and began to mount, up from the mile-long darkness, into the sunset light.</content>
<content>First to appear was a young lay-sister, carrying a lantern.</content>
<content>Hastening up the steps, she extinguished the flame, grown sickly in the sunshine, placed the lantern in a niche, and, dropping upon her knees, opposite old Mary Antony, sought to join in the latter's pious recitations.</content>
<content>"Adhaesit pavimento anima mea," chanted Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"Wherefore are the holy Ladies late to-day?"</content>
<content>"One fell to weeping in the darkness," intoned the young lay-sister, "whereupon Mother Sub-Prioress caused all to stand still while she strove, by the light of my lantern held high, to discover who had burst forth with a sob.</content>
<content>None shewing traces of tears, she gave me back the lantern, herself walking last in the line, as all moved on."</content>
<content>"Convertentur ad vesperam, and the devil catch the hindmost," chanted Mary Antony, with fervour.</content>
<content>"Amen," intoned Sister Abigail, eyes bent upon the ground; for the tall figure of the Prioress, mounting the steps, now came into view.</content>
<content>The Prioress passed up the cloister with a stately grace of motion which, even beneath the heavy cloth of her white robe, revealed the noble length of supple limbs.</content>
<content>Her arms hung by her sides, swaying gently as she walked.</content>
<content>There was a look of strength and of restfulness about the long fingers and beautifully moulded hands.</content>
<content>Her face, calm and purposeful, was lifted to the sunlight.</content>
<content>Suffering and sorrow had left thereon indelible marks; but the clear grey eyes, beneath level brows, were luminous with a light betokening the victory of a pure and noble spirit over passionate and most human flesh.</content>
<content>No sinner, in her presence, ever felt crushed by hopeless weight of sin; no saint, before the gaze of her calm eyes, felt sure of being altogether faultless.</content>
<content>So truly was she woman, that all humanity seemed lifted to her level; so completely was she saint, that sin did slink away abashed before her coming.</content>
<content>They who feared her most, were most conscious of her kindness.</content>
<content>They who loved her best, were least able to venture near.</content>
<content>In the first bloom of her womanhood she had left the world, resigning high rank, fair lands, and the wealth which makes for power.</content>
<content>Her faith in human love having been rudely shattered, she had sought security in Divine compassion, and consolation in the daily contemplation of the Man of Sorrows.</content>
<content>In her cell, on a rough wooden cross, hung a life-size figure of the dying Saviour.</content>
<content>She had not reached her twenty-fifth year when, fleeing from the world, she joined the Order of the White Ladies of Worcester, and passed into the seclusion and outward calm of the Nunnery at Whytstone.</content>
<content>Five years later, on the death of the aged Prioress, she was elected, by a large majority, to fill the vacant place.</content>
<content>She had now, during two years, ruled the Nunnery wisely and well.</content>
<content>She had ruled her own spirit, even better.</content>
<content>She had won the victory over the World and the Flesh; there remained but the Devil.</content>
<content>The Devil, alas, always remains.</content>
<content>As she moved, with uplifted brow and mien of calm detachment, along the sunlit cloister to the lofty, stone passage, within, the Convent, she was feared by many, loved by most, and obeyed by all.</content>
<content>And, as she passed, old Mary Antony, bowing almost to the ground, dropped a large white pea, from between her right thumb and finger, into the horny palm of her left hand.</content>
<content>Behind the Prioress there followed a nun, tall also, but ungainly.</content>
<content>Her short-sighted eyes peered shiftily to right and left; her long nose went on before, scenting possible scandal and wrong-doing; her weak lips let loose a ready smile, insinuating, crafty, apologetic.</content>
<content>She walked with hands crossed upon her breast, in attitude of adoration and humility.</content>
<content>As she moved by, old Mary Antony let drop the pale and speckled pea.</content>
<content>Keeping their distances, mostly with shrouded faces, bent heads, and folded hands, all the White Ladies passed.</content>
<content>Each went in silence to her cell, there kneeling in prayer and contemplation until the Refectory bell should call to the evening meal.</content>
<content>As the last, save one, went by, the keen eyes of the old lay-sister noted that her hands were clenched against her breast, that she stumbled at the topmost step, and caught her breath with a half sob.</content>
<content>Behind her, moving quickly, came the spare form of the Sub-Prioress, ferret-faced, alert, vigilant; fearful lest sin should go unpunished; wishful to be the punisher.</content>
<content>She must have heard the half-strangled sob burst from the slight figure stumbling up the steps before her, had not old Mary Antony been suddenly moved at that moment to uplift her voice in a cracked and raucous "Amen."</content>
<content>Startled, and vexed at being startled, the Sub-Prioress turned upon
Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"Peace, woman!"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"The Convent cloister is not a hen-yard.</content>
<content>Such ill-timed devotion well-nigh merits penance.</content>
<content>Rise from thy knees, and go at once about thy business."</content>
<content>The Sub-Prioress hastened on.</content>
<content>Scowling darkly, old Antony bent forward, looking, past Mother
Sub-Prioress, up the cloister to the distant passage.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Seraphine had reached her cell.</content>
<content>The door was shut.</content>
<content>Old Antony's knees creaked as she arose, but her wizened face was once more cheerful.</content>
<content>"Beans in her broth to-night," she said.</content>
<content>"One for 'woman'; another for the hen-yard; a third for threatening penance when I did but chant a melodious 'Amen.'</content>
<content>I'll give her beans castor beans!"</content>
<content>Down the steps she went, pushed the heavy door to, locked it, and drew forth the key; then turned her steps toward the cell of the Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>On her way thither, she paused at a certain door and listened, her ear against the oaken panel.</content>
<content>Then she hurried onward, knocked upon the door of the Reverend Mother's cell and, being bidden to enter, passed within, closed the door behind her, and dropped upon her knees.</content>
<content>The Prioress stood beside the casement, gazing at the golden glory of the sunset.</content>
<content>She was, for the moment, unconscious of her surroundings.</content>
<content>Her mind was away behind those crimson battlements.</content>
<content>Presently she turned and saw the old woman, kneeling at the door.</content>
<content>"How now, dear Antony?"</content>
<content>she said, kindly.</content>
<content>"Get up!</content>
<content>Hang the key in its appointed place, and make me thy report.</content>
<content>Have all returned?</content>
<content>As always, is all well?"</content>
<content>The old lay-sister rose, hung the massive key upon a nail; then came to the feet of the Prioress, and knelt again.</content>
<content>"Reverend Mother," she said, "all who went forth have returned.</content>
<content>But all is not well.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Seraphine is uttering wild cries in her cell; and much I fear me, Mother Sub-Prioress may pass by, and hear her."</content>
<content>The face of the Prioress grew stern and sad; yet, withal, tender.</content>
<content>She raised the lay-sister, and gently patted the old hands which trembled.</content>
<content>"Go thy ways, dear Antony," she said.</content>
<content>"I myself will visit the little
Sister in her cell.</content>
<content>None will attempt to enter while I am there."</content>
<content>CHAPTER IV

"GIVE ME TENDERNESS," SHE SAID

The Prioress knelt before a marble group of the Virgin and Child, placed where the rays of evening sunshine, entering through the western casement, played over its white beauty, shedding a radiance on the pure face of the Madonna, and a halo of golden glory around the Infant Christ.</content>
<content>"Mother of God," prayed the Prioress, with folded hands, "give me patience in dealing with wilfulness; grant me wisdom to cope with unreason; may it be given me to share the pain of this heart in torment, even as when thou didst witness the sufferings of thy dear Son, our Lord, on Calvary a sword pierced through thine own soul also.</content>
<content>"Give me this gift of sympathy with suffering, though the cross be not mine own, but another's.</content>
<content>"But give me firmness and authority: even as when thou didst say to the servants at Cana: 'Whatsoever He saith unto you, do it.'"</content>
<content>The Prioress waited, with bowed head.</content>
<content>Then, of a sudden she put forth her hand, and touched the marble foot of the Babe.</content>
<content>"Give me tenderness," she said.</content>
<content>CHAPTER V

THE WAYWARD NUN

Sister Mary Seraphine lay prone upon the floor of her cell.</content>
<content>Tightly clenched in her hands were fragments of her torn veil.</content>
<content>She beat her knuckles upon the stones with rhythmic regularity; then, when her arms would lift no longer, took up the measure with her toes, in wild imitation of a galloping horse.</content>
<content>As she lay, she repeated with monotonous reiteration: "Trappings of crimson, and silver bells: mane and tail, like foam of the waves; a palfrey as white as snow!"</content>
<content>The Prioress entered, closed the door behind her, and looked searchingly at the prostrate figure; then, lifting the master-key which hung from her girdle, locked the door on the inside.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Seraphine had been silent long enough to hear the closing and locking of the door.</content>
<content>Now she started afresh.</content>
<content>"Trappings of crimson, and silver bells "

The Prioress walked over to the narrow casement, and stood looking out at the rosy clouds wreathing a pale green sky.</content>
<content>"Oh!</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Oh!</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Oh!</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>."</content>
<content>wailed Sister Mary Seraphine, writhing upon the floor; "mane and tail, like foam of the waves; a palfrey as white as snow!"</content>
<content>The Prioress watched the swallows on swift wing, chasing flies in the evening light.</content>
<content>So complete was the silence, that Sister Mary Seraphine notwithstanding that turning of the key in the lock fancied she must be alone.</content>
<content>"Trappings of crimson, and silver bells!"</content>
<content>she declaimed with vehemence; then lifted her face to peep, and saw the tall figure of the Prioress standing at the casement.</content>
<content>Instantly, Sister Mary Seraphine dropped her head.</content>
<content>"Mane and tail," she began then her courage failed; the "foam of the waves" quavered into indecision; and indecision, in such a case, is fatal.</content>
<content>For a while she lay quite still, moaning plaintively, then, of a sudden, quivered from head to foot, starting up alert, as if to listen.</content>
<content>"Wilfred!"</content>
<content>she shrieked; "Wilfred!</content>
<content>Are you coming to save me?"</content>
<content>Then she opened her eyes, and peeped again.</content>
<content>The Prioress, wholly unmoved by the impending advent of "Wilfred," stood at the casement, calmly watching the swallows.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Seraphine began to weep.</content>
<content>At last the passionate sobbing ceased.</content>
<content>Unbroken silence reigned in the cell.</content>
<content>From without, the latch of the door was lifted; but the lock held.</content>
<content>Presently Sister Mary Seraphine dragged herself to the feet of the
Prioress, seized the hem of her robe, and kissed it.</content>
<content>Then the Prioress turned.</content>
<content>She firmly withdrew her robe from those clinging hands; yet looked, with eyes of tender compassion, upon the kneeling figure at her feet.</content>
<content>"Sister Seraphine," she said, " for you must shew true penitence e'er I can permit you to be called by our Lady's name you will now come to my cell, where I will presently speak with you."</content>
<content>Sister Seraphine instantly fell prone.</content>
<content>"I cannot walk," she said.</content>
<content>"You will not walk," replied the Prioress, sternly.</content>
<content>"You will travel upon your hands and knees."</content>
<content>She crossed to the door, unlocked and set it wide.</content>
<content>"Moreover," she added, from the doorway, "if you do not appear in my presence in reasonable time, I shall be constrained to send for Mother Sub-Prioress."</content>
<content>The cell of the Prioress was situated at the opposite end of the long, stone passage; but in less than reasonable time, Sister Seraphine crawled in.</content>
<content>The unwonted exercise had had a most salutary effect upon her frame of mind.</content>
<content>Her straight habit, of heavy cloth, had rendered progress upon her knees awkward and difficult.</content>
<content>Her hands had become entangled in her torn veil.</content>
<content>Each moment she had feared lest cell doors, on either side, should open; old Antony might appear from the cloisters, or greatest disaster of all Mother Sub-Prioress might advance toward her from the Refectory stairs!</content>
<content>In order to attain a greater rate of speed, she had tried lifting her knees, as elephants lift their feet.</content>
<content>This mode of progress, though ungainly, had proved efficacious; but would have been distinctly mirth-provoking to beholders.</content>
<content>The stones had hurt her hands and knees far more than she hurt them when she beat upon the floor of her own cell.</content>
<content>She arrived at the Reverend Mother's footstool, heated in mind and body, ashamed of herself, vexed with her garments, in fact in an altogether saner frame of mind than when she had called upon "Wilfred," and made reiterated mention of trappings of crimson and silver bells.</content>
<content>Perhaps the Prioress had foreseen this result, when she imposed the penance.</content>
<content>Leniency or sympathy, at that moment, would have been fatal and foolish; and had not the Prioress made special petition for wisdom?</content>
<content>She was seated at her table, when Sister Seraphine bumped and shuffled into view.</content>
<content>She did not raise her eyes from the illuminated missal she was studying.</content>
<content>One hand lay on the massive clasp, the other rested in readiness to turn the page.</content>
<content>Her noble form seemed stately calm personified.</content>
<content>When she heard Sister Seraphine panting close to her foot, she spoke; still without lifting her eyes.</content>
<content>"You may rise to your feet," she said, "and shut to the door."</content>
<content>Then the waiting hand turned the page, and silence fell.</content>
<content>"You may arrange the disorder of your dress," said the Prioress, and turned another page.</content>
<content>When at length she looked up, Sister Seraphine, clothed and apparently in her right mind, stood humbly near the door.</content>
<content>The Prioress closed the book, and shut the heavy clasps.</content>
<content>Then she pointed to an oaken stool, signing to the nun to draw it forward.</content>
<content>"Be seated, my child," she said, in tones of infinite tenderness.</content>
<content>"There is much which must now be said, and your mind will pay better heed, if your body be at rest."</content>
<content>With her steadfast eyes the Prioress searched the pretty, flushed face, swollen with weeping, and now gathering a look of petulant defiance, thinly veiled beneath surface humility.</content>
<content>"What was the cause of this outburst, my child?"</content>
<content>asked the Prioress, very gently.</content>
<content>"While in the Cathedral, Reverend Mother, up in our gallery, I, being placed not far from a window, heard, in a moment of silence, the neighing of a horse in the street without.</content>
<content>It was like to the neighing of mine own lovely palfrey, waiting in the castle court at home, until I should come down and mount him.</content>
<content>Each time that steed neighed, I could see Snowflake more clearly, in trappings of gay crimson, with silver bells, amid many others prancing impatiently, champing their bits as they waited; for it pleased me to come out last, when all were mounted.</content>
<content>Then the riders lifted their plumed caps when I appeared, while Wilfred, pushing my page aside, did swing me into the saddle.</content>
<content>Thus, with shouting and laughter and winding of horn, we would all ride out to the hunt or the tourney; I first, on Snowflake; Wilfred, close behind."</content>
<content>Very quietly the Prioress sat listening.</content>
<content>She did not take her eyes from the flushed face.</content>
<content>A slight colour tinged her own cheeks.</content>
<content>"Who was Wilfred?"</content>
<content>she asked, when Sister Seraphine paused for breath.</content>
<content>"My cousin, whom I should have wed if "

"If?"</content>
<content>"If I had not left the world."</content>
<content>The Prioress considered this.</content>
<content>"If your heart was set upon wedding your cousin, my child, why did you profess a vocation and, renouncing all worldly and carnal desires, gain admission to our sacred Order?"</content>
<content>"My heart was not set on marrying my cousin!"</content>
<content>cried Sister Seraphine, with petulance.</content>
<content>"I was weary of Wilfred.</content>
<content>I was weary of everything!</content>
<content>I wanted to profess.</content>
<content>I wished to become a nun.</content>
<content>There were people I could punish, and people I could surprise, better so, than in any other way.</content>
<content>But Wilfred said that, when the time came, he would be there to carry me off."</content>
<content>"And when the time came?"</content>
<content>"He was not there.</content>
<content>I never saw him again."</content>
<content>The Prioress turned, and looked out through the oriel window.</content>
<content>She seemed to be weighing, carefully, what she should say.</content>
<content>When at length she spoke, she kept her eyes fixed upon the waving tree-tops beyond the Convent wall.</content>
<content>"Sister Seraphine," she said, "many who embrace the religious life, know what it is to pass through the experience you have now had; but, as a rule, they fight the temptation and conquer it in the secret of their own hearts, in the silence of their own cells.</content>
<content>"Memories of the life that was, before, choosing the better part, we left the world, come back to haunt us, with a wanton sweetness.</content>
<content>Such memories cannot change the state, fixed forever by our vows; but they may awaken in us vain regrets or worldly longings.</content>
<content>Therein lies their sinfulness.</content>
<content>"To help you against this danger, I will now give you two prayers, which you must commit to memory, and repeat whenever need arises.</content>
<content>The first is from the Breviary."</content>
<content>The Prioress drew toward her a black book with silver clasps, opened it, and read therefrom a short prayer in Latin.</content>
<content>But seeing no light of response or of intelligence upon the face of Sister Seraphine, she slowly repeated a translation.</content>
<content>Almighty and Everlasting God, grant that our wills be ever meekly subject to Thy will, and our hearts be ever honestly ready to serve Thee.</content>
<content>Amen.</content>
<content>Her eyes rested, with a wistful smile, upon the book.</content>
<content>"This prayer might suffice," she said, "if our hearts were truly honest, if our wills were ever yielded.</content>
<content>But, alas, our hearts are deceitful above all things, and our wills are apt to turn traitor to our good intentions.</content>
<content>"Therefore I have found for you, in the Gregorian Sacramentary, another prayer less well-known, yet much more ancient, written over six hundred years ago.</content>
<content>It deals effectually with the deceitful heart, the insidious, tempting thoughts, and the unstable will.</content>
<content>Here is a translation which I have myself inscribed upon the margin."</content>
<content>The Prioress laid her folded hands upon the missal and as she repeated the ancient sixth-century prayer, in all its depth of inspired simplicity, her voice thrilled with deep emotion, for she was giving to another that which had meant infinitely much to her own inner life.</content>
<content>Almighty God, unto Whom all hearts be open, all desires known, and from Whom no secrets are hid: Cleanse the thoughts of our hearts by the inspiration of Thy Holy Spirit, that we may perfectly love Thee, and worthily magnify Thy Holy Name, through Christ our Lord.</content>
<content>Amen.</content>
<content>The Prioress turned her face from Sister Seraphine's unresponsive countenance and fixed her eyes once more upon the tree-tops.</content>
<content>She was thinking of the long years of secret conflict, known only to Him from Whom no secrets are hid; of the constant cleansing of her thoughts, for which she had so earnestly pleaded; of the fear lest she should never worthily magnify that Holy Name.</content>
<content>Presently her heart filled with humble tenderness she turned to
Sister Seraphine.</content>
<content>"These prayers, my child, which you will commit to memory before you sleep this night, will protect you from a too insistent recollection of the world you have resigned; and will assist you, with real inward thoroughness, to die daily to self, in order that the Holy Name of our dear Lord may be more worthily magnified in you."</content>
<content>But, alas!</content>
<content>this gentle treatment, these long silences, this quiet recitation of holy prayers, had but stirred the naughty spirit in Sister Seraphine.</content>
<content>Her shallow nature failed to understand the deeps of the noble heart, dealing thus tenderly with her.</content>
<content>She measured its ocean-wide greatness, by the little artificial runnels of her own morbid emotions.</content>
<content>She mistook gentleness for weakness; calm self-control, for lack of strength of will.</content>
<content>Her wholesome awe of the Prioress was forgotten.</content>
<content>"But I do not want to die!"</content>
<content>she exclaimed.</content>
<content>"I want to live to live to live!"</content>
<content>The Prioress looked up, astonished.</content>
<content>The surface humility had departed from the swollen countenance of
Sister Seraphine.</content>
<content>The petulant defiance was plainly visible.</content>
<content>"Kneel!"</content>
<content>commanded the Prioress, with authority.</content>
<content>The wayward nun jerked down upon her knees, upsetting the stool behind her.</content>
<content>The Prioress made a quick movement, then restrained herself.</content>
<content>She had prayed for patience in dealing with wilfulness.</content>
<content>"We die that we may live," she said, solemnly.</content>
<content>"Sister Seraphine, this is the lesson your wayward heart must learn.</content>
<content>Dying to self, we live unto God.</content>
<content>Dying to sin, we live unto righteousness.</content>
<content>Dying to the world, we find the Life Eternal."</content>
<content>On her knees upon the floor, Sister Seraphine felt her position to be such as lent itself to pathos.</content>
<content>"But I want to live to the world!"</content>
<content>she cried, and burst into tears.</content>
<content>Now Convent life does not tend to further individual grief.</content>
<content>Constant devout contemplation of the Supreme Sorrow which wrought the world's salvation lessens the inclination to shed tears of self-pity.</content>
<content>The Prioress was startled and alarmed by the pathetic sobs of Sister
Seraphine.</content>
<content>This young nun had but lately been sent on to the Nunnery at Whytstone from a convent at Tewkesbury in which she had served her novitiate, and taken her final vows.</content>
<content>The Prioress now realised how little she knew of the inner working of the mind of Sister Seraphine, and blamed herself for having looked upon the outward appearance rather than upon the heart, taken too much for granted, and relied too entirely upon the reports of others.</content>
<content>Her sense of failure, toward the Community in general, and toward Seraphine in particular, lent her a fresh stock of patience.</content>
<content>She raised the weeping nun from the floor, put her arm around her, with protective gesture, and led her before the Shrine of the Madonna.</content>
<content>"My child," she said, "there are things we are called upon to suffer which we can best tell to our blessed Lady, herself.</content>
<content>Try to unburden your heart and find comfort .</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Does your mind hark back to the thought of the earthly love you resigned in order to give yourself solely to the heavenly?</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Are you troubled by fears lest you wronged the man you loved, when, leaving him, you became the bride of Heaven?"</content>
<content>Sister Seraphine smiled a scornful little smile.</content>
<content>"Nay," she said, "I was weary of Wilfred.</content>
<content>But there were others."</content>
<content>The voice of the Prioress grew even graver, and more sad.</content>
<content>"Is it then the Fact of marriage which you desired and regret?"</content>
<content>Sister Seraphine laughed a hard, self-conscious, little laugh.</content>
<content>"Nay, I could not have brooked to be bound to any man.</content>
<content>But I liked to be loved, and I liked to be First in the thought and heart of another."</content>
<content>The Prioress looked at the pretty, tear-stained face, at the softly moulded form.</content>
<content>Then an idea came to her.</content>
<content>To voice it, lifted the veil from the very Holy of Holies of her own heart's sufferings; but she would not shrink from aught which could help this soul she was striving to uplift.</content>
<content>With her eyes resting upon the Babe in the arms of the Virgin Mother, she asked, gravely and low:

"Is it the ceaseless longing to have had a little child of your own to hold in your arms, to gather to your breast, to put to sleep upon your knees, which keeps your heart turning restlessly back to the world?"</content>
<content>Sister Seraphine gazed at the Prioress, in utter amazement.</content>
<content>"Nay, then, indeed!"</content>
<content>she replied, impatiently.</content>
<content>"Always have I hated children.</content>
<content>To escape from the vexations of motherhood were reason enough for leaving the world."</content>
<content>Then the Prioress withdrew her protective arm, and looked sternly upon
Sister Seraphine.</content>
<content>"You are playing false to your vows," she said; "you are slighting your vocation; yet no worthy or noble feeling draws your heart back to the world.</content>
<content>You do but desire vain pomp and show; all those things which minister to the enthronement of self.</content>
<content>Return to your cell and spend three hours in prayer and penitence before the crucifix."</content>
<content>The Prioress lifted her hand and pointed to the figure of the Christ, hanging upon the great rugged cross against the wall, facing the door.</content>
<content>The sublimity of a supreme adoration was in her voice, as she made her last appeal.</content>
<content>"Surely," she said, 'surely no love of self can live, in view of the death and sacrifice of our blessed Lord!</content>
<content>Kneel then before the crucifix and learn "

But the over-wrought mind of Sister Seraphine, suddenly convinced of the futility of its hopeless rebellion, passed, in that moment, altogether beyond control.</content>
<content>With a shout of wild laughter, she flung back her head, pointing with outstretched finger at the crucifix.</content>
<content>"Death!</content>
<content>Death!</content>
<content>Death!"</content>
<content>she shrieked, "helpless, hopeless, terrible!</content>
<content>I ask for life, I want to live; I am young, I am gay, I am beautiful.</content>
<content>And they bid bid bid me kneel long hours watching death."</content>
<content>Her
voice rose to a piercing scream.</content>
<content>"Ah, HA!</content>
<content>That will I NOT!</content>
<content>A dead
God cannot help me!</content>
<content>I want life, not death!"</content>
<content>Shrieking she leapt to her feet, flew across the room, beat upon the sacred Form with her fists; tore at It with her fingers.</content>
<content>One instant of petrifying horror.</content>
<content>Then the Prioress was upon her.</content>
<content>Seizing her by both wrists she flung her to the floor, then pulled a rope passing over a pulley in the wall, which started the great alarm-bell, in the passage, clanging wildly.</content>
<content>At once there came a rush of flying feet; calls for the Sub-Prioress; but she was already there.</content>
<content>When they flung wide the door, lo, the Prioress stood with white face and blazing eyes, her arms outstretched between them and the crucifix.</content>
<content>Upon the floor, a crumpled heap, lay Sister Mary Seraphine.</content>
<content>The nuns, in a frightened crowd, filled the doorway, none daring to speak, or to enter; till old Mary Antony, pushing past the Sub-Prioress, kneeled down beside the Reverend Mother, and, lifting the hem of her robe, kissed it and pressed it to her breast.</content>
<content>Slowly the Prioress let fall her arms.</content>
<content>"Enter," she said; and they flocked in.</content>
<content>"Sister Seraphine," said the Prioress, in awful tones, "has profaned the crucifix, reviling our blessed Lord, Who hangs thereon."</content>
<content>All the nuns, falling upon their knees, hid their faces in their hands.</content>
<content>There was a terrifying quality in the silence of the next moments.</content>
<content>Slowly the Prioress turned, prostrated herself at the foot of the cross, and laid her forehead against the floor at its base.</content>
<content>Then the nuns heard one deep, shuddering sob.</content>
<content>Not a head was lifted.</content>
<content>The only nun who peeped was Sister Mary
Seraphine, prone upon the floor.</content>
<content>After a while, the Prioress arose, pale but calm.</content>
<content>"Carry her to her cell," she said.</content>
<content>Two tall nuns to whom she made sign lifted Sister Seraphine, and bore her out.</content>
<content>When the shuffling of their feet died away in the distance, the
Prioress gave further commands.</content>
<content>"All will now go to their cells and kneel in adoration before the crucifix.</content>
<content>Doors are to be left standing wide.</content>
<content>The Miserere is to be chanted, until the ringing of the Refectory bell.</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress will remain behind."</content>
<content>The nuns dispersed, as quickly as they had gathered; seeking their cells, like frightened birds fleeing before a gathering storm.</content>
<content>The tall nuns who had carried Sister Seraphine returned and waited outside the Reverend Mother's door.</content>
<content>The Prioress stood alone; a tragic figure in her grief.</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress drew near.</content>
<content>Her narrow face, peering from out her veil, more than ever resembled a ferret.</content>
<content>Her small eyes gleamed with a merciless light.</content>
<content>"Is mine the task, Reverend Mother?"</content>
<content>she whispered.</content>
<content>The Prioress inclined her head.</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress murmured a second question.</content>
<content>The Prioress turned and looked at the crucifix.</content>
<content>"Yes," she said, firmly.</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress sidled nearer; then whispered her third question.</content>
<content>The Prioress did not answer.</content>
<content>She was looking at the carved, oaken stool, overthrown.</content>
<content>She was wondering whether she could have acted with better judgment, spoken more wisely.</content>
<content>Her heart was sore.</content>
<content>Such noble natures ever blame themselves for the wrong-doing of the worthless.</content>
<content>Receiving no reply, Mother Sub-Prioress whispered a suggestion.</content>
<content>"No," said the Prioress.</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress modified her suggestion.</content>
<content>The Prioress turned and looked at the tender figure of the Madonna, brooding over the blessed Babe.</content>
<content>"No," said the Prioress.</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress frowned, and made a further modification; but in tones which suggested finality.</content>
<content>The Prioress inclined her head.</content>
<content>The Sub-Prioress, bowing low, lifted the hem of the Reverend Mother's veil, and kissed it; then passed from the room.</content>
<content>The Prioress moved to the window.</content>
<content>The sunset was over.</content>
<content>The evening star shone, like a newly-lighted lamp, in a pale purple sky.</content>
<content>The fleet-winged swallows had gone to rest.</content>
<content>Bats flitted past the casement, like homeless souls who know not where to go.</content>
<content>Low chanting began in the cells; the nuns, with open doors, singing Miserere.</content>
<content>But, as she looked at the evening star, the Prioress heard again, with startling distinctness, the final profanity of poor Sister Seraphine: "I want life not death!"</content>
<content>Along the corridor passed a short procession, on its way to the cell of
Mary Seraphine.</content>
<content>First went a nun, carrying a lighted taper.</content>
<content>Next, the two tall nuns who had borne Mary Seraphine to her cell.</content>
<content>Behind them, Mother Sub-Prioress, holding something beneath her scapulary which gave to her more of a presence than she usually possessed.</content>
<content>Solemn and official, nay, almost sacrificial was their measured shuffle, as they moved along the passage, and entered the cell of Mary Seraphine.</content>
<content>The Prioress closed her door, and, kneeling before the crucifix, implored forgiveness for the sacrilege which, all unwittingly, she had provoked.</content>
<content>The nuns, in their separate cells, chanted the Miserere.</content>
<content>But suddenly with one accord, their voices fell silent; then hastened on, in uncertain, agitated rhythm.</content>
<content>Old Mary Antony below, playing her favourite game, also paused, and pricked up her ears: then filliped the wizen pea, which stood for Mother Sub-Prioress, into the darkest corner, and hurried off to brew a soothing balsam.</content>
<content>So, when the Refectory bell had summoned all to the evening meal, the old lay-sister crept to the cell of Mary Seraphine, carrying broth and comfort.</content>
<content>But Sister Seraphine was better content than she had been for many weeks.</content>
<content>At last she had become the centre of attention; and, although, during the visit of Mother Sub-Prioress to her cell, this had been a peculiarly painful position to occupy, yet to the morbid mind of Mary Seraphine, the position seemed worth the discomfort.</content>
<content>Therefore, her mind now purged of its discontent, she cheerfully supped old Antony's broth, and applied the soothing balsam; yet planning the while, to gain favour with the Prioress, by repeating to her, at the first convenient opportunity, the naughty remarks concerning Mother Sub-Prioress, now being made for her diversion, by the kind old woman who had risked reproof, in order to bring to her, in her disgrace, both food and consolation.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VI

THE KNIGHT OF THE BLOODY VEST

"Nay, I have naught for thee this morning," said Mary Antony to the robin; "naught, that is, save spritely conversation.</content>
<content>I can tell thee a tale or two; I can give thee sage advice; but, in my wallet, little Master Mendicant, I have but my bag of peas."</content>
<content>The old lay-sister sat resting in the garden.</content>
<content>She had had a busy hour, yet complicated in its busy-ness, for, starting out to do weeding, she had presently fancied herself intent upon making a posy, and now, sat upon the stone seat beneath the beech tree, holding a large nosegay made up of many kinds of flowering weeds, arranged with much care, and bound round with convolvulus tendrils.</content>
<content>Keen and uncommon shrewd though old Antony certainly was in many ways, her great age occasionally betrayed itself by childish vagaries.</content>
<content>Her mind would start off along the lines of a false premise, landing her eventually in a dream-like conclusion.</content>
<content>As now, when waking from a moment's nodding in the welcome shade, she wondered why her old back seemed well-nigh broken, and marvelled to find herself holding a big posy of dandelions, groundsel, plantain, and bindweed.</content>
<content>On the other end of the seat, stood the robin.</content>
<content>The beech was just near enough to the cloisters, the pieman's tree, and his own particular yew hedge, to come within his little kingdom.</content>
<content>Having mentioned her bag of peas, Mary Antony experienced an irresistible desire to view them and, moreover, to display them before the bright eyes of the robin.</content>
<content>She laid the queer nosegay down upon the grass at her feet, turned sidewise on the stone slab, and drew the bag from her wallet.</content>
<content>"Now, Master Pieman!"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"At thine own risk thou doest it; but with thine own bright eyes thou shalt see the holy Ladies; the Unnamed, all like peas in a pod, as the Lord knows they do look, when they walk to and fro; but first, if so be that I can find them, the Few which I distinguish from among the rest."</content>
<content>Presently, after much peering into the bag, the fine white pea, the wizened pea, and the pale and speckled pea, lay in line upon the stone.</content>
<content>"This," explained Mary Antony, pointing, with knobby forefinger, to the first, "is the Reverend Mother, Herself large, and pure, and noble.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Nay, hop not too close, Sir Redbreast!</content>
<content>When we enter her chamber we kneel at the threshold, till she bids us draw nearer.</content>
<content>True, we are merely soberly-clad, holy women, whereas thou art a gay, gaudy man; bold-eyed, and, doubtless, steeped in sin.</content>
<content>But even thou must keep thy distance, in presence of this most Reverend Pea of great price.</content>
<content>"This," indicating the shrivelled pea, "is Mother Sub-Prioress, who would love to have the whipping of thee, thou naughty little rascal!</content>
<content>"This is Sister Mary Rebecca who daily grows more crooked, both in mind and body; yet who ever sweetly smileth.</content>
<content>"Now will I show thee, if so be that I can find her, Sister Teresa, a kindly soul and gracious, but with a sniff which may be heard in the kitchens when that holy Lady taketh her turn at the Refectory reading.</content>
<content>And when, the reading over, having sniffed every other minute, she at length, feels free to blow, beshrew me, Master Redbreast, one might think our old dun cow had just been parted from a newly-born calf.</content>
<content>Yea, a kind, gracious soul; but noisy about the nose, and forgetful of the ears of other people, her own necessity seeming excuse enough for veritable trumpet blasts."</content>
<content>Mary Antony, half turning as she talked, peered into the open bag in search of Sister Teresa.</content>
<content>Then, quick as thought, the unexpected happened.</content>
<content>Three rapid hops, a jerky bend of the red breast, a flash of wings 

The robin had flown off with the white pea!</content>
<content>The shrivelled and the speckled alone remained upon the seat.</content>
<content>Uttering a cry of horror and dismay, the old lay-sister fell upon her knees, lifting despairing hands to trees and sky.</content>
<content>Down by the lower wall, in earnest meditation, the Prioress moved back and forth, on the Cypress Walk.</content>
<content>Mary Antony's shriek of dismay, faint but unmistakable, reached her ears.</content>
<content>Turning, she passed noiselessly up the green sward, on the further side of the yew hedge; but paused, in surprise, as she drew level with the beech; for the old lay-sister's voice penetrated the hedge, and the first words she overheard seemed to the Prioress wholly incomprehensible.</content>
<content>"Ah, thou Knight of the Bloody Vest!"</content>
<content>moaned Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"Heaven send thy wicked perfidy may fall on thine own pate!</content>
<content>Intruding thyself into our most private places; begging food, which could not be refused; wheedling old Mary Antony into letting thee have a peep at the holy Ladies thou bold, bad man!</content>
<content>and then carrying off the Reverend Mother, Herself!</content>
<content>Ha!</content>
<content>Hadst thou but caught away Mother Sub-Prioress, she would have reformed thy home, whipped thy children, and mended thine own vile manners, thou graceless churl!</content>
<content>Or hadst thou taken Sister Mary Rebecca, she would have brought the place about thine ears, telling thy wife fine tales of thine unfaithfulness; whispering that Mary Antony is younger and fairer than she.</content>
<content>But, nay, forsooth!</content>
<content>Neither of these will do!</content>
<content>Thou must needs snatch away the Reverend Mother, Herself!</content>
<content>Oh, sacrilegious fiend!</content>
<content>Stand not there mocking me!</content>
<content>Where is the Reverend Mother?"</content>
<content>"Why, here am I, dear Antony," said the Prioress, in soothing tones, coming quickly from behind the hedge.</content>
<content>One glance revealed, to her relief, that the lay-sister was alone.</content>
<content>Tears ran down the furrows of her worn old face.</content>
<content>She knelt upon the grass; beside her a large nosegay of flowering weeds; upon the seat, peas strewn from out a much-used, linen bag.</content>
<content>Above her on a bough, a robin perched, bending to look, with roguish eye, at the scattered peas.</content>
<content>To the Prioress it seemed that indeed the old lay-sister must have taken leave of her senses.</content>
<content>Stooping, she tried to raise her; but Mary Antony, flinging herself forward, clasped and kissed the Reverend Mother's feet, in an abandonment of penitence and grief.</content>
<content>"Nay, rise, dear Antony," said the Prioress, firmly.</content>
<content>"Rise!</content>
<content>I command it.</content>
<content>The day is warm.</content>
<content>Thou hast been dreaming.</content>
<content>No bold, bad man has forced his way within these walls.</content>
<content>No 'Knight of the Bloody Vest' is here.</content>
<content>Rise up and look.</content>
<content>We are alone."</content>
<content>But Mary Antony, still on her knees, half raised herself, and, pointing to the bough above, quavered, amid her sobs: "The bold, bad man is there!"</content>
<content>Looking up, the Prioress met the bright eye of the robin, peeping down.</content>
<content>Why, surely?</content>
<content>Yes!</content>
<content>There was the "Bloody Vest."</content>
<content>The Prioress smiled.</content>
<content>She began to understand.</content>
<content>The robin burst into a stream of triumphant song.</content>
<content>At which, old Mary
Antony, still kneeling, shook her uplifted fist.</content>
<content>The Prioress raised and drew her to the seat.</content>
<content>"Now sit thee here beside me," she said, "and make full confession.</content>
<content>Ease thine old heart by telling me the entire tale.</content>
<content>Then I will pass sentence on the robin if, true to his name, he turns out to be a thief."</content>
<content>So there, in the Convent garden, while the robin sang overhead, the Prioress listened to the quaint recital; the dread of making mistake in the daily counting; the elaborate plan of dropping peas; the manner in which the peas became identified with the personalities of the White Ladies; the games in the cell; the taming of the robin; the habit of sharing with the little bird, interests which might not be shared with others, which had resulted that morning in the display of the peas, and this undreamed of disaster the abduction of the Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>The Prioress listened with outward gravity, striving to conceal all signs of the inward mirth which seized and shook her.</content>
<content>But more than once she had to turn her face from the peering eyes of Mary Antony, striving anxiously to gather whether her chronicle of sins was placing her outside the pale of possible forgiveness.</content>
<content>The Prioress did not hasten the recital.</content>
<content>She knew the importance, to the mind with which she dealt, of even the most trivial detail.</content>
<content>To be checked or hurried, would leave Mary Antony with the sense of an incomplete confession.</content>
<content>Therefore, with infinite patience the Prioress listened, seated in the sunlit garden, undisturbed, save for the silent passing, once or twice, of a veiled figure through the cloisters, who, seeing the Reverend Mother seated beneath the beech, did reverence and hastened on, looking not again.</content>
<content>When the garrulous old voice at last fell silent, the Prioress, with kind hand, covered the restless fingers clasping and unclasping in anxious contortions and firmly held them in folded stillness.</content>
<content>Her first words were of a thing as yet unmentioned.</content>
<content>"Dear Antony," she said, "is that thy posy lying at our feet?"</content>
<content>"Ah, Reverend Mother," sighed the old lay-sister, "in this did I again do wrong meaning to do right.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Augustine, coming into the kitchens with leave, from Mother Sub-Prioress, to make the pasties, and desiring to be free to make them heavy unhampered by my advice which, of a surety, would have helped them to lightness bade me go out and weed the garden.</content>
<content>"Weeding, I bethought me how much liefer I would be gathering a posy of choicest flowers for our sweet Lady's shrine; and, thus thinking, I began to do, not according to Sister Mary Augustine's hard task, but according to mine own heart's promptings.</content>
<content>Yet, when the posy was finished, alack-a-day!</content>
<content>it was a posy of weeds!"</content>
<content>Tears filled the eyes of the Prioress; at first she could not trust her voice to make reply.</content>
<content>Then, stooping she picked up the nosegay.</content>
<content>"Our Lady shall have it," she said.</content>
<content>"I will place it before her shrine, in mine own cell.</content>
<content>She will understand knowing how often, though the hands perforce do weeding, yet, all the time, the heart is gathering choicest flowers.</content>
<content>"Aye, and sometimes when we bring to God offerings of fairest flowers, He sees but worthless weeds.</content>
<content>And, when we mourn, because we have but weeds to offer, He sees them fragrant blossoms.</content>
<content>Whatever, to the eye of man, the hand may hold, God sees therein the bouquet of the heart's intention."</content>
<content>The Prioress paused, a look of great gladness on her face; then, as she saw the old lay-sister still eyeing her posy with dissatisfaction: "And, after all, dear Antony," she said, "who shall decide which flowers shall be dubbed 'weeds'?</content>
<content>No plant of His creation, however humble, was called a 'weed' by the Creator.</content>
<content>When, for man's sin, He cursed the ground, He said: 'Thorns also and thistles shall it cause to bud.'</content>
<content>Well?</content>
<content>Sharpest thorns are found around the rose; the thistle is the royal bloom of Scotland; and, if our old white ass could speak her mind, doubtless she would call it King of Flowers.</content>
<content>"Nowhere in Holy Books, is any plant named a 'weed.'</content>
<content>It is left to man to proclaim that the flowers he wants not, are weeds.</content>
<content>"Look at each one of these.</content>
<content>Could you or I, labouring for years, with all our skill, make anything so perfect as the meanest of these weeds?</content>
<content>"Nay; they are weeds, because they grow, there where they should not be.</content>
<content>The gorgeous scarlet poppy is a weed amid the corn.</content>
<content>If roses overgrew the wheat, we should dub them weeds, and root them out.</content>
<content>"And some of us have had, perforce, so to deal with the roses in our lives; those sweet and fragrant things which overgrew our offering of the wheat of service, our sacrifice of praise and prayer.</content>
<content>"Perhaps, when our weeds are all torn out, and cast in a tangled heap before His Feet, our Lord beholds in them a garland of choice blossoms.</content>
<content>The crown of thorns on earth, may prove, in Paradise, a diadem of flowers."</content>
<content>The Prioress laid the posy on the seat beside her.</content>
<content>"Now, Antony, about thy games with peas.</content>
<content>There is no wrong in keeping count with peas of those who daily walk to and from Vespers; though, I admit, it seems to me, it were easier to count one, two, three, with folded hands, than to let fall the peas from one hand to the other, beneath thy scapulary.</content>
<content>Howbeit, a method which would be but a pitfall to one, may prove a prop to another.</content>
<content>So I give thee leave to continue to count with thy peas.</content>
<content>Also the games in thy cell are harmless, and lead me to think, as already I have sometimes thought, that games with balls or rings, something in which eye guides the hand, and mind the eye, might be helpful for all, on summer evenings.</content>
<content>"But I cannot have thee take upon thyself to decide the future state of the White Ladies.</content>
<content>Who art thou, to send me to Paradise with a fillip of thine old finger-nail, yet to keep our excellent Sub-Prioress in Purgatory?</content>
<content>Shame upon thee, Mary Antony!"</content>
<content>But the sternness of the Reverend Mother's tone was belied by the merriment in her grey eyes.</content>
<content>"So no more of that, my Antony; though, truth to tell, thy story gives me relief, answering a question I was meaning to put to thee.</content>
<content>I heard, not an hour ago, that Sister Antony had boasted that with a turn of her thumb and finger she could, any night, send Mother Sub-Prioress to Purgatory."</content>
<content>"Who said that of me?"</content>
<content>stuttered Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"Who said it, Reverend
Mother?"</content>
<content>"A little bird," murmured the Prioress.</content>
<content>"A little bird, dear Antony; but not thy pretty robin.</content>
<content>Also, the boast was taken to mean poison in the broth of Mother Sub-Prioress.</content>
<content>Hast thou ever put harmful things in the broth of Mother Sub-Prioress?"</content>
<content>Mary Antony slipped to her knees.</content>
<content>"Only beans, Reverend Mother, castor beans; and, when her temper was vilest, purging herbs.</content>
<content>Nothing more, I swear it!</content>
<content>Old Antony knows naught of poisons; only of mixing balsams ah, ha!</content>
<content>and soothing ointments!</content>
<content>Our blessed Lady knows the tale is false."</content>
<content>Hastily the Prioress lifted the nosegay and buried her face in bindweed and dandelions.</content>
<content>"I believe thee," she said, in a voice not over steady.</content>
<content>"Rise from thy knees.</content>
<content>But, remember, I forbid thee to put aught into Mother Sub-Prioress's broth, save things that soothe and comfort.</content>
<content>Give me thy word for this, Antony."</content>
<content>The old woman humbly lifted the hem of the Prioress's robe, and pressed it to her lips.</content>
<content>"I promise, Reverend Mother," she said, "and I do repent me of my sin."</content>
<content>"Sit beside me," commanded the Prioress.</content>
<content>"I have more to say to thee.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Think not hard thoughts of the Sub-Prioress.</content>
<content>She is stern, and extreme to mark what is done amiss, but this she conceives to be her duty.</content>
<content>She is a most pious Lady.</content>
<content>Her zeal is but a sign of her piety."</content>
<content>Mary Antony's keen eyes, meeting those of the Prioress, twinkled.</content>
<content>Once again the Prioress took refuge in the posy.</content>
<content>She was beginning to have had enough of the scent of dandelions.</content>
<content>"Mother Sub-Prioress is sick," she said.</content>
<content>"The cold struck her last evening, after sunset, in the orchard.</content>
<content>I have bidden her to keep her bed awhile.</content>
<content>We must tend her kindly, Antony, and help her back to health again.</content>
<content>"Sister Mary Rebecca is also sick, with pains in her bones and slight fever.</content>
<content>She too keeps her bed to-day.</content>
<content>Strive to feel kindly toward her, Antony.</content>
<content>I know she oft thinks evil where none was meant, telling tales of wrong which are mostly of her own imagining.</content>
<content>But, in so doing, she harms herself more than she can harm others.</content>
<content>"By stirring up the mud in a dark pool, you dim the reflection of the star which, before, shone bright within it.</content>
<content>But you do not dim the star, shining on high.</content>
<content>"So is it with the slanderous thoughts of evil minds.</content>
<content>They stir up their own murkiness; but they fail to dim the stars.</content>
<content>"We must bear with Sister Mary Rebecca."</content>
<content>"Go not nigh them, Reverend Mother," begged old Antony.</content>
<content>"I will tend them with due care and patience.</content>
<content>These pains in bones, and general shiverings, are given quickly from one to another.</content>
<content>I pray you, go not near.</content>
<content>Remember you were taken alas!</content>
<content>alas!</content>
<content>and they were left!"</content>
<content>At this the Prioress laughed, gaily.</content>
<content>"But I was not taken decently, with pains in my bones and a-bed, dear
Antony.</content>
<content>I was carried off by a bold, bad man thy Knight of the Bloody
Vest."</content>
<content>"Oh, pray!"</content>
<content>cried the old lay-sister.</content>
<content>"I fear me it is an omen.</content>
<content>The angel Gabriel, Reverend Mother, sent to bear you from earth to heaven.</content>
<content>'The one shall be taken, and the other left.'</content>
<content>Ah, if he had but flown off with Mother Sub-Prioress!"</content>
<content>The Prioress laughed again.</content>
<content>"Dear Antony, thy little bird took the first pea he saw.</content>
<content>Had there but been a crumb, or a morsel of cheese, he would have left thee thy white pea.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Hark how he sings his little song of praise!</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Is it not wonderful to call to mind how, centuries ago, when white-robed Druids cut mistletoe from British oaks, the robin redbreast hopped around, and sang; when, earlier still, men were wild and savage, dwelling in holes and caves and huts of mud, when churches and cloisters were unknown in this land and the one true God undreamed of, robins mated and made their nests, the speckled thrushes sang, 'Do it now Do it now,' as they sought food for their young, the blackbirds whistled, and the swallows flashed by on joyous wing.</content>
<content>Aye, and when Eve and Adam walked in Eden, amid strange beasts and gaily plumaged birds, here in these Isles the robin redbreast sang, and all our British birds busily built their nests and reared their young; living their little joyous lives, as He Who made them taught them how to do.</content>
<content>"And, in the centuries to come, when all things may be changed in this our land, when we shall long have gone to dust, when our loved cloisters may have crumbled into ruin; still the hills of Malvern will stand, and the silvery Severn flow along the valley; while here, in this very garden if it be a garden still the robin will build his nest, and carol his happy song.</content>
<content>"Mark you this, dear Mary Antony: all things made by man hold within them the elements of change and of decay.</content>
<content>But nature is at one with God, and therefore immutable.</content>
<content>Earthly kingdoms may rise and wane; mighty cities may spring up, then fall into ruin.</content>
<content>Nations may conquer and, in their turn, be conquered.</content>
<content>Man may slay man and, in his turn, be slain.</content>
<content>But, through it all, the mountains stand, the rivers flow, the forests wave, and the redbreast builds his nest in the hawthorn, and warbles a love-song to his mate."</content>
<content>The Prioress rose and stretched wide her arms to the sunlit garden, to the bough where the robin sang.</content>
<content>"Oh, to be one with God and with Nature!"</content>
<content>she cried.</content>
<content>"Oh, to know the essential mysteries of Life and Light and Love!</content>
<content>This is Life Eternal!"</content>
<content>She had forgotten the old lay-sister; aye, for the moment she had forgotten the Convent and the cloister, the mile-long walk in darkness, the chant of the unseen monks.</content>
<content>She trod again the springy heather of her youth; she heard the rush of the mountain stream; the sigh of the great forest; the rustle of the sunlit glades, alive with, life.</content>
<content>These all were in the robin's song.</content>
<content>Then 

Within the Convent, the Refectory bell clanged loudly.</content>
<content>The Prioress let fall her arms.</content>
<content>She picked up the nosegay of weeds.</content>
<content>"Come, Antony," she said, "let us go and discover whether Sister Mary Augustine hath contrived to make the pasties light and savoury, even without the aid of the advice she might have had from thee."</content>
<content>Old Mary Antony, gleeful and marvelling, followed the stately figure of the Prioress.</content>
<content>Never was shriven soul more blissfully at peace.</content>
<content>She had kept back nothing; yet the Reverend Mother had imposed no punishment, had merely asked a promise which, in the fulness of her gratitude, Mary Antony had found it easy to give.</content>
<content>Truly the broth of Mother Sub-Prioress should, for the future, contain naught but what was grateful and soothing.</content>
<content>But, as she entered the Refectory behind the Reverend Mother and saw all the waiting nuns arise, old Mary Antony laid her finger to her nose.</content>
<content>"That 'little bird' shall have the castor beans," she said, "That 'little bird' shall have them.</content>
<content>Not my pretty robin, but the other!"</content>
<content>And, sad to say, poor Sister Seraphine was sorely griped that night, and suffered many pangs.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VII

THE MADONNA IN THE CLOISTER

The Prioress knelt, in prayer and meditation, before the figure of the
Virgin Mother holding upon her knees the holy Babe.</content>
<content>Moonlight flooded the cell with a pure radiance.</content>
<content>Mary Antony's posy of weeds, offered, according to promise, at the Virgin's shrine, took on, in that silver splendour, the semblance of lilies and roses.</content>
<content>The Prioress knelt long, with clasped hands and bowed head, as white and as motionless as the marble before her.</content>
<content>But at length she lifted her face, and broke into low pleading.</content>
<content>"Mother of God," she said, "help this poor aching heart; still the wild hunger at my breast.</content>
<content>Make me content to be at one with the Divine, and to let Nature go.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Thou knowest it is not the man I want.</content>
<content>In all the long years since he played traitor to his troth to me, I have not wanted the man.</content>
<content>The woman he wed may have him, unbegrudged by me.</content>
<content>I do not envy her the encircling of his arms, though time was when I felt them strong and tender.</content>
<content>I do not want the man, but O, sweet Mother of God I want the man's little child!</content>
<content>I envy her the motherhood which, but for her, would have been mine.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>I want the soft dark head against my breast.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>I want sweet baby lips drawing fresh life from mine.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>I want the little feet, resting together in my hand.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>All Nature sings of life, and the power to bestow life.</content>
<content>Yet mine arms are empty, and my strength does but carry mine own self to and fro.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Oh, give me grace to turn my thoughts from Life to Sacrifice."</content>
<content>The Prioress rose, crossed the floor, and knelt long in prayer and contemplation before the crucifix.</content>
