<content>The moonlight fell upon the dying face of the suffering Saviour, upon the crown of thorns, the helpless arms out-stretched, the bleeding feet.</content>
<content>O, Infinite Redeemer!</content>
<content>O, mighty Sacrifice!</content>
<content>O, Love of God, made manifest!</content>
<content>The Prioress knelt long in adoring contemplation.</content>
<content>At intervals she prostrated herself, pressing her forehead against the base of the cross.</content>
<content>At length she rose and moved toward the inner room, where stood her couch.</content>
<content>But even as she reached the threshold she turned quickly back, and kneeling before the Virgin and Child clasped the little marble foot of the Babe, covered it with kisses, and pressed it to her breast.</content>
<content>Then, lifting despairing eyes to the tender face of the Madonna: "O,
Mother of God," she cried, "grant unto me to love the pierced feet of
thy dear Son crucified, more than I love the little, baby feet of the
Infant Jesus on thy knees."</content>
<content>A great calm fell upon her after this final prayer.</content>
<content>It seemed, of a sudden, more efficacious than all the long hours of vigil.</content>
<content>She felt persuaded that it would be granted.</content>
<content>She rose to her feet, almost too much dazed and too weary to cross to the inner cell.</content>
<content>A breath of exquisite fragrance filled the air.</content>
<content>At the feet of the Madonna stood a wondrous bouquet of lilies of the valley and white roses.</content>
<content>Pale but radiant, the Prioress passed into her sleeping-chamber.</content>
<content>The loving heart of old Mary Antony had been full of lilies and roses.</content>
<content>It was not her fault that her old hands had been filled with weeds.</content>
<content>Divine Love, understanding, had wrought this gracious miracle.</content>
<content>As the Prioress stretched herself upon her couch, she murmured softly: "The Lord seeth not as man seeth: for man looketh on the outward appearance, but the Lord looketh on the heart.</content>
<content>"And, after all, this miracle of the Divine perception doth take place daily.</content>
<content>"Alas, when our vaunted roses and lilies appear, in His sight, as mere worthless weeds.</content>
<content>"The Lord looketh on the heart."</content>
<content>When the Prioress awoke, the sunlight filled her chamber.</content>
<content>She hastened to the archway between the cells, and looked.</content>
<content>The dandelions seemed more gaily golden, in the morning light.</content>
<content>The bindweed had faded.</content>
<content>The Prioress was disappointed.</content>
<content>She had counted upon sending early for old Mary Antony.</content>
<content>She had pictured her bewildered joy.</content>
<content>Yet now the nosegay was as before.</content>
<content>Morning light is ever a test for transformations.</content>
<content>Things are apt to look again as they were.</content>
<content>But a fragrance of roses and lilies still lingered in the chamber.</content>
<content>The blessed Virgin smiled upon the Babe.</content>
<content>And there was peace in the heart of the Prioress.</content>
<content>Her long vigil, her hours of prayer, had won for her the sense of a calm certainty of coming victory.</content>
<content>Strong in that certainty, she bent, and gently kissed the little feet of the holy Babe.</content>
<content>Then, as was her wont, she sounded the bell which called the entire community to arise, and to begin a new day.</content>
<content>CHAPTER VIII

ON THE WINGS OF THE STORM

In the afternoon of that day, Mary Antony awaited, in the cloisters, the return of the White Ladies from Vespers.</content>
<content>Twenty only, had gone; and, fearful lest she should make mistake with the unusual number, the old lay-sister spent the time of waiting in counting the twenty peas afresh, passing them back and forth from one hand to the other.</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress was still unable to leave her bed.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Augustine stayed to tend her.</content>
<content>Sister Teresa was in less pain, but fevered still, and strangely weak.</content>
<content>The Reverend Mother forbade her to rise.</content>
<content>Shortly before the bell rang calling the nuns to form procession in the cloisters, Sister Seraphine declared herself unable for the walk, and begged to be allowed to remain behind.</content>
<content>The Prioress found herself misdoubting this sudden indisposition of Sister Seraphine who, though flushed and excited, shewed none of the usual signs of sickness.</content>
<content>Not wishing, however, to risk having a third patient upon her hands, the Reverend Mother gave leave for her to stay, but also elected to remain behind, herself; letting Sister Mary Rebecca, who had recovered from her indisposition, lead the procession.</content>
<content>Thus the Reverend Mother contrived to keep Sister Seraphine with her during the absence of the other nuns, giving her translations from the Sacramentaries to copy upon strips of vellum, until shortly before the hour when the White Ladies would return from Vespers, when she sent her to her cell for the time of prayer and meditation.</content>
<content>Left alone, the Prioress examined the copies, fairly legible, but sadly unlike her own beautiful work.</content>
<content>She sighed and, putting them away, rose and paced the room, questioning how best to deal with the pretty but wayward young nun.</content>
<content>Two definite causes led the Prioress to mistrust Sister Seraphine: one, that she had called upon "Wilfred" to come and save her, and had admitted having expected him to appear and carry her off before she made her final profession; the other, that she had tried to start an evil report concerning the old lay-sister, Mary Antony.</content>
<content>The Prioress pondered what means to take in order to bring Sister Seraphine to a better mind.</content>
<content>As the Prioress walked to and fro, unconsciously missing the daily exercise of the passage to the Cathedral, she noted a sudden darkening of her chamber.</content>
<content>Going to the window, she saw the sky grown black with thunder clouds.</content>
<content>So quickly the storm gathered, that the bright summer world without seemed suddenly hung over with a deep purple pall.</content>
<content>Birds screamed and darted by, on hurried wing; then, reaching home, fell silent.</content>
<content>All nature seemed to hold its breath, awaiting the first flash, and the first roll of thunder.</content>
<content>Still standing at her window, the Prioress questioned whether the nuns were returned, and safely in their cells.</content>
<content>While underground they would know nothing of it; but they loved not passing along the cloisters in a storm.</content>
<content>The Prioress wondered why she had not heard the bell announcing their return, and calling to the hour of prayer and silence.</content>
<content>Also why Mary Antony had not brought in the key and her report.</content>
<content>Thinking to inquire into this, she turned from the window, just as a darting snake of fire cleft the sky.</content>
<content>A crash of thunder followed; and, at that moment, the door of the chamber bursting open, old Mary Antony, breathless, stumbled in, forgetting to knock, omitting to kneel, not waiting leave to speak, both hands outstretched, one tightly clenched, the other holding the great key: "Oh, Reverend Mother!"</content>
<content>she gasped.</content>
<content>Then the stern displeasure on that loved face silenced her.</content>
<content>She dropped upon her knees, ashen and trembling.</content>
<content>Now the Prioress held personal fear in high scorn; and if, after ninety years' experience of lightning and thunder, Mary Antony was not better proof against their terrors, the Prioress felt scant patience with her.</content>
<content>She spoke sternly.</content>
<content>"How now, Mary Antony!</content>
<content>Why this unseemly haste?</content>
<content>Why this rush into my presence; no knock; no pause until I bid thee enter?</content>
<content>Is the storm-fiend at thy heels?</content>
<content>Now shame upon thee!"</content>
<content>For only answer, Mary Antony opened her clenched hand: whereupon twenty peas fell pattering to the floor, chasing one another across the Reverend Mother's cell.</content>
<content>The Prioress frowned, growing suddenly weary of these games with peas.</content>
<content>"Have the Ladies returned?"</content>
<content>she asked.</content>
<content>Mary Antony grovelled nearer, let fall the key, and seized the robe of the Prioress with both hands, not to carry it to her lips, but to cling to it as if for protection.</content>
<content>With the clang of the key on the flags, a twisted blade of fire rent the sky.</content>
<content>As the roar which followed rolled away, echoed and re-echoed by distant hills, the old lay-sister lifted her face.</content>
<content>Her lips moved, her gums rattled; the terror in her eyes pleaded for help.</content>
<content>This was the moment when it dawned on the Prioress that there was more here than fear of a storm.</content>
<content>Stooping she laid her hands firmly, yet with kindness in their strength, on the shaking shoulders.</content>
<content>"What is it, dear Antony?"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"Twenty White Ladies went," whispered the old lay-sister.</content>
<content>"I counted them.</content>
<content>Twenty White Ladies went; but "

"Well?"</content>
<content>"Twenty-one returned," chattered Mary Antony, and hid her face in the Reverend Mother's robe.</content>
<content>Two flashes, with their accompanying peals of thunder passed, before the Prioress moved or spoke.</content>
<content>Then raising Mary Antony she placed her in a chair, disengaged her robe from the shaking hands, passed out into the cell passage, and herself sounded the call to silence and prayer.</content>
<content>Returning to her cell she shut the door, poured out a cordial and put it to the trembling lips of Mary Antony.</content>
<content>Then taking a seat just opposite, she looked with calm eyes at the lay-sister.</content>
<content>"What means this story?"</content>
<content>said the Prioress.</content>
<content>"Reverend Mother, twenty holy Ladies went "

"I know.</content>
<content>And twenty returned."</content>
<content>"Aye," said the old woman more firmly, nettled out of her speechlessness; "twenty returned; and twenty peas I dropped from hand to hand.</content>
<content>Then when no pea remained yet another White Lady glided by; and with her went an icy wind, and around her came the blackness of the storm.</content>
<content>"Down the steps I fled, locked the door, and took the key.</content>
<content>How I mounted again, I know not.</content>
<content>As I drew level with the cloisters, I saw that twenty-first White Lady, for whom Saint Peter knows I held no pea, passing from the cloisters into the cell passage.</content>
<content>As I hastened on, fain to see whither she went, a blinding flash, like an evil twisting snake, shot betwixt her and me.</content>
<content>When I could see again, she was gone.</content>
<content>I fled to the Reverend Mother, and ran in on the roar of the thunder."</content>
<content>"Saw you her face, Mary Antony?"</content>
<content>"Nay, Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>But, of late, the holy Ladies mostly walk by with their faces shrouded."</content>
<content>"I know.</content>
<content>Now, see here, dear Antony.</content>
<content>Two peas dropped together, the while you counted one."</content>
<content>"Nay, Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>Twenty peas dropped one by one; also I counted twenty White Ladies.</content>
<content>And, after I had counted twenty, yet another passed."</content>
<content>"But how could that be?"</content>
<content>objected the Prioress.</content>
<content>"If twenty went, but twenty could return.</content>
<content>Who should be the twenty-first?"</content>
<content>Then old Mary Antony leaned forward, crossing herself.</content>
<content>"Sister Agatha," she whispered, tremulously.</content>
<content>"Poor Sister Agatha returned to us again."</content>
<content>But, even as she said it, swift came a name to the mind of the Prioress, answering her own question, and filling her with consternation and a great anger.</content>
<content>"Wilfred!</content>
<content>Wilfred, are you come to save me?"</content>
<content>foolish little Seraphine had said.</content>
<content>Was such sacrilege possible?</content>
<content>Could one from the outside world have dared to intrude into their holy Sanctuary?</content>
<content>Yet old Antony's tale carried conviction.</content>
<content>Her abject fear was now explained.</content>
<content>That the Dead should come again, and walk and move among the haunts of men, seeking out the surroundings they have loved and left, seems always to hold terror for the untutored mind, which knows not that the Dead are more alive than the living; and that there is no death, saving the death of sin.</content>
<content>But to the Reverend Mother, guarding her flock from sin or shame, a visitor from the Unseen World held less of horror than a possible intruder from the Seen.</content>
<content>A rapid glance as she sounded the bell, had shown her that the passage was empty.</content>
<content>Which cell now sheltered two, where there should be but one?</content>
<content>The Prioress walked across to a recess near the south window, touched a spring, and slid back a portion of the oak panelling.</content>
<content>Passing her hand into a secret hiding place in the wall, she drew forth a beautifully fashioned dagger, with carved ivory handle, crossed metal thumb-guard, blade of bevelled steel, polished and narrowing to a sharp needle point.</content>
<content>She tested the point, then slipped the weapon into her belt, beneath her scapulary.</content>
<content>As she closed the panel, and turned back into the chamber, a light of high resolve was in her eyes.</content>
<content>Her whole bearing betokened so fine a fearlessness, such noble fixity of purpose that, looking on her, Mary Antony felt her own fears vanishing.</content>
<content>"Now listen, dear Antony," said the Prioress, holding the old woman with her look.</content>
<content>"I must make sure that this twenty-first White Lady of thine is but a trick played on thee by thy peas.</content>
<content>Should she be anywhere in the Convent I shall most certainly have speech with her.</content>
<content>"Meanwhile, go thou to thy kitchens, and give thy mind to the preparing of the evening meal.</content>
<content>But ring not the Refectory bell until I bid thee.</content>
<content>Nay, I myself will sound it this evening.</content>
<content>It may suit me to keep the nuns somewhat longer at their devotions.</content>
<content>"Should I sound the alarm bell, let all thy helpers run up here; but go thou to the cell of Mother Sub-Prioress and persuade her not to rise.</content>
<content>If needful say that it is my command that she keep her bed.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Great heavens!</content>
<content>What a crash!</content>
<content>May our Lady defend us!</content>
<content>The lightning inclines to strike.</content>
<content>I shall pass to each cell and make sure that none are too greatly alarmed."</content>
<content>"Now, haste thee, Antony; and not a word concerning thy fears must pass thy lips to any; no mention of a twenty-first White Lady nor" the Prioress crossed herself "of Sister Agatha, to whom may our Lord grant everlasting rest."</content>
<content>Mary Antony, kneeling, kissed the hem of the Prioress's robe.</content>
<content>Then, rising, she said with unwonted solemnity and restraint: "The Lord defend you, Reverend Mother, from foes, seen and unseen," and, followed by another blinding flash of lightning, she left the cell.</content>
<content>CHAPTER IX

THE PRIORESS SHUTS THE DOOR

The Prioress waited until the old lay-sister's shuffling footsteps died away.</content>
<content>Then she passed out into the long, stone passage, leaving her own door open wide.</content>
<content>Into each cell the Prioress went.</content>
<content>In each she found a kneeling nun, absorbed in her devotions.</content>
<content>In no cell were there two white figures.</content>
<content>So simple were the fittings of these cells, that no place of concealment was possible.</content>
<content>One look, from the doorway, sufficed.</content>
<content>Outside the cell of Sister Seraphine the Prioress paused, hearing words within; then entered swiftly.</content>
<content>But Sister Seraphine was alone, reciting aloud, for love of hearing her own voice.</content>
<content>The Prioress now moved toward the heavy door in the archway leading into the cloisters.</content>
<content>It opened inwards, and had been left standing wide, by Mary Antony.</content>
<content>Indeed, in summer it stood open day and night, for coolness.</content>
<content>As the Prioress walked along the dimly lighted passage, she could see, through the open door, sheets of rain driving through the cloisters.</content>
<content>The storm-clouds had burst, at last, and were descending in floods.</content>
<content>The Prioress stood in the shelter of the doorway, looking out into the cloisters.</content>
<content>The only places she could not view, were the entrance to the subterranean way, and the flight of steps leading thereto.</content>
<content>She would have wished to examine these; but it seemed scarcely worth passing into the driving rain, now sweeping through the cloister arches.</content>
<content>After all, whatever possible danger lurked down those steps, the safety of the Convent would be assured if she closed this door, between the passage and the cloisters, and locked it.</content>
<content>Stepping back into the passage, she seized the heavy door and swung it to, noting as she did so, how far too heavy it was for the feeble arms of old Mary Antony, and deciding for the future to allot the task of closing it to a young lay-sister, leaving to Mary Antony merely the responsibility of turning the key in the lock.</content>
<content>This the Prioress was herself proceeding to do, when something impelled her to turn her eyes to the angle of wall laid bare by the closing of the door.</content>
<content>In that dark corner, motionless, with shrouded face, stood a tall figure, garbed in the dress of the nuns of the Order of the White Ladies of Worcester.</content>
<content>Perhaps the habit of silence is never of greater value than in moments of sudden shock and horror.</content>
<content>One cry from the Prioress would have meant the instant opening of many doors, and the arrival, on flying feet, of a score of frightened nuns.</content>
<content>Instead of screaming, the Prioress stood silent and perfectly still; while every pulse in her body ceased beating, during one moment of uncontrollable, cold horror.</content>
<content>Then, with a leap, her heart went on; pounding so loudly, that she could hear it in the silence.</content>
<content>Yet she kept command of every impulse which drove to sound or motion.</content>
<content>Before long her pulses quieted; her heart, beating steadily, was once again the well-managed steed upon which her high courage could ride to victory.</content>
<content>And, all the while, her eyes never left the white figure; knowing it knew itself discovered and observed.</content>
<content>Her hand was still upon the key.</content>
<content>She turned it, and withdrew it from the lock.</content>
<content>A deafening crash of thunder shook the walls.</content>
<content>A swirl of wind and rain beat on the door.</content>
<content>When the last echo of the thunder had died away, the Prioress spoke; and that calm voice, sounding amid the storm, fell on the only ears that heard it, like the Voice of Power on Galilee, which bid the tempest cease, and the wild waves be still.</content>
<content>"Who art thou, and what doest thou here?"</content>
<content>The figure answered not.</content>
<content>"Art thou a ghostly visitor come back amongst us, from the Realm of the
Unseen?"</content>
<content>The figure made no sign.</content>
<content>"Art thou then flesh and blood, and mortal as ourselves?"</content>
<content>Slowly the figure bowed its head.</content>
<content>"Now I adjure thee by our blessed Lady to tell me truly.</content>
<content>Art thou, in very deed a holy nun, a member of our sacred Order?</content>
<content>Answer me, yea or nay?"</content>
<content>The figure shook its head.</content>
<content>The Prioress advanced a step, passed the key into her left hand and, slipping her right beneath her scapulary, took firm grip of the dagger at her girdle.</content>
<content>"Then, masquerader in our sacred dress," she said, "to me you have to answer for double sacrilege: the wearing of these robes, and your presence here, unbidden.</content>
<content>I warn you that your life has never hung by frailer thread than now it hangs.</content>
<content>Your only hope of safety lies in doing as I bid you.</content>
<content>Pass before me along this passage until you reach a chamber on the right, of which the door stands open.</content>
<content>Enter, and place yourself against the wall on the side farthest from the door.</content>
<content>There I will speak with you."</content>
<content>With the shuffling steps of a woman, and the bent shoulders of the very old, the figure moved slowly forward, stepped upon the front of the white robe, stumbled, but recovered.</content>
<content>The Prioress watching, laughed a short scornful laugh, holding more of anger than of merriment.</content>
<content>With an abrupt movement the figure straightened, stood at its full height, and strode forward.</content>
<content>The Prioress marked the squaring of the broad shoulders; the height, greater than her own, though she was more than common tall; the stride, beneath the folds of the long robe; and she knit her level brows, for well she knew with whom she had to deal.</content>
<content>She was called to face a desperate danger.</content>
<content>Single-handed, she had to meet a subtle foe.</content>
<content>She asked no help from others, but she took no needless risks.</content>
<content>As she passed the cell of Mary Seraphine, using her master-key, she locked that lady in!</content>
<content>CHAPTER X

"I KNOW YOU FOR A MAN"

Entering her cell, the Prioress saw at once that her orders had been obeyed.</content>
<content>The hooded figure stood on the far side of the chamber, leaning broad shoulders against the wall.</content>
<content>Under the cape, the arms were folded; she could see that the feet were crossed beneath the robe.</content>
<content>The dress was indeed the dress of a White Lady, but the form within it was so obviously that of a man a big man, at bay, and inclined to be defiant that, despite the strange situation, despite her anger, and her fears, the contrast between the holy habit and its hidden wearer, forced from the Prioress an unwilling smile.</content>
<content>Closing the door, she drew forward a chair of dark Spanish wood, the gift of the Lord Bishop; a chair which well betokened the dignity of her high office.</content>
<content>Seating herself, she laid her left hand lightly upon the mane of one of the carved lions which formed, on either side, the arms of the chair; but her right hand still gripped unseen the ivory hilt; while leaning slightly forward, with feet firmly planted, she was ready at any moment to spring erect.</content>
<content>"I know you for a man," she said.</content>
<content>The thunder rumbled far away in the distance.</content>
<content>The rain still splashed against the casement, but the storm had spent itself; the sky was brightening.</content>
<content>A pale slant of sunshine broke through the parting clouds and, entering the casement, gleamed on the jewelled cross at the breast of the Prioress, and kindled into peculiar radiance the searching light of her clear eyes.</content>
<content>"I know you for a man," she said again.</content>
<content>"You stand there, revealed; and surely you stand there, shamed.</content>
<content>By plotting and planning, by assuming our dress, you have succeeded in forcing your undesired presence into this sacred cloister, where dwells a little company of women who have left the world, never to return to it again; who have given up much in order to devote themselves to a life of continual worship and adoration, gaining thereby a power in intercession which brings down blessing upon those who still fight life's battles in the world without.</content>
<content>"But it has meant the breaking of many a tender tie.</content>
<content>There are fathers and brothers dear to them, whom the nuns would love to see again; but they cannot do so, save, on rare occasions, in the guest-room at the gate; and then, with the grille between.</content>
<content>"Saving Bishop or Priest, no foot of man may tread our cloisters; no voice of man may be heard in these cells.</content>
<content>"Yet by trick and subterfuge you have intruded.</content>
<content>Methinks I scarce should let you leave this place alive, to boast what you have done."</content>
<content>The Prioress paused.</content>
<content>The figure stood, with folded arms, immovable, leaning against the
wall.</content>
<content>There was a quality in this motionless silence such as the
Prioress had not connected with her idea of Mary Seraphine's "Cousin
Wilfred."</content>
<content>This was not a man to threaten.</content>
<content>Her threat came back to her, as if she had flung it against a stone wall.</content>
<content>She tried another line of reasoning.</content>
<content>"I know you, Sir Wilfred," she said.</content>
<content>"And I know why you are here.</content>
<content>You have come to tempt away, or mayhap, if possible, to force away one of our number who but lately took her final vows.</content>
<content>There was a time, not long ago, when you might have thwarted her desire to seek and find the best and highest.</content>
<content>But now you come too late.</content>
<content>No bride of Heaven turns from her high estate.</content>
<content>Her choice is made.</content>
<content>She will abide by it; and so, Sir Knight, must you."</content>
<content>The rain had ceased.</content>
<content>The storm was over.</content>
<content>Sunshine flooded the cell.</content>
<content>Once more the Prioress spoke, and her voice was gentle.</content>
<content>"I know the disappointment to you must be grievous.</content>
<content>You took great risks; you adventured much.</content>
<content>How long you have plotted this intrusion, I know not.</content>
<content>You have been thwarted in your evil purpose by the faithfulness of one old woman, our aged lay-sister, Mary Antony, who never fails to count the White Ladies as they go and as they return, and who reported at once to me that one more had returned than went.</content>
<content>"Do you not see in this the Hand of God?</content>
<content>Will you not bow in penitence before Him, confessing the sinfulness of the thing you had in mind to do?"</content>
<content>The shrouded head was lifted higher, as if with a proud gesture of disavowal.</content>
<content>At the same time, the hood slightly parting, the hand of a man, lean and brown, gripped it close.</content>
<content>The Prioress looked long at that lean, brown hand.</content>
<content>Then she rose slowly to her feet.</content>
<content>"Shew me thy face," she said; and the tension of each word was like a naked blade passing in and out of quivering flesh.</content>
<content>At sound of it the figure stood erect, took one step forward, flung back the hood, tore open the robe and scapulary, loosing his arms from the wide sleeves.</content>
<content>And as the hood fell back the Prioress found herself looking into a face she had not thought to see again in life the face of him who once had been her lover.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XI

THE YEARS ROLL BACK

"Hugh!"</content>
<content>exclaimed the Prioress.</content>
<content>And again, in utter bewilderment: "Hugh?"</content>
<content>And yet a third time, in a low whisper of horror, passing her left hand across her eyes, as if to clear from her outer vision some nightmare of the inner mind: "Hugh!"</content>
<content>The silent Knight still made no answer; but he flung aside the clinging robes, stepped from out them, and strode forward, both arms outstretched.</content>
<content>"Back!"</content>
<content>cried the Prioress.</content>
<content>But her hand had left the hilt of the dagger.</content>
<content>"Come no nearer," she commanded.</content>
<content>Then she sank into her chair, spreading her trembling hands upon the carven manes of the lions.</content>
<content>The Knight, still silent, folded his arms across his breast.</content>
<content>Thus for a space they gazed on one another these two, who had parted, eight years before, with clinging lips and straining arms, a deep, pure passion of love surging within them; a union of heart, made closer by the wrench of outward separation.</content>
<content>The Knight looked at the lips of the noble woman before him; and as he looked those firm lips quivered, trembled, parted 

Then the years rolled back 

           

It was moonlight on the battlements.</content>
<content>The horses champed in the courtyard below.</content>
<content>They two had climbed to the topmost turret, that they might part as near the stars as possible, and that, unseen by others, she might watch him ride away.</content>
<content>How radiant she looked, in her robe of sapphire velvet, jewels at her breast and girdle, a mantle of ermine hanging from her shoulders.</content>
<content>But brighter than any jewels were the eyes full of love and tears; and softer than softest velvet, the beautiful hair which, covered her, as with a golden veil.</content>
<content>Standing with his arms around her, it flowed over his hands.</content>
<content>Silent he stood, looking deep into her eyes.</content>
<content>Below they could hear Martin Goodfellow calling to the men-at-arms.</content>
<content>Her lips being free, she spoke.</content>
<content>"Thou wilt come back to me, Hugh," she said.</content>
<content>"The Saracens will not slay thee, will not wound thee, will not touch thee.</content>
<content>My love will ever be around thee, as a silver shield."</content>
<content>She flung her strong young arms about him, long and supple, enfolding him closely, even as his enfolded her.</content>
<content>He filled his hands with her soft hair, straining her closer.</content>
<content>"I would I left thee wife, not maid.</content>
<content>Could I have wed thee first, I would go with a lighter heart."</content>
<content>"Wife or maid," she answered, her face lifted to his, "I am all thine own.</content>
<content>Go with a light heart, dear man of mine, for it makes no difference.</content>
<content>Maid or wife, I am thine, and none other's, forever."</content>
<content>"Let those be the last words I hear thee say," he murmured, as his lips sought hers.</content>
<content>So, a little later, standing above him on the turret steps, she bent and clasped her hands about his head, pushing her fingers into the thickness of his hair.</content>
<content>Then: "Maid or wife," she said, and her voice now steady, was deep and tender; "Maid or wife, God knows, I am all thine own."</content>
<content>Then she caught his face to her breast.</content>
<content>"Thine and none other's, forever," she said; and he felt her bosom heave with one deep sob.</content>
<content>Then turning quickly he ran down the winding stair, reached the courtyard, mounted, and rode out through the gates of Castle Norelle, and into the fir wood; and so down south to follow the King, who already had started on the great Crusade.</content>
<content>And, as he rode, in moonlight or in shadow, always he saw the sweet lips that trembled, always he felt the soft heave of that sob, and the low voice so tender, said: "Thine and none other's, forever."</content>
<content>And now 

The Prioress sat in her chair of state.</content>
<content>Each moment her face grew calmer and more stern.</content>
<content>The Knight let his eyes dwell on the fingers which once crept so tenderly into his hair.</content>
<content>She hid them beneath her scapulary, as if his gaze scorched them.</content>
<content>He looked at the bosom against which his head had been pressed.</content>
<content>A jewelled cross gleamed, there where his face had laid hidden.</content>
<content>Then the Knight lifted his eyes again to that stern, cold face.</content>
<content>Yet still he kept silence.</content>
<content>At length the Prioress spoke.</content>
<content>"So it is you," she said.</content>
<content>"Yes," said the Knight, "it is I."</content>
<content>Wroth with her own poor heart because it thrilled at his voice, the
Prioress spoke with anger.</content>
<content>"How did you dare to force your way into this sacred cloister?"</content>
<content>The Knight smiled.</content>
<content>"I have yet to find the thing I dare not do."</content>
<content>"Why are you not with your wife?"</content>
<content>demanded the Prioress; and her tone was terrible.</content>
<content>"I am with my wife," replied the Knight.</content>
<content>"The only wife I have ever wanted, the only woman I shall ever wed, is here."</content>
<content>"Coward!"</content>
<content>cried the Prioress, white with anger.</content>
<content>"Traitor!"</content>
<content>She leaned forward, clenching her hands upon the lions' heads.</content>
<content>"Liar!</content>
<content>You wedded your cousin, Alfrida, less than one year after you went from me."</content>
<content>"Cease to be angry," said the Knight.</content>
<content>"Thine anger affrights me not, yet it hurts thyself.</content>
<content>Listen, mine own beloved, and I will tell thee the cruel, and yet blessed, truth.</content>
<content>"Seven months after I left thee, a messenger reached our camp, bearing letters from England; no word for me from thee; but a long missive from thy half-sister Eleanor, breaking to me the news that, being weary of my absence, and somewhat over-persuaded, thou hadst wedded Humphry; Earl of Carnforth.</content>
<content>"It was no news to me, that Humphry sought to win thee; but, that thou hadst let thyself be won away from thy vow to me, was hell's own tidings.</content>
<content>"In my first rage of grief I would have speech with none.</content>
<content>But, by-and-by, I sought the messenger, and asked him casually of things at home.</content>
<content>He told me he had seen thy splendid nuptials with the lord of Carnforth, had been present at the marriage, and joined in the after revels and festivities.</content>
<content>He said thou didst make a lovely bride, but somewhat sad, as if thy mind strayed elsewhere.</content>
<content>The fellow was a kind of lawyer's clerk, but lean, and out at elbow.</content>
<content>"Then I sought 'Frida, my cousin.</content>
<content>She too had had a letter, giving the news.</content>
<content>She told me she long had feared this thing for me, knowing the heart of Humphry to be set on winning thee, and that Eleanor approved his suit, and having already heard that of late thou hadst inclined to smile on him.</content>
<content>She begged me to do nothing rash or hasty.</content>
<content>"'What good were it,' she said, 'to beg the King for leave to hasten home?</content>
<content>If you kill Humphry, Hugh, you do but make a widow of the woman you have loved; nor could you wed the widow of a man yourself had slain.</content>
<content>If Humphry kills you well, a valiant arm is lost to the Holy Cause, and other hearts, more faithful than hers, may come nigh to breaking.</content>
<content>Stay here, and play the man.'</content>
<content>"So, by the messenger, I sent thee back a letter, asking thee to write me word how it was that thou, being my betrothed, hadst come to do this thing; and whether Humphry was good to thee, and making thy life pleasant.</content>
<content>To Humphry I sent a letter saying that, thy love being round him as a silver shield, I would not slay him, wound him, or touch him!</content>
<content>But if he used thee ill, or gave thee any grief or sorrow, then would I come, forthwith, and send him straight to hell.</content>
<content>"These letters, with others from the camp, went back to England by that clerkly messenger.</content>
<content>No answers were returned to mine.</content>
<content>"Meanwhile I went, with my despair, out to the battlefield.</content>
<content>"No tender shield was round me any more.</content>
<content>I fought, like a mad wild beast.</content>
<content>So often was I wounded, that they dubbed me 'The Knight of the Bloody Vest.'</content>
<content>"At last they brought me back to camp, delirious and dying.</content>
<content>My cousin 'Frida, there biding her time, nursed me back to life, and sought to win for herself  I shame to say it  the love which thou hadst flouted.</content>
<content>I need not tell thee, my cousin 'Frida failed.</content>
<content>The Queen herself as good as bid me wed her favourite Lady.</content>
<content>The Queen herself had to discover that she could command an English soldier's life, but not his love.</content>
<content>"Back in the field again, I found myself one day, cut off, surrounded, hewn down, taken prisoner; but by a generous foe.</content>
<content>"Thereafter followed years of much adventure; escapes, far distant wanderings, strange company.</content>
<content>Many months I spent in a mountain fastness with a wise Hebrew Rabbi, who taught me his sacred Scriptures; going back to the beginning of all things, before the world was; yet shrewd in judgment of the present, and throwing a weird light forward upon the future.</content>
<content>A strange man; wise, as are all of that Chosen Race; and a faithful friend.</content>
<content>He did much to heal my hurt and woo me back to sanity.</content>
<content>"Later, more than a year with a band of holy monks in a desert monastery, high among the rocks; good Fathers who believed in Greek and Latin as surest of all balsams for a wounded spirit, and who made me to become deeply learned in Apostolic writings, and in the teachings of the Church.</content>
<content>But, for all their best endeavours, I could not feel called to the perpetual calm of the Cloister.</content>
<content>We are a line of fighters and hunters, men to whom pride of race and love of hearth and home, are primal instincts.</content>
<content>"Thus, after many further wanderings and much varying adventure, having by a strange chance heard news of the death of my father, and that my mother mourned.</content>
<content>In solitude, the opening of this year found me landed in England I who, by most, had long been given up for dead; though Martin Goodfellow, failing to find trace of me in Palestine, had gone back to Cumberland, and staunchly maintained his belief that I lived, a captive, and should some day make my escape, and return.</content>
<content>"I passed with all speed to our Castle on the moors, knowing a mother's heart waited here, for mothers never cease to watch and hope.</content>
<content>And, sure enough, as I rode up, the great doors flew wide; the house waited its master; the mother was on the threshold to greet her son.</content>
<content>Aye!</content>
<content>It was good to be at home once more even in the land where my woman was bearing children to another man.</content>
<content>"We spent a few happy days, I and my mother, together.</content>
<content>Then the joy of hope fulfilled being sometimes a swifter harbinger to another world than the heaviest load of sorrow she passed, without pain or sickness, smiling, in her sleep; she passed leaving my home desolate indeed.</content>
<content>"Not having known of my betrothal to thee, because of the old feud between our families, and my reluctance to cross her wish that I should wed Alfrida, thy name was not spoken between us; but I learned from her that my cousin 'Frida lay dying at her manor, nigh to Chester, of some lingering disease contracted in eastern lands."</content>
<content>"With the first stirrings of Spring in forest and pasture, I felt moved to ride south to the Court, and report my return to the King; yet waited, strangely loath to go abroad where any turn of the road might bring me face to face with Humphry.</content>
<content>I doubted, should we meet, if I could pass, without slaying him, the man who had stolen my betrothed from me.</content>
<content>So I stayed in my own domain, bringing things into order, working in the armoury, and striving by hard exercise to throttle the grim demon of despair.</content>
<content>"April brought a burst of early summer; and, on the first day of May, I set off for Windsor.</content>
<content>"Passing through Carnforth on my way, I found the town keeping high holiday.</content>
<content>I asked the reason, and was told of a Tourney now in progress in the neighbourhood, to which the Earl had that morning ridden in state, accompanied by his Countess, who indeed was chosen Queen of Beauty, and was to sit enthroned, attended by her little daughter, two tiny sons acting as pages.</content>
<content>"A sudden mad desire came on me, to look upon thy face again; to see thee with the man who stole thee from me; with the children, who should have been mine own.</content>
<content>"Ten minutes later, I rode on to the field.</content>
<content>Pushing in amid the gay crowd, I seemed almost at once to find myself right in front of the throne.</content>
<content>"I saw the Queen of Beauty, in cloth of gold.</content>
<content>I saw the little maiden and the pages in attendance.</content>
<content>I saw Humphry, proud husband and father, beside them.</content>
<content>All this I saw, which I had come to see.</content>
<content>But the face of Humphry's Countess was not thy face!</content>
<content>In that moment I knew that, for seven long years, I had been fooled!</content>
<content>"I started on a frenzied quest after the truth, and news of thee.</content>
<content>"Thy sister Eleanor had died the year before.</content>
<content>To thy beautiful castle and lands, so near mine own, Eleanor's son had succeeded, and ruled there in thy stead.</content>
<content>He being at Court just then, I saw him not, nor could I hear direct news of thee, though rumour said a convent.</content>
<content>"Then I remembered my cousin, Alfrida, lying sick at her manor in Chester.</content>
<content>To her I went; and, walking in unannounced I, whom she had long thought dead I forced the truth from her.</content>
<content>The whole plot stood revealed.</content>
<content>She and Eleanor had hatched it between them.</content>
<content>Eleanor desiring thy lands for herself and her boy, and knowing children of thine would put hers out of succession; Alfrida it shames me to say it desiring for herself, thy lover.</content>
<content>"The messenger who brought the letters was bribed to give details of thy supposed marriage.</content>
<content>On his return to England, my letters to thee and to Humphry he handed to Eleanor; also a lying letter from 'Frida, telling of her marriage with me, with the Queen's consent and approval, and asking Eleanor to break the news to thee.</content>
<content>The messenger then mingled with thy household, describing my nuptials in detail, as, when abroad, he had done thine.</content>
<content>Hearing of this, my poor Love did even as I had done, sent for him, questioned him, heard the full tale he had to tell, and saw, alas!</content>
<content>no reason to misdoubt him.</content>
<content>"By the way, my cousin 'Frida knew where to lay her hand upon that clerkly fellow.</content>
<content>Therefore we sent for him.</content>
<content>He came in haste to see the Lady Alfrida, from whom, during all the years, he had extorted endless hush-money.</content>
<content>"I and my men awaited him.</content>
<content>"He had fattened on his hush-money!</content>
<content>He was no longer lean and out at elbow.</content>
<content>"He screeched at sight of me, thinking me risen from the dead.</content>
<content>"He screeched still louder when he saw the noose, flung over a strong bough.</content>
<content>"We left him hanging, when we rode away.</content>
<content>That Judas kind will do the darkest deeds for greed of gain.</content>
<content>The first of the tribe himself shewed the way by which it was most fitting to speed them from a world into which it had been good for them never to have been born.</content>
<content>"From Alfrida I learned that, as Eleanor had foreseen, thy grief at my perfidy drove thee to the Cloister.</content>
<content>Also that thy Convent was near Worcester.</content>
<content>"To Worcester I came, and made myself known to the Lord Bishop, with whom I supped; and finding him most pleasant to talk with, and ready to understand, deemed it best, in perfect frankness, to tell him the whole matter; being careful not to mention thy name, nor to give any clue to thy person.</content>
<content>"Through chance remarks let fall by the Bishop while giving me the history of the Order, I learned that already thou wert Prioress of the White Ladies.</content>
<content>'The youngest Prioress in the kingdom,' said the Bishop, 'yet none could be wiser or better fitted to hold high authority.'</content>
<content>Little did he dream that any mention of thee was as water to the parched desert; yet he talked on, for love of speaking of thee, while I sat praying he might tell me more; yet barely answering yea or nay, seeming to be absorbed in mine own melancholy thoughts.</content>
<content>"From the Bishop I learned that the Order was a strictly close one, and that no man could, on any pretext whatsoever, gain speech alone with one of the White Ladies.</content>
<content>"But I also heard of the underground way leading from the Cathedral to the Convent, and of the daily walk to and from Vespers.</content>
<content>"I went to the crypt, and saw the doorway through which the White Ladies pass.</content>
<content>Standing unseen amid the many pillars, I daily watched the long line of silent figures, noted that they all walked veiled, with faces hidden, keeping a measured distance apart.</content>
<content>Also that several were above usual height.</content>
<content>Then I conceived the plan of wearing the outer dress, and of stepping in amongst those veiled figures just at the foot of the winding stair in the wall, leading down from the clerestory to the crypt.</content>
<content>I marked that the nun descending, could not keep in view the nun in front who had just stepped forth into the crypt; while she, moving forward, would not perceive it if, slipping from behind a pillar, another white figure silently joined the procession behind her.</content>
<content>Once within the Convent, I trusted to our Lady to help me to speech alone with thee; and our blessed Lady hath not failed me.</content>
<content>"Now I have told thee all."</content>
<content>With that the Knight left speaking; and, after the long steady recitation, the ceasing of his voice caused a silence which, seemed, to hold the very air suspended.</content>
<content>Not once had the Prioress made interruption.</content>
<content>She had sat immovable, her eyes upon his face, her hands gripping the arms of her chair.</content>
<content>Long before the tale was finished her sad eyes had overflowed, the tears raining down her cheeks, and falling upon the cross at her breast.</content>
<content>When he had told all, when the deep, manly voice now resolute, now eager, now vibrant with fierce indignation, yet tender always when speaking of her at last fell silent, the Prioress fought with her emotion, and mastered it; then, so soon as she could safely trust her voice, she spoke.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XII

ALAS, THE PITY OF IT!</content>
<content>At length the Prioress spoke.</content>
<content>"Alas," she said, "the pity of it!</content>
<content>Ah, the cruel, cruel pity of it!"</content>
<content>Her voice, so sweet and tender, yet so hopeless in the unquestioning finality of its regret, struck cold upon the heart of the Knight.</content>
<content>"But, my beloved, I have found thee," he said, and dropping upon one knee at her feet, he put out his hands to cover both hers.</content>
<content>But the Prioress was too quick for him.</content>
<content>She hid her hands beneath her scapulary.</content>
<content>The Knight's brown fingers closed on the lions' heads.</content>
<content>"Touch me not," said the Prioress.</content>
<content>The Knight flushed, darkly.</content>
<content>"You are mine," he said.</content>
<content>"Mine to have and to keep.</content>
<content>During these wretched years we have schooled ourselves each to think of the other as wedded.</content>
<content>Now we know that neither has been faithless.</content>
<content>I have found thee, my beloved, and I will not let thee go."</content>
<content>"Hugh," said the Prioress, "I am wedded.</content>
<content>You come too late.</content>
<content>Saw you not the sacred ring upon my hand?</content>
<content>Know you not that every nun is the bride of Christ?"</content>
<content>"You are mine!"</content>
<content>said the Knight, fiercely; and he laid his great hand upon her knee.</content>
<content>From beneath her scapulary, the Prioress drew the dagger.</content>
<content>"Before I went to the cloister door," she said, "I took this from its hiding-place, and put it in my girdle.</content>
<content>I guessed I had a man to deal with; though, Heaven knows, I dreamed not it was thou!</content>
<content>But I tell thee, Hugh, if thou, or any man, attempt to lay defiling touch upon any nun in this Priory myself, or another I strike, and I strike home.</content>
<content>This blade will be driven up to the hilt in the offender's heart."</content>
<content>The Knight rose to his feet, stepped to the window and leaned, with folded arms, against the wall.</content>
<content>"Put back thy weapon," he said, sternly, "into its hiding-place.</content>
<content>No other man is here; yet, should another come, my sword would well suffice to guard thine honour, and the honour of thy nuns."</content>
<content>She looked at his dark face, scornful in its pain; then went at once, obedient, to the secret panel.</content>
<content>"Yes, Hugh," she said.</content>
<content>"That much of trust indeed I owe thy love."</content>
<content>As she placed the dagger in the wall and closed the panel, something fell from her, intangible, yet real.</content>
<content>For so long, she had had to command.</content>
<content>Bowing, kneeling, hurrying women flew to do her behests.</content>
<content>Each vied with the others to magnify her Office.</content>
<content>Often, she felt lonely by reason of her dignity.</content>
<content>And now a man's dark face frowned on her in scornful anger; a man's stern voice flung back her elaborate threat with a short command, which disarmed her, yet which she obeyed.</content>
<content>Moreover, she found it strangely sweet to obey.</content>
<content>Behind the sternness, behind the scornful anger, there throbbed a great love.</content>
<content>In that love she trusted; but with that love she had to deal, putting it from her with a finality which should be beyond question.</content>
<content>Yet the "Prioress" fell from her, as she closed the panel.</content>
<content>It was the
Woman and the Saint who moved over to the window and stood beside the
Knight, in the radiance of a golden sunset after storm.</content>
<content>There was about her, as she spoke, a wistful humbleness; and a patient sadness, infinitely touching.</content>
<content>"Sir Hugh," she said, "my dear Knight, whom I ever found brave and tender, and whom I now know to have been always loyal and true there is no need that I should add a word to your recital.</content>
<content>The facts you wrung from Alfrida God grant forgiveness to that tormented heart are all true.</content>
<content>Believing the messenger, not dreaming of doubting Eleanor, my one thought was to hide from the world my broken heart, my shattered pride.</content>
<content>I hastened to offer to God the love and the life which had been slighted by man.</content>
<content>I confess this has since seemed to me but a poor second-best to have brought to Him, Who indeed should have our very best.</content>
<content>But, daily kneeling at His Feet, I said: 'A broken and a contrite heart, Lord, Thou wilt not despise.'</content>
<content>My heart was 'broken,' when I brought it here.</content>
<content>It has been 'contrite' since.</content>
<content>And well I know, although so far from worthy, it has not been despised."</content>
<content>She lifted her eyes to the golden glory behind the battlements of purple cloud.</content>
<content>"Our blessed Lady interceded," she said, simply; 'she, who understands a woman's heart."</content>
<content>The Knight was breathing hard.</content>
<content>The folded arms rose and fell, with the heaving of his chest.</content>
<content>But he kept his lips firm shut; though praying, all the while, that our Lady might have, also, some understanding of the heart of a man!</content>
<content>"I think it right that you should know, dear Hugh," went on the sad voice, gently; "that, at first, I suffered greatly.</content>
<content>I spent long agonizing nights, kneeling before our Lady's shrine, imploring strength to conquer the love and the longing which had become sin."</content>
<content>A stifled groan broke from the Knight.</content>
<content>The golden light shone in her steadfast eyes, and played about her noble brow.</content>
<content>"And strength was given," she said, very low.</content>
<content>"Mora!"</content>
<content>cried the Knight She started.</content>
<content>It was so long since she had heard her own name "You prayed for strength to conquer, when you thought it sin; just as I rode out to meet the foe, to fight and slay, and afterward wrestled with unknown tongues, doing all those things which were hardest, while striving to quench my love for you.</content>
<content>But when I knew that no other man had right to you or ever had had right, why then I found that nothing had slain my love, nor ever could.</content>
<content>And Mora, now you know that I am free, is your love dead?"</content>
<content>She clasped her hands over the cross at her breast.</content>
<content>His voice held a deep passion of appeal; yet he strove, loyally, to keep it calm.</content>
<content>"Listen, Hugh," she said.</content>
<content>"If, thinking me faithless, you had turned for consolation to another; if, though you brought her but your second best, you yet had won and wed her; now, finding after all that I had not wedded Humphry, would you leave your bride, and try to wake again your love for me?"</content>
<content>"You seek to place me," he said, "in straits in which, by mine own act, I shall never be.</content>
<content>Loving you as I love you, I could wed no other while you live."</content>
<content>She paled, but persisted.</content>
<content>"But, if, Hugh?</content>
<content>If?"</content>
<content>"Then, no," he said.</content>
<content>"I should not leave one I had wed.</content>
<content>But "

"Hugh," she said, "thinking you faithless, I took the holy vows which wedded me to Heaven.</content>
<content>How can I leave my heavenly Bridegroom, for love of any man upon this earth?"</content>
<content>"Not 'any man,'" he answered; "but your betrothed, returned to claim you; the man to whom you said as parting words: 'Maid or wife, I am all thine own; thine and none other's forever.'</content>
<content>Ah, that brings the warm blood to thy cheek!</content>
<content>Oh, my Heart's Life, if it was true then, it is true still!</content>
<content>God is not a man that he should lie, or rob another of his bride.</content>
<content>If I had wed another woman, I should have done that thing, honestly believing thee the wife of another man.</content>
<content>But, all these years, while thou and I were both deceived, He, Who knoweth all, has known the truth.</content>
<content>He knew thee betrothed to me.</content>
<content>He heard thee say, upon the battlements, when last we stood together: 'God knows, I am all thine own.'</content>
<content>He knew how, when I thought I had lost thee, I yet lived faithful to the pure memory of our love.</content>
<content>The day thy vows were made, He knew that I was free, and thou, therefore, still pledged to me.</content>
<content>Shall a man rob God?</content>
<content>Ay, he may.</content>
<content>But shall God rob a man?</content>
<content>Nay, then, never!"</content>
<content>She trembled, wavered; then fled to the shrine of the Virgin, kneeling with hands outstretched.</content>
<content>"Holy Mother of God," she sobbed, "teach him that I dare not do this thing!</content>
<content>Shew him that I cannot break my vows.</content>
<content>Help him to understand that I would not, if I could."</content>
<content>He followed, and kneeled beside her; his proud head bent; his voice breaking with emotion.</content>
<content>"Blessed Virgin," he said.</content>
<content>"Thou who didst dwell in the earthly home at Nazareth, help this woman of mine to understand, that if she break her troth to me, holding herself from me, now when I am come to claim her, she sends me forth to an empty life, to a hearth beside which no woman will sit, to a home forever desolate."</content>
<content>Together they knelt, before the tender image of Mother and Child; together, yet apart; he, loyally mindful not so much as to brush against a fold of her veil.</content>
<content>The dark face, and the fair, were lifted, side by side, as they knelt before the Madonna.</content>
<content>For a while so motionless they kneeled, they might have been finely-modelled figures; he, bronze; she, marble.</content>
<content>Then, with a sudden movement, she put out her right hand, and caught his left.</content>
<content>Firmly his fingers closed over hers; but he drew no nearer.</content>
<content>Yet as they knelt thus with clasped hands, his pulsing life seemed to flow through her, undoing, in one wild, sweet moment, the work of years of fast and vigil.</content>
<content>"Ah, Hugh," she cried, suddenly, 'spare me!</content>
<content>Spare me!</content>
<content>Tempt me not!"</content>
<content>Loosing her hand from his, she clasped both upon her breast.</content>
<content>The Knight rose, and stood beside her.</content>
<content>"Mora," he said, and his voice held a new tone, a tone of sadness and solemnity; "far be it from me to tempt you.</content>
<content>I will plead with you but once again, in presence of our Lady and of the Holy Child; and, having so done, I will say no more.</content>
<content>"I ask you to leave this place, which you would never have entered had you known your lover was yours, and needing you.</content>
<content>I ask you to keep your plighted word to me, and to become my wife.</content>
<content>If you refuse, I go, returning not again.</content>
<content>I leave you here, to kneel in peace, by night or day, before the shrine of the Madonna.</content>
<content>But I bid you to remember, day and night, that because of this which you have done, there can be no Madonna in my home.</content>
<content>No woman will ever sit beside my hearth, holding a little child upon her knees.</content>
<content>"You leave to me the crucifix heart broken, love betrayed; feet and hands nailed to the wood of cruel circumstance; side pierced by spear of treachery lonely, forsaken.</content>
<content>But you take from me all the best, both in life and in religion; all that tells of love, of joy, of hope for the years to come.</content>
<content>"Oh, my beloved, weigh it well!</content>
<content>There are so many, with a true vocation, serving Heaven in Convent and in Cloister.</content>
<content>There is but one woman in the whole world for me.</content>
<content>In the sight of Heaven, nothing divides us.</content>
<content>Convent walls now stand between but they were built by man, not God.</content>
<content>Vows of celibacy were not meant to sunder loving hearts.</content>
<content>Mora?</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Come!"</content>
<content>The Prioress rose and faced him.</content>
<content>"I cannot come," she said.</content>
<content>"That which I have taught to others, I must myself perform.</content>
<content>Hugh, I am dead to the world; and if I be dead to the world, how can I live to you?</content>
<content>Had I, in very deed, died and been entombed, you would not have gone down into the vaults and forced my resting-place, that you might look upon my face, clasp my cold hand, and pour into deaf ears a tale of love.</content>
<content>Yet that is what, by trick and artifice, you now have done.</content>
<content>You come to a dead woman, saying; 'Love me, and be my wife.'</content>
<content>She must, perforce, make answer: 'How shall I, who am dead to the world, live any longer therein?'</content>
<content>Take a wife from among the Living, Hugh.</content>
<content>Come not to seek a bride among the Dead."</content>
<content>"Mother of God!"</content>
<content>exclaimed the Knight, "is this religion?"</content>
<content>He turned to the window, then to the door.</content>
<content>"How can I go from here?"</content>
<content>The stifled horror in his voice chilled the very soul of the woman to whom he spoke.</content>
<content>She had, indeed at last made him to understand.</content>
<content>"I must get you hence unseen," she said.</content>
<content>"I dare not pass you out by the Convent gate.</content>
<content>I fear me, you must go back the way you came; nor can you go alone.</content>
<content>We hold the key to unlock the door leading from our passage into the Cathedral crypt.</content>
<content>I will now send all the nuns to the Refectory.</content>
<content>Then I myself must take you to the crypt."</content>
<content>"Can I not walk alone," asked the Knight, brusquely; "returning you the key by messenger?"</content>
<content>"Nay," said the Prioress, "I dare run no risks.</content>
<content>So quickly rumours are afloat.</content>
<content>To-morrow, this strange hour must be a dream; and you and I alone, the dreamers.</content>
<content>Now, while I go and make safe the way, put you on again the robe and hood.</content>
<content>When I return and beckon, follow silently."</content>
<content>The Prioress passed out, closing the door behind her.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XIII

"SEND HER TO ME!"</content>
<content>The Prioress stood for a moment outside the closed door.</content>
<content>The peaceful silence of the passage helped her to the outward calm which must be hers before she could bring herself to face her nuns.</content>
<content>Moving slowly to the farther end, she unlocked the cell of Sister Mary Seraphine, feeling a shamed humility that she should have made so sure she had to deal with "Wilfred," and have thought such scorn of him and Seraphine.</content>
<content>Alas!</content>
<content>The wrong deeds of those they love, oft humble the purest, noblest spirits into the soiling dust.</content>
<content>Next, the Prioress herself rang the Refectory bell.</content>
<content>The hour for the evening meal was long passed; the nuns hastened out, readily.</content>
<content>As they trooped toward the stairs leading down to the Refectory, they saw their Prioress, very pale, very erect, standing with her back to the door of her chamber.</content>
<content>Each nun made a genuflexion as she passed; and to each, the Prioress slightly inclined her head.</content>
<content>To Sister Mary Rebecca, who kneeled at once, she spoke: "I come not to the meal this evening.</content>
<content>In the absence of Mother Sub-Prioress, you will take my place."</content>
<content>"Yes, Reverend Mother," said Sister Mary Rebecca, meekly, and kissed the hem of the robe of the Prioress; then rising, hastened on, charmed to have a position of authority, however temporary.</content>
<content>When all had passed, the Prioress went into the cloisters, walked round them; looked over into the garden, observing every possible place from which prying eyes might have sight of the way from the passage to the crypt entrance.</content>
<content>But the garden, already full of purple shadows, was left to the circling swifts.</content>
<content>The robin sang an evening song from the bough, of the pieman's tree.</content>
<content>The Prioress returned along the passage, looking into every cell.</content>
<content>Each door stood open wide; each cell was empty.</content>
<content>The sick nuns were on a further passage, round the corner, beyond the Refectory stairs.</content>
<content>Yet she passed along this also, making sure that the door of each occupied cell was shut.</content>
<content>Standing motionless at the top of the Refectory steps, she could hear the distant clatter of platters, the shuffling feet of the lay-sisters as they carried the dishes to and from the kitchens; and, above it all, the monotonous voice of Sister Mary Rebecca reading aloud to the nuns while they supped.</content>
<content>Then the Prioress took down one of the crypt lanterns and lighted it.</content>
<content>Meanwhile the Knight, left alone, stood for a few moments, as if stunned.</content>
<content>He had played for a big stake and lost; yet he felt more unnerved by the unexpected finality of his own acquiescence in defeat, than by the firm refusal which had brought that defeat about.</content>
<content>It seemed to him, as he now stood alone, that suddenly he had realised the extraordinary detachment wrought by years of cloistered life.</content>
<content>Aflame with love and longing he had come, seeking the Living among the Dead.</content>
<content>It would have been less bitter to have knelt beside her tomb, knowing the heart forever still had, to the last, beat true with love for him; knowing the dead arms, lying cold and stiff, had he come sooner, would have been flung around him; knowing the lips, now silent in death, living, would have called to him in tenderest greeting.</content>
<content>But this cold travesty of the radiant woman he had left, said: "Touch me not," and bade him seek a wife elsewhere; he, who had remained faithful to her, even when he had thought her faithless.</content>
<content>And yet, cold though she was, in her saintly aloofness, she was still the woman he loved.</content>
<content>Moreover she still had the noble carriage, the rich womanly beauty, the look of vital, physical vigour, which marked her out as meant by Nature to be the mother of brave sons and fair daughters.</content>
<content>Yet he must leave her to this!</content>
<content>He looked round the room, noted the low archway leading to the sleeping chamber, took a step toward it, then fell back as from a sanctuary; marked the great table, covered with missals, parchments, and vellum.</content>
<content>It might well have been the cell of a learned monk, rather than the chamber of the woman he loved.</content>
<content>His eye, travelling round, fell upon the Madonna and Child.</content>
<content>In the pure evening light there was a strangely arresting quality about the marble group; something infinitely human in the brooding tenderness of the Mother, as she bent over the smiling Babe.</content>
<content>It spoke of home, rather than of the cloister.</content>
<content>It struck a chord in the heart of the Knight, a chord which rang clear and true, above the jangle of disputation and bitterness.</content>
<content>He put out his hand and touched the little foot of the Holy Babe.</content>
<content>"Mother of God," he said aloud, 'send her to me!</content>
<content>Take pity on a hungry heart, a lonely home, a desolate hearth.</content>
<content>Send her to me!"</content>
<content>Then he lifted from the floor the white robe and hood, and drew them on.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XIV

FAREWELL HERE, AND NOW

When the Prioress, a lighted lantern in her hand, opened the door of her chamber, a tall figure in the dress of the White Ladies of Worcester stood motionless against the wall, facing the door.</content>
<content>"Come!"</content>
<content>she whispered, beckoning; and, noiselessly, it stood beside her.</content>
<content>Then she closed the door and, using her master-key, locked it behind her.</content>
<content>Silently the two white figures passed along the passage, through the cloister, and down the flight of steps into the Convent crypt.</content>
<content>The Prioress unlocked the door and stooping they passed under the arch, and entered the subterranean way.</content>
<content>Placing the lantern on the ground, the Prioress drew out the key, closed the door, and locked it on the inside.</content>
<content>She turned, and lifting the lantern, saw that the Knight had rid himself of his disguise, and now stood before her, very straight and tall, just within the circle of light cast by her lantern.</content>
<content>With the closing and locking of the door a strange sense came over them, as of standing together in a third world neither his nor hers tomblike in its complete isolation and darkness; heavy with a smell of earth and damp stones; the slightest sound reverberating in hollow exaggeration; yet, in itself, silent as the grave.</content>
<content>This tomblike quality in their surroundings seemed to make their own vitality stronger and more palpitating.</content>
<content>The seconds of silence, after the grating of the key in the lock ceased, seemed hours.</content>
<content>Then the Knight spoke.</content>
<content>"Give me the lantern," he said.</content>
<content>She met his eyes.</content>
<content>Again the dignity of her Office slipped from her.</content>
<content>Again it was sweet to obey.</content>
<content>He held the lantern so that its light illumined her face and his.</content>
<content>"Mora," he said, "it is long since thou and I last walked together over the sunny fields, amid buttercups and cowslips, and the sweet-smelling clover.</content>
<content>To-night we walk beneath the fields instead of through them.</content>
<content>We are under the grass, my sweet.</content>
<content>I seem to stand beside thee in the grave.</content>
<content>And truly my hopes lie slain; the promise of our love is dead, and shall soon be buried.</content>
<content>Yet thou and I still live, and now must walk together side by side, the sad ghosts of our former selves.</content>
<content>"So now I ask thee, Mora, for the sake of those past walks among the flowers, to lay thy hand within my arm and walk with me in gentle fellowship, here in this place of gloom and darkness, as, long ago, we walked among the flowers."</content>
<content>His dark eyes searched her face.</content>
<content>An almost youthful eagerness vibrated in his voice.</content>
<content>She hesitated, lifting her eyes to his.</content>
<content>Then slowly moved toward him and laid her hand within his arm.</content>
<content>Then, side by side, they paced on through the darkness; he, in his right hand, holding the lantern, swinging low, to light their feet; she, leaning on his left arm, keeping slow pace with him.</content>
<content>Over their heads, in the meadows, walked lovers, arm in arm; young men and maidens out in the gathering twilight.</content>
<content>All nature, refreshed, poured forth a fragrant sweetness.</content>
<content>But the rose, with its dewy petals, seemed to the youth less sweet than the lips of the maid.</content>
<content>This, he shyly ventured to tell her; whereupon, as she bent to its fragrance, her cheeks reflected the crimson of those delicate folds.</content>
<content>So walked and talked young lovers in the Worcester meadows; little dreaming that, beneath their happy feet, the Knight and the Prioress paced slowly, side by side, through the darkness.</content>
<content>No word passed between them.</content>
<content>With, her hand upon his arm, her face so near his shoulder, his arm pressing her hand closer and closer against his heart, silence said more than speech.</content>
<content>And in silence they walked.</content>
<content>They passed beneath the city wall, under the Foregate.</content>
<content>The Sheriff rode home to supper, well pleased with a stroke of business accomplished in a house in which he had chanced to shelter during the storm.</content>
<content>The good people of Worcester bought and sold in the market.</content>
<content>Men whose day's work was over, hastened to reach the rest and comfort of wife and home.</content>
<content>Crowds jostled gaily through the streets, little dreaming that beneath their hurrying, busy feet, the Knight and the Prioress paced slowly, side by side, through the darkness.</content>
<content>Had the Knight spoken, her mind would have been up in arms to resist him.</content>
<content>But, because he walked in silence, her heart had leisure to remember; and, remembering, it grew sorely tender.</content>
<content>At length they reached the doorway leading into the Cathedral crypt.</content>
<content>The Prioress carried the key in her left hand.</content>
<content>Freeing her right from the grip of his arm, she slipped the key noiselessly into the lock; but, leaving it there unturned, she paused, and faced the Knight.</content>
<content>"Hugh," she said, "I beg you, for my sake and for the sake of all whose fair fame is under my care, to pass through quickly into the crypt, and to go from thence, if possible, unseen, or in such manner as shall prevent any suspicion that you come from out this hidden way.</content>
<content>Tales of wrong are told so readily, and so quickly grow."</content>
<content>"I will observe the utmost caution," said the Knight.</content>
<content>"Hugh," she said, "I grieve to have had, perforce, to disappoint you."</content>
<content>The brave voice shook.</content>
<content>"This is our final farewell.</content>
<content>Do you forgive me, Hugh?</content>
<content>Will you think kindly, if you ever think on me?"</content>
<content>The Knight held the lantern so that its rays illumined both her face and his.</content>
<content>"Mora," he said, "I cannot as yet take thine answer as final.</content>
<content>I will return no more, nor try to speak with thee again.</content>
<content>But five days longer, I shall wait.</content>
<content>I shall have plans made with the utmost care, to bear thee, in safety and unseen, from the Cathedral.</content>
<content>I know the doors are watched, and that all who pass in and out are noted and observed.</content>
<content>But, if thou wilt but come to me, beloved, trust me to know how to guard mine own.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Nay, speak not!</content>
<content>Hear me out.</content>
<content>"Daily, after Vespers, I shall stand hidden among the pillars, close to the winding stair.</content>
<content>One step aside only one step and my arm will be around thee.</content>
<content>A new life of love and home will lie before us.</content>
<content>I shall take thee, safely concealed, to the hostel where I and my men now lodge.</content>
<content>There, horses will stand ready, and we shall ride at once to Warwick.</content>
<content>At Warwick we shall find a priest one in high favour, both in Church and State who knows all, and is prepared to wed us without delay.</content>
<content>After which, by easy stages, my wife, I shall take thee home."</content>
<content>He swung the lantern high.</content>
<content>She saw the lovelight and the triumph, in his eyes.</content>
<content>"I shall take thee home!"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>She stepped back a pace, lifting both hands toward him, palms outward, and stood thus gazing, with eyes full of sorrow.</content>
<content>"My poor Hugh," she whispered; "it is useless to wait.</content>
<content>I shall not come."</content>
<content>"Yet five days," said the Knight, "I shall tarry in Worcester.</content>
<content>Each day, after Vespers, I shall be here."</content>
<content>"Go to-day, dear Hugh.</content>
<content>Ride to Warwick and tell thy priest, that which indeed he should know without the telling: that a nun does not break her vows.</content>
<content>This is our final farewell, Hugh.</content>
<content>Thou hadst best believe it, and go."</content>
<content>"Our last farewell?"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>"Our last."</content>
<content>"Here and now?"</content>
<content>"Here and now, dear Hugh."</content>
<content>Looking into that calm face, so lovely in its sadness, he saw that she meant it.</content>
<content>Of a sudden he knew he had lost her; he knew life's way stretched lonely before him, evermore.</content>
<content>"Yes," he said, "yes.</content>
<content>It is indeed farewell here and now forever."</content>
<content>The dull despair in the voice which, but a few moments before, had vibrated with love and hope, wrung her heart.</content>
<content>She still held her hands before her, as if to ward him off.</content>
<content>"Ah, Hugh," she cried, sharply, "be merciful, and go!</content>
<content>Spare me, and go quickly."</content>
<content>The Knight heard in her voice a tone it had not hitherto held.</content>
<content>But he loved her loyally; therefore he kept his own anguish under strong control.</content>
<content>Placing the lantern on the ground, he knelt on one knee before her.</content>
<content>"Farewell, my Love," he said.</content>
<content>"Our Lady comfort thee; and may Heaven forgive me, for that I have disturbed thy peace."</content>
<content>With which he lifted the hem of her robe, and pressed his lips upon it.</content>
<content>Thus he knelt, for a space, his dark head bent.</content>
<content>Slowly, slowly, the Prioress let drop her hands until, lightly as the fall of autumn leaves, sad autumn leaves they rested upon his head, in blessing and farewell.</content>
<content>But feeling his hair beneath her hands, she could not keep from softly smoothing it, nor from passing her fingers gently in and out of its crisp thickness.</content>
<content>Then her heart stood still, for of a sudden, in the silence, she heard a shuddering sob.</content>
<content>With a cry, she bent and gathered him to her, holding his head first against her knees, then stooping lower to clasp it to her breast; then as his strong arms were flung around her, she loosed his head, and, as he rose to his feet, slipped her arms about his neck, and surrendered to his embrace.</content>
<content>His lips sought hers, and at once she yielded them.</content>
<content>His strong hands held her, and she, feeling the force of their constraint, did but clasp him closer.</content>
<content>Long they stood thus.</content>
<content>In that embrace a life-time of pain passed from them, a life-time of bliss was born, and came with a rush to maturity, bringing with it a sense of utter completeness.</content>
