<content>A world of sweetest trust and certainty filled them; a joy so perfect, that the lonely vista of future years seemed, in that moment, to matter not at all.</content>
<content>All about them was darkness, silence as of the tomb; the heavy smell of earth; the dank chill of the grave.</content>
<content>Yet theirs was life more abundant; theirs, joy undreamed of; theirs, love beyond all imagining, while those moments lasted.</content>
<content>Then 

The hands about his neck loosened, unclasped, fell gently away.</content>
<content>He set free her lips, and they took their liberty.</content>
<content>He unlocked his arms, and stepping back she stood erect, like a fair white lily, needing no prop nor stay.</content>
<content>So they stood for a space, looking upon one another in silence.</content>
<content>This thing which had happened, was too wonderful for speech.</content>
<content>Then the Prioress turned the key in the lock.</content>
<content>The heavy door swung open.</content>
<content>A dim, grey light, like a pearly dawn at sea, came downwards from the crypt.</content>
<content>Without a word the Knight, bending his head, passed under the archway, mounted the steps, and was lost to view among the many pillars.</content>
<content>She closed the door, locked it, and withdrawing the key, stood alone where they had stood together.</content>
<content>Then, sinking to the ground, she laid her face in the dust, there where his feet had been.</content>
<content>It was farewell, here and now; farewell forever.</content>
<content>After a while the Prioress rose, took up the lantern, and started upon her lonely journey, back to the cloister door.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XV

"SHARPEN THE WITS OF MARY ANTONY"

When the Prioress started upon her pilgrimage to the Cathedral with the Knight, she locked the door of her chamber, knowing that thus her absence would remain undiscovered; for if any, knocking on the door, received no answer, or trying it, found it fast, they would hasten away without question; concluding that some special hour of devotion or time of study demanded that the Reverend Mother should be free from intrusion.</content>
<content>The atmosphere of the empty cell, charged during the past hour with such unaccustomed forces of conflict and of passion, settled into the quietude of an unbroken stillness.</content>
<content>The Madonna smiled serenely upon the Holy Babe.</content>
<content>The dead Christ, with bowed head, hung forlorn upon the wooden cross.</content>
<content>The ponderous volumes in black and silver bindings, lay undisturbed upon the table; and the Bishop's chair stood empty, with that obtrusive emptiness which, in an empty seat, seems to suggest an unseen presence filling it.</content>
<content>The silence was complete.</content>
<content>But presently a queer shuffling sound began in the inner cell, as of something stiff and torpid compelling itself to action.</content>
<content>Then a weird figure, the wizen face distorted by grief and terror, appeared in the doorway old Mary Antony, holding a meat chopper in her shaking hands, and staring, with chattering gums, into the empty cell.</content>
<content>That faithful soul, although dismissed, had resolved that the adored Reverend Mother should not go forth to meet dangers ghostly or corporeal alone and unprotected.</content>
<content>Hastening to the kitchens, she had given instructions that the evening meal was not to be served until the Reverend Mother herself should sound the bell.</content>
<content>Then, catching up a meat chopper, as being the most murderous-looking weapon at hand, and the most likely to strike terror into the ghostly heart of Sister Agatha, old Antony had hastened back to the passage.</content>
<content>Creeping up the stairs, hugging the wall, she had reached the top just in time to see, in the dim distance, the two tall white figures confronting one another.</content>
<content>Clinging to her chopper, motionless with horror, she had watched them, until they began, to come toward her, moving in the direction of the Reverend Mother's cell.</content>
<content>They were still thirty yards away, at the cloister end of the passage.</content>
<content>Old Antony was close to the open door.</content>
<content>Through it she had scurried, unheard, unseen, a terrified black shadow; yet brave withal; for with her went the meat chopper.</content>
<content>Also she might have turned and fled back down the stairs, rather than into the very place whither she knew the Reverend Mother was conducting this tall spectre of the long dead Sister Agatha, grown to most alarming proportions during her fifty years' entombment!</content>
<content>But being brave and faithful old Antony had sped into the inner cell, and crouched there in a corner; ready to call for help or strike with her chopper, should need arise.</content>
<content>Thus it came to pass that this old weaver of romances had perforce become a listener to a true romance so thrilling, so soul-stirring, that she had had to thrust the end of the wooden handle of the chopper into her mouth, lest she should applaud the noble Knight, cry counsel in his extremities, or invoke blessings on his enterprise.</content>
<content>At each mention of the Ladies Eleanor and Alfrida, she shook her fist, and made signs with her old fingers, as of throttling, in the air.</content>
<content>And when the clerkly messenger, arriving to speak with the Lady Alfrida who, Saint Luke be praised, was by that time dying found the Knight awaiting him with a noose flung over a strong bough, old Antony had laid down the chopper that she might the better hug herself with silent glee; and when the Knight rode away and left him hanging, she had whispered "Pieman!</content>
<content>Pieman!"</content>
<content>then clapped her hands over her mouth, rocking to and fro with merriment.</content>
<content>When the Knight made mention that they called him "Knight of the Bloody Vest," old Antony had started; then had shaken her finger toward the entrance, as she was used to shake it at the robin, and had opened her wallet to search for crumbs of cheese.</content>
<content>But soon again the story held her and, oblivious of the present, she had been back in the realms of romance.</content>
<content>Not until the Knight ceased speaking and the Reverend Mother's sad voice fell upon her ear, had old Antony realised the true bearing of the tale.</content>
<content>Thereafter her heart had been torn by grief and terror.</content>
<content>When they kneeled together, before the Madonna, with uplifted faces, Mary Antony had crawled forward and peeped.</content>
<content>She had seen them kneeling a noble pair had seen the Prioress catch at his hand and clasp it; then, crawling back had fallen prostrate, overwhelmed, a huddled heap upon the floor.</content>
<content>The ringing of the Refectory bell had roused her from her stupor in time to hear the impassioned appeal of the Knight, as he kneeled alone before the Virgin's shrine.</content>
<content>Then, the Knight and the Prioress both being gone, Mary Antony had arisen, lifted her chopper with hands that trembled, and now stood with distraught mien, surveying the empty cell.</content>
<content>At length it dawned upon her that she and her weapon were locked into the Reverend Mother's cell; she, who had been most explicitly bidden to go to the kitchens and to remain there.</content>
<content>It had been a sense of the enormity of her offence in having disobeyed the Reverend Mother's orders which, unconsciously, had caused her to stifle all ejaculations and move without noise, lest she should be discovered.</content>
<content>Yet now her first care was not for her own predicament, but for the two noble hearts, of whose tragic grief she had secretly been a witness.</content>
<content>Her eye fell on the Madonna, calmly smiling.</content>
<content>She tottered forward, kneeling where the Prioress had knelt.</content>
<content>"Holy Mother of God," she whispered, "teach him that she cannot do this thing!"</content>
<content>Then, moving along on her knees to where the Knight had kneeled: "Blessed Virgin!"</content>
<content>she cried, 'shew her that she cannot leave him desolate!"</content>
<content>Then shuffling back to the centre, and kneeling between the two places:
"Sweetest Lady," she said, "be pleased to sharpen the old wits of Mary
Antony."</content>
<content>Looking furtively at the Madonna, she saw that our Lady smiled.</content>
<content>The blessed Infant, also, looked merry.</content>
<content>Mary Antony chuckled, and took heart.</content>
<content>When the Reverend Mother smiled, she always knew herself forgiven.</content>
<content>Moreover, without delay, her request was granted; for scarcely had she arisen from her knees, when she remembered the place where the Reverend Mother kept the key of her cell; and she, having locked the door, on leaving, with her own master-key, the other was quickly in old Antony's hand, and she out once more in the passage, locking the door behind her; sure of being able to restore the key to its place, before it should be missed by the Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Antony slipped unseen past the Refectory and into the kitchens.</content>
<content>Once there, she fussed and scolded and made her presence felt, implying that she had been waiting, a good hour gone, for the thing for which she had but that moment asked.</content>
<content>The younger lay-sisters might make no retort; but Sister Mary Martha presently asked: "What have you been doing since Vespers, Sister Antony?"</content>
<content>By aid of the wits our Lady had sharpened, old Antony, at that moment, realised that sometimes, when you needs must deceive, there is nothing so deceptive as the actual truth.</content>
<content>"Listening to a wondrous romantic tale," she made answer, "told by the
Knight of the Bloody Vest."</content>
<content>"You verily are foolish about that robin, Sister Antony," remarked Mary Martha; "and you will take your death of cold, sitting out in the garden in the damp, after sunset."</content>
<content>"Well so long as I take only that which is mine own, others have no cause to grumble," snapped Mary Antony, and turned her mind upon the making of a savoury broth, favoured by the Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>And all the while the Devil was whispering in the old woman's ear: "She will not return.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Make thy broth, fool; but she will not be here to drink it.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>The World and the Flesh have called; the Reverend Mother will not come back.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Stir the broth well, but flavour it to thine own taste.</content>
<content>Thou wilt sup on it thyself this night.</content>
<content>When the World and the Flesh call loudly enough, the best of women go to the Devil."</content>
<content>"Liar!"</content>
<content>said Mary Antony, brandishing her wooden spoon.</content>
<content>"Get thee behind me nay, rather, get thee in front of me!</content>
<content>I have had thee skulking behind me long enough.</content>
<content>Also in front of me, just now, being into the fire, thou wilt feel at home, Master Devil!</content>
<content>Only, put not thy tail into the Reverend Mother's broth."</content>
<content>When the White Ladies passed up from the Refectory, Mary Antony chanced to be polishing the panelling around the picture of Saint Mary Magdalen, beside the door of the Reverend Mother's cell.</content>
<content>Presently Sister Mary Rebecca, arriving, lifted her hand to knock.</content>
<content>"Stay!"</content>
<content>whispered Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"The Reverend Mother may not be disturbed."</content>
<content>Sister Mary Rebecca veiled her scowl with a smile.</content>
<content>"And wherefore not, good Sister Antony?"</content>
<content>"'Wherefore not' is not my business," retorted old Antony, as rudely as she knew how.</content>
<content>"It may be for special study; it may be for an hour of extra devotion; it may be only the very natural desire for a little respite from the sight of two such ugly faces as yours and mine.</content>
<content>But, be the reason what it may, Reverend Mother has locked her door, and sees nobody this even."</content>
<content>After which old Antony proceeded to polish the outside of the Reverend Mother's door panels.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Rebecca lifted her knuckles to rap; but old Antony's not over clean clout was pushed each time between Sister Mary Rebecca's tap, and the woodwork.</content>
<content>Muttering concerning the report she would make to the Prioress in the morning, Sister Mary Rebecca went to her cell.</content>
<content>When all was quiet, when every door was closed, the old lay-sister crept into the cloisters and, crouching in an archway just beyond the flight of steps leading to the underground way, watched and waited.</content>
<content>Storm clouds were gathering again, black on a purple sky.</content>
<content>The after-glow in the west had faded.</content>
<content>It was dark in the cloisters.</content>
<content>Thunder growled in the distance; an owl hooted in the Pieman's tree.</content>
<content>Mary Antony's old bones ached sorely, and her heart failed her.</content>
<content>She had sat so long in cramped positions, and she had not tasted food since the mid-day meal.</content>
<content>The Devil drew near, as he is wont to do, when those who have fasted long, seek to keep vigil.</content>
<content>"The Reverend Mother will not return," he whispered.</content>
<content>"What wait you for?"</content>
<content>"Be off!"</content>
<content>said Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"I am too old to be keeping company, even with thee.</content>
<content>Also Sister Mary Rebecca awaits thee in her cell."</content>
<content>"The Reverend Mother ever walked with her head among the stars," sneered the Devil.</content>
<content>"Why do the highest fall the lowest, when temptation comes?"</content>
<content>"Ask that of Mother Sub-Prioress," said Mary Antony, "next time she bids thee to supper."</content>
<content>Then she clasped her old hands upon her breast; for, very softly, in the lock below, a key turned.</content>
<content>Steps, felt rather than heard, passed up into the cloister.</content>
<content>Then, in the dim light, the tall figure of the Prioress moved noiselessly over the flagstones, passed through the open door and up the deserted passage.</content>
<content>Peering eagerly forward, the old lay-sister saw the Prioress pause outside the door of her chamber, lift her master-key, unlock the door, and pass within.</content>
<content>As the faint sound of the closing of the door reached her straining ears, old Mary Antony began to sob, helplessly.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XVI

THE ECHO OF WILD VOICES

When the Prioress entered her cell, she stood for a moment bewildered by the rapid walk in the darkness.</content>
<content>She could hardly realise that the long strain was over; that she had safely regained her chamber.</content>
<content>All was as she had left it.</content>
<content>Apparently she had not been missed, and had returned unobserved.</content>
<content>Hugh was by now safely in the hostel at Worcester.</content>
<content>None need ever know that he had been here.</content>
<content>None need ever know Yet, alas, it was that knowledge which held the
Prioress rooted to the spot on which she stood, gazing round her cell.</content>
<content>Hugh had been here; and when he was here, her one desire had been to get him speedily away.</content>
<content>But now?</content>
<content>Dumb with the pain of a great yearning, she looked about her.</content>
<content>Yes; just there he had stood; here he had knelt, and there he had stood again.</content>
<content>This calm monastic air had vibrated to the fervour of his voice.</content>
<content>It had grown calm again.</content>
<content>Would her poor heart in time also grow calm?</content>
<content>Would her lips stop trembling, and cease to feel the fire of his?</content>
<content>Yet for one moment, only, her mind dwelt upon herself.</content>
<content>Then all thought of self was merged in the realisation of his loneliness, his suffering, his bitter disillusion.</content>
<content>To have found her dead, would have been hard; to have lost her living, was almost past bearing.</content>
<content>Would it cost him his faith in God, in truth, in purity, in honour?</content>
<content>The Prioress felt the insistent need of prayer.</content>
<content>But passing the gracious image of the Virgin and Child, she cast herself down at the foot of the crucifix.</content>
<content>She had seen a strong man in agony, nailed, by the cruel iron of circumstance, to the cross-beams of sacrifice and surrender.</content>
<content>To the suffering Saviour she turned, instinctively, for help and consolation.</content>
<content>Thus speedily had her prayer of the previous night been granted.</content>
<content>The pierced feet of our dear Lord, crucified, had become more to her than the baby feet of the Infant Jesus, on His Mother's knee.</content>
<content>Yet, even as she knelt supplicating, interceding, adoring there echoed in her memory the wicked shriek of Mary Seraphine: "A dead God cannot help me!</content>
<content>I want life, not death!"</content>
<content>followed almost instantly by Hugh's stern question: "Is this religion?"</content>
<content>Truly, of late, wild voices had taken liberty of speech in the cell of the Prioress, and had left their impious utterances echoing behind them.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XVII

THE DIMNESS OF MARY ANTONY

The Prioress had been back in her cell for nearly an hour, when a gentle tap came on the door.</content>
<content>"Enter," commanded the Prioress, and Mary Antony appeared, bearing broth and bread, fruit and a cup of wine.</content>
<content>The Prioress sat at her table, parchment and an open missal before her.</content>
<content>Her face was very white; also there were dark shadows beneath her eyes.</content>
<content>She did not smile at sight of old Antony, thus laden.</content>
<content>"How now, Antony?"</content>
<content>she said, almost sternly.</content>
<content>"I did not bid thee to bring me food."</content>
<content>"Reverend Mother," said the old lay-sister, in a voice which strove to be steady, yet quavered; "for long hours you have studied, not heeding that the evening meal was over.</content>
<content>Chide not old Antony for bringing you some of that broth, which you like the best.</content>
<content>You will not sleep unless you eat."</content>
<content>The Prioress looked at her uncomprehendingly; as if, for the moment, words conveyed no meaning to her mind.</content>
<content>Then she saw those old hands trembling, and a sudden flood of colour flushed the pallor of her face.</content>
<content>This sweet stirring of fresh life within her own heart gave her to see, in the old woman's untiring devotion, a human element hitherto unperceived.</content>
<content>It brought a rush of comfort, in her sadness.</content>
<content>She closed the volume, and pushed aside the parchment.</content>
<content>"How kind of thee, dear Antony, to take so much thought for me.</content>
<content>Place the bowls on the table.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Now draw up that stool, and stay near me while I sup.</content>
<content>I am weary this night, and shall like thy company."</content>
<content>Had the golden gates of heaven opened before her, and Saint Peter himself invited her to enter, Sister Mary Antony would not have been more astonished and certainly could hardly have been more gratified.</content>
<content>It was a thing undreamed of, that she should be bidden to sit with the Reverend Mother in her cell.</content>
<content>Drawing the carven stool two feet from the wall, Mary Antony took her seat upon it.</content>
<content>"Nearer, Antony, nearer," said the Prioress.</content>
<content>"Place the stool here, close beside the corner of my table.</content>
<content>I have much to say to thee, and would wish to speak low."</content>
<content>Truly Sister Antony found herself in the seventh heaven!</content>
<content>Yet, quietly observing, the Prioress could not fail to note the drawn weariness on the old face, the yellow pallor of the wizen skin, which usually wore the bright tint of a russet apple.</content>
<content>The Prioress took a portion of the broth; then pushed the bowl from her, and turned to the fruit.</content>
<content>"There, Antony," she said.</content>
<content>"The broth is excellent; but I have enough.</content>
<content>Finish it thyself.</content>
<content>It will pleasure me to see thee enjoy it."</content>
<content>Faint and thankful, old Antony seized the bowl.</content>
<content>And as she drank the broth, her shrewd eyes twinkled.</content>
<content>For had not the Devil said she would sup on it herself; knowing that much, yet not knowing that she would receive it from the hand of the Reverend Mother?</content>
<content>It has been ever so, from Eden onwards, when the Devil tries his hand at prophecy.</content>
<content>For a while the Prioress talked lightly, of flowers and birds; of the garden and the orchard; of the gift of three fine salmon, sent to them by the good monks of the Priory at Worcester.</content>
<content>But, presently, when the broth was finished and a faint colour tinted the old cheeks, she passed on to the storm and the sunset, the rolling thunder and the torrents of rain.</content>
<content>Then of a sudden she said:

"By the way, Antony, hast thou made mention, to any, of thy fearsome tale of the walking through the cloisters, in line with the White Ladies, of the Spectre of the saintly Sister Agatha?"</content>
<content>"Nay, Reverend Mother," said Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"Did not you forbid me to speak of it?"</content>
<content>"True," said the Prioress.</content>
<content>"Well, Antony, I went in the storm, to look for her; but I found not Sister Agatha."</content>
<content>"That I already knew," said Mary Antony, nodding her head sagaciously.</content>
<content>The Prioress cast upon her a quick, anxious look.</content>
<content>"What mean you, Antony?"</content>
<content>Then old Mary Antony fell upon her knees, and kissed the hem of the Prioress's robe.</content>
<content>"Oh, Reverend Mother," she stammered, "I have a confession to make!"</content>
<content>"Make it," said the Prioress, with white lips.</content>
<content>"Reverend Mother, when you sent me from you, after making my report, I went first, as commanded, to the kitchens.</content>
<content>But afterward, in my cell, I found these."</content>
<content>Mary Antony opened her wallet and drew out the linen bag in which she kept her peas.</content>
<content>Shaking its contents into the palm of her hand, she held out six peas to view.</content>
<content>"Reverend Mother," she said, "there were twenty-five in the bag.</content>
<content>I thought I had counted twenty out into my hand; so when all the peas had dropped and yet another holy Lady passed, I thought that made twenty-one.</content>
<content>But when I found six peas in my bag, I became aware of my folly.</content>
<content>I had but counted nineteen, and had no pea to let fall for the twentieth holy Lady.</content>
<content>Yet I ran in haste with my false report, when, had I but thought to look in my wallet, all would have been made clear.</content>
<content>Will the Reverend Mother forgive old Mary Antony?"</content>
<content>She shot a quick glance at the Prioress; and, at sight of the immense relief on that loved face, felt ready for any punishment with which it might please Heaven to visit her deceit.</content>
<content>"Dear Antony," began the Reverend Mother, smiling.</content>
<content>"Dear Antony " she said, and laughed aloud.</content>
<content>Then she placed her hand beneath the old woman's arm, and gently raised her.</content>
<content>"Mistakes arise so easily," she said.</content>
<content>"With the best of intentions, we all sometimes make mistakes.</content>
<content>There is nothing to forgive, my Antony."</content>
<content>"I am old, and dim, and stupid," said the lay-sister, humbly; "but I have begged of our sweet Lady to sharpen the old wits of Mary Antony."</content>
<content>After which statement, made in a voice of humble penitence, Mary Antony, unseen by the thankful Prioress, did give a knowing wink with the eye next to the Madonna.</content>
<content>Our blessed Lady smiled.</content>
<content>The sweet Babe looked merry.</content>
<content>The Prioress rose, a great light of relief illumining her weary face.</content>
<content>"Let us to bed, dear Antony; then, with the dawn of a new day we shall all arise with hearts refreshed and wits more keen.</content>
<content>So now God rest thee."</content>
<content>Left alone, the Prioress knelt long in prayer before the shrine of the
Madonna.</content>
<content>Once, she reached out her right hand to the empty space where
Hugh had knelt, striving to feel remembrance of his strong clasp.</content>
<content>At length she sought her couch.</content>
<content>But sleep refused to come, and presently she crept back in the white moonlight, and kneeling pressed her lips to the stone on which Hugh had kneeled; then fled, in shame that our Lady should see such weakness; and dared not glance toward the shadowy form of the dead Christ, crucified.</content>
<content>For with the coming of Love to seek her, Life had come; and where Life enters, Death is put to flight; even as before the triumphant march of the rising sun, darkness and shadows flee away.</content>
<content>Yet, even then, our Lady gently smiled, and the Babe on her knees looked merry.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XVIII

IN THE CATHEDRAL CRYPT

On the day following, in the afternoon, shortly before the hour of Vespers, a stretcher was carried through the streets of Worcester, by four men-at-arms wearing the livery of Sir Hugh d'Argent.</content>
<content>Beside it walked the Knight, with bent head, his eyes upon the ground.</content>
<content>The body of the man upon the stretcher was covered by a fine linen sheet, over which lay a blue cloak, richly embroidered with silver.</content>
<content>His head was swathed in a bandage of many folds, partially concealing the face.</content>
<content>The little procession passed through the Precincts; then entered the
Cathedral by the great door leading into the nave.</content>
<content>Here a monk stood, taking careful note of all who passed in or out of the building.</content>
<content>As the stretcher approached, he stepped forward with hand upraised.</content>
<content>There was a pause in the measured tramp of the bearers' feet.</content>
<content>The Knight lifted his eyes, and seeing the monk barring the way, he drew forth a parchment and tendered it.</content>
<content>"I have the leave of the Lord Bishop, good father," he said, "to carry this man upon the stretcher daily into the crypt, and there to let him lie before the shrine of Saint Oswald, during the hour of Vespers; from which daily pilgrimage and prayer, we hope a great recovery and restoration."</content>
<content>At sight of the Lord Bishop's signature and seal, the monk made deep obeisance, and hastened to call the Sacristan, bidding him attend the Knight on his passage to the crypt and give him every facility in placing the sick man there where he might most conveniently lie before the holy altar of the blessed Saint Oswald.</content>
<content>So presently, the stretcher being safely deposited, the men-at-arms stood each against a pillar, and the Knight folded back the coverings, in order that the man who lay beneath, might have sight of the altar and the shrine.</content>
<content>As the Knight stood gazing through the vista of many columns, he found the old Sacristan standing at his elbow.</content>
<content>"Most worshipful Knight," said the old man, with deference, "our Lord Bishop's mandate supersedes all rules.</content>
<content>Were it not so, it would be my duty to clear the crypt before Vespers.</content>
<content>See you that stairway yonder, beneath the arch?</content>
<content>Not many minutes hence, up those steps will pass the holy nuns from the Convent of the White Ladies at Whytstone noble ladies all, and of great repute for saintliness.</content>
<content>Daily they come to Vespers by a secret way; entering the crypt, they pass across to a winding stair in the wall, and so arrive at a gallery above the choir, from which they can, unseen, hear the chanting of the monks.</content>
<content>I must to my duties above.</content>
<content>Will you undertake, Sir Knight, that your men go not nigh where the White Ladies pass, nor in any way molest them?"</content>
<content>"None shall stir hand or foot, as they pass, nor in any way molest them," said the Knight.</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent was kneeling before the altar, his folded hands resting upon the cross on the hilt of his sword, when the faint sound of a key turning in a distant lock, caught his ear.</content>
<content>Then up the steps and across the crypt passed, in silent procession, the White Ladies of Worcester.</content>
<content>There was something ghostly and awe-inspiring about those veiled figures, moving noiselessly among the pillars in the dimly-lighted crypt; then vanishing, one by one, up the winding stairway in the wall.</content>
<content>The Knight did not stir.</content>
<content>He stayed upon his knees, his hands clasped upon his sword-hilt; but he followed each silent figure with his eyes.</content>
<content>The last had barely disappeared from view when, from above, came the solemn chanting of monks and choristers.</content>
<content>This harmony, descending from above, seemed to uplift the soul all the more readily, because the sacred words and noble sounds reached the listener, unhampered by association with the personalities, either youthful or ponderous, of the singers.</content>
<content>All that was of the earth remained unseen; while that which was so near akin to heaven, entered the listening ear.</content>
<content>Kneeling in lowly reverence with bowed head, the Knight found himself wondering whether the ascending sounds reached that distant gallery in the clerestory where the White Ladies knelt, as greatly softened, sweetened, and enriched, as they now came stealing down into the crypt.</content>
<content>Were the hearts of those veiled worshippers also lifted heavenward; or being already above the music did the ascending voices rather tend to draw them down to earth?</content>
<content>Upon which the Knight fell to meditating as to whether that which is higher always uplifts; whereas that which is lower tends to debase.</content>
<content>Certainly the upward look betokens hope and joy; while the downward casting of the eye, is sign of sorrow and despondency.</content>
<content>"Levavi oculos meos in montes" chanted the monks, in the choir above.</content>
<content>He certainly looked high when he lifted the eyes of his insistent desire to the Prioress of the White Ladies.</content>
<content>So high did he lift them, and so unattainable was she, that most men would say he might as well ask the silvery moon, sailing across the firmament, to come down and be his bride!</content>
<content>He had held her high, in her maiden loveliness and purity.</content>
<content>But now that he had found her, a noble woman, matured, ripened by sorrow rather than hardened, yet firm in her determination to die to the world, to deny self, crucify the flesh, and resist the Devil he felt indeed that she walked among the stars.</content>
<content>Yet he could not bring himself to regard her as unattainable.</content>
<content>It had ever been his firm belief that a man could win any woman upon whom he wholly set his heart always supposing that no other man had already won her.</content>
<content>And this woman had been his own betrothed, when treachery intervened and sundered them.</content>
<content>Yet that did not now count for much.</content>
<content>He had left a girl; he had come back to find a woman.</content>
<content>That woman had infinitely more to give; but it would be infinitely more difficult to persuade her to give it.</content>
<content>At the close of their interview in her cell, the day before, all hope had left him.</content>
<content>But later, as they paced together in the darkness, hope had revived.</content>
<content>The strange isolation in which they then found themselves between locked doors a mile apart, earth above, earth beneath, earth all around them, they two alone, entombed yet vividly conscious of glowing life had brought her nearer to him; and when at last the moment of parting arrived and again he faced it as final, there had come all unheralded the sudden wonder of her surrender.</content>
<content>True, she had afterwards withdrawn herself; true, she had sent him from her; true, he had gone, without a word.</content>
<content>But that was because no promise could have been so binding, as that silent embrace.</content>
<content>He had gone from her on the impulse of the sweetness of obeying instantly her slightest wish; buoyed up by the certainty that no Convent walls could long divide lips which had met and clung with such a passion of mutual need.</content>
<content>That evening when, after much adventure, he at length gained the streets of the city, he had trodden them with the mien of a victor.</content>
<content>That night he had slept as he had not slept since the hour when his whole life had been embittered by a lying letter and a traitorous tongue.</content>
<content>But morning, alas, had brought its doubts; noon, its dark uncertainties; and as the hour of Vespers drew near, he had realised, with the helpless misery of despair, that it was madness to expect the Prioress of the White Ladies to break her vows, leave her Nunnery, and fly with him to Warwick.</content>
<content>Yet he carried out his plan, and kept to his undertaking, though here, in the calm atmosphere of the crypt, holy chanting descending from above, the remembrance still with him of the aloofness of those stately white figures gliding between the pillars in the distance, he faced the madness of his hopes, and the mournful prospect of a life of loneliness.</content>
<content>Presently he arose, crossed the crypt, and took up his position behind a pillar to the right of the exit from the winding stair.</content>
<content>The chanting ceased.</content>
<content>Vespers were over.</content>
<content>He heard the sound of soft footsteps drawing nearer.</content>
<content>The White Ladies were coming.</content>
<content>They came.</content>
<content>The Knight was not kept long in suspense.</content>
<content>The Prioress walked first.</content>
<content>Her face was hidden, but her height and carriage revealed her to her lover.</content>
<content>She looked neither to right nor left but, turning away from the pillar behind which the Knight stood concealed, crossed to the steps leading down to the subterranean way, and so passed swiftly out of sight.</content>
<content>The Knight stood motionless until all had appeared, and had vanished once more from view.</content>
<content>One, tall but ungainly, crooked of body, and doubtless short of vision, missed her way among the columns and passed perilously near to the Knight.</content>
<content>With his long arm, he could have clasped her.</content>
<content>How old Antony would have chuckled, could she but have known!</content>
<content>"Sister Mary Rebecca embraced by the Knight of the Bloody Vest?</content>
<content>Nay then; the Saints forbid!"</content>
<content>The stretcher, borne by four men-at-arms, passed out from the Cathedral.</content>
<content>The Knight walked beside it, with bent head, and eyes upon the ground.</content>
<content>As it passed through the Precincts, the Lord Bishop himself rode out on his white palfrey, on his way to the Nunnery at Whytstone.</content>
<content>The Knight, being downhearted, did not lift his eyes.</content>
<content>The Bishop looked, kindly, upon the stretcher and upon the Knight's dark face.</content>
<content>The Bishop had known Hugh d'Argent as a boy.</content>
<content>He grieved to see him thus in sorrow.</content>
<content>Yet the Bishop smiled as he rode on.</content>
<content>Perhaps he did not put much faith in the efficacy of relics, for so heavily bandaged a broken head as that upon the stretcher.</content>
<content>For there was a whimsical tenderness about the Bishop's smile.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XIX

THE BISHOP PUTS ON HIS BIRETTA

Symon, Lord Bishop of Worcester, having received a letter from the Prioress of the White Ladies, praying him for an interview at his leisure, sent back at once a most courtly and gracious answer, that he would that same day give himself the pleasure of visiting the Reverend Mother, at the Nunnery, an hour after Vespers.</content>
<content>The great gates were thrown open, and the Bishop rode his palfrey into the courtyard.</content>
<content>The Prioress herself met him at the door and, kneeling, kissed his ring; then led him through the lower hall, where the nuns knelt to receive his blessing, and up the wide staircase, to the privacy of her own cell.</content>
<content>There she presently unfolded to him the history of her difficulties with that wayward little nun, Sister Mary Seraphine.</content>
<content>"But the point which I chiefly desire to lay before you, Reverend Father," concluded the Prioress, "is this: If the neighing of a palfrey calls more loudly to her than the voice of God; if her mind is still set upon the things of the world; if she professed without a true vocation, merely because she wished to be the central figure of a great ceremony, yet was all the while expecting a man to intervene and carry her off; if all this bespeaks her true state of heart, then to my mind there comes the question: Is she doing good, either to herself or to others, by belonging to our Order?</content>
<content>Would she not be better away?</content>
<content>"My lord, I fear I greatly shock you by naming such a possibility.</content>
<content>But truly I am pursued by the remembrance of that young thing, beating the floor with her hands, and singing a mournful dirge about the crimson trappings of her palfrey.</content>
<content>And, alas!</content>
<content>when I reasoned with her and exhorted, she broke out, as I have told you, Reverend Father, into grievous blasphemy for which she was severely dealt with by Mother Sub-Prioress, and has since been outwardly amenable to rules and discipline.</content>
<content>"But, though she may outwardly conform, how about her inward state?</content>
<content>Well I know that our vows are lifelong vows; all who belong to our Order are wedded to Heaven; we are thankful to know that the calm of the Cloister shall be exchanged only for the greater peace of Paradise.</content>
<content>But, supposing a young heart has mistaken its vocation; supposing the voice of an earthly lover calls when it is too late; would it seem right or possible to you, Reverend Father, to grant any sort of absolution from the vows; tacitly to allow the opening of the cage door, that the little foolish bird might, if it wished, escape into the liberty for which it chafes and sighs?"</content>
<content>The Bishop sat in the Spanish chair, drawn up near the oriel window, so that he could either gaze at the glories of the distant sunset, or, by slightly turning his head, look on the beautiful but grave face of the Prioress, seated before him.</content>
<content>While she was speaking he watched her keenly, with those bright searching eyes, so much more youthful than aught else about him.</content>
<content>But now that he must make reply, he looked away to the sunset.</content>
<content>The light shone on the plain gold cross at his breast, and on the violet silk of his cassock.</content>
<content>His face, against the background of the black Spanish wood, looked strangely white and thin; strong in contour, with a virile strength; in expression, sensitive as a woman's.</content>
<content>He had removed his biretta, and placed it upon the table.</content>
<content>His silvery hair rolled back from his forehead in silky waves.</content>
<content>His was the look of the saint and the scholar, almost of the mystic save for the tender humour in those keen blue eyes, gleaming like beacon lights from beneath the level eyebrows; eyes which had won the confidence of many a man who else had not dared unfold his very human story, to one of such saintly aspect as Symon, Bishop of Worcester.</content>
<content>They were turned toward the sunset, as he made answer to the Prioress.</content>
<content>"The little foolish bird," said the Bishop and he spoke in that gently musing tone, which conveys to the mind of the hearer a sense of infinite leisure in which to weigh and consider the subject in hand "The little foolish bird might soon wish herself back in the safety of the cage.</content>
<content>On such as she, the cruel hawks of life do love to prey.</content>
<content>Absorbed in the contemplation of her own charms, she sees not, until too late, the dangers which surround her.</content>
<content>Such little foolish birds, my daughter, are best in the safe shelter of the cloister.</content>
<content>Moreover, of what value are they in the world?</content>
<content>None.</content>
<content>If Popinjays wed them, they do but hatch out broods of foolish little Popinjays.</content>
<content>If true men, caught by mere surface beauty, wed them, it can mean naught save heartbreak and sorrow, and deterioration of the race.</content>
<content>Women of finer mould" for an instant the Bishop's eyes strayed from the sunset "are needed, to be the mothers of the men who, in the years to come, are to make England great.</content>
<content>Nay, rather than let one escape, I would shut up all the little foolish birds in a Nunnery, with our excellent Sub-Prioress to administer necessary discipline."</content>
<content>With his elbows resting upon the arms of the chair, the Bishop put his fingers together, so that the tips met most precisely; then bent his lips to them, and looked at the Prioress.</content>
<content>She, troubled and sick at heart, lifting deep pools of silent misery, met the merry twinkle in the Bishop's eyes, and sat astonished.</content>
<content>What was it like?</content>
<content>Why it was like the song of a robin, perched on a frosty bough, on Christmas morning!</content>
<content>It was so young and gay; so jocund, and so hopeful.</content>
<content>Meeting it, the Prioress realised fully, what she had many times half-divined, that the revered and reverend Prelate sitting opposite, for all his robes and dignity, his panoply of Church and State, had the heart of a merry schoolboy out on a holiday.</content>
<content>For the moment she felt much older than the Bishop, infinitely sadder; more travel-worn and worldly-wise.</content>
<content>Then she looked at the silver hair; the firm mouth, with a shrewd curve at either corner; the thoughtful brow.</content>
<content>And then she looked at the Bishop's ring.</content>
<content>The Bishop wore a remarkable ring; not a signet, but a large gem of great value, beautifully cut in many facets, and clear set in massive gold.</content>
<content>This precious stone, said to be a chrysoprasus, had been given to the Bishop by a Russian prince, in acknowledgment of a great service rendered him when he came on pilgrimage to Rome.</content>
<content>The rarity of these gems arose partly from the fact that the sovereigns of Russia had decreed that they should be held exclusively for royal ornament, forbidding their use or purchase by people of lesser degree.</content>
<content>But its beauty and its rarity were not the only qualities of the precious stone in the Bishop's ring.</content>
<content>The strangest thing about it was that its colour varied, according to the Bishop's mood and surroundings.</content>
<content>When the Prioress looked up and met the gay twinkle, the stone in the Bishop's ring was a heavenly blue, the colour of forget-me-nots beside a meadow brook, or the clear azure of the sky above a rosy sunset.</content>
<content>But presently he passed his hand over his eyes, as if to shut out some bright vision, and to turn his mind to more sober thought; and, at that moment, the stone in his ring gleamed a pale opal, threaded with flashes of green.</content>
<content>The Prioress returned to the subject, with studied seriousness.</content>
<content>"I did not suppose, Reverend Father, that it was to be of any advantage to the world, that Sister Seraphine should return to it.</content>
<content>The advantage was to be to her, and also to this whole Community, well rid of the presence of one who finds our sacred exercises irksome; our beautiful Nunnery, a prison; her cell, a living tomb.</content>
<content>She cries out for life.</content>
<content>'I want to live,' she said, 'I am young, I am gay, I am beautiful!</content>
<content>I want life.'"</content>
<content>"To such as Sister Seraphine," remarked the Bishop, gravely, "life is but a mirror which reflects themselves.</content>
<content>Other forms and faces may flit by, in the background; dimly seen, scarcely noticed.</content>
<content>There is but one face and form occupying the entire foreground.</content>
<content>Life is, to such, the mirror which ministers to vanity.</content>
<content>Should a husband appear in the picture, he is soon relegated to the background, receiving only occasional glances over the shoulder.</content>
<content>If children dance into the field of vision, they are petulantly driven elsewhere.</content>
<content>Tell me?</content>
<content>Did Sister Seraphine's desire for life include any expression of the desire to give life?"</content>
<content>Involuntarily the Prioress glanced at the sweet Babe upon the Virgin's knees.</content>
<content>"No," she said, very low.</content>
<content>"I thought not," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Self-centred, shallow natures are not capable of the sublime passion for motherhood; partly, no doubt, because they themselves possess no life worth passing on."</content>
<content>The Prioress rose quickly and, moving to the window, flung open a second casement.</content>
<content>It was imperative, at that moment, to hide her face; for the uncontrollable flood of emotion at her heart, could scarce fail to send a tell-tale wave to disturb the calm of her countenance.</content>
<content>Whereupon the Bishop turned, to see at what the Prioress had glanced before answering his question.</content>
<content>"No," he mused, as she resumed her seat, his eyes upon the tree-tops beyond the casement, "the Seraphines have not the instinct of motherhood.</content>
<content>And the future greatness of our race depends upon those noble women who are able to pass on to their sons and daughters a life which is true, and brave, and worthy; a life whose foundation is self-sacrifice, whose cornerstone is loyalty, and from whose summit waves the banner of unsullied love of hearth and home.</content>
<content>"A woman with the true instinct of motherhood cannot see a little child without yearning to clasp it to her bosom.</content>
<content>When she finds her mate, she thinks more of being the mother of his children than the object of his devotion, because the Self in her is subservient to the maternal instinct for self-sacrifice.</content>
<content>These women are pure as snow, and they hold their men to the highest and the best.</content>
<content>Such women are needed in the world.</content>
<content>Our Lady knoweth, I speak not lightly, unadvisedly nor wantonly; but were Seraphine such an one as this, I should say; 'Leave the door on the latch.</content>
<content>Without permission, yet without reproach let her go.'"</content>
<content>"Were Seraphine such an one as that, my lord," said the Prioress, firmly, "then would there be no question of her going.</content>
<content>If the cornerstone of character be loyalty, the very essential of loyalty is the keeping of vows."</content>
<content>"Quite so," murmured the Bishop; "undoubtedly, my daughter.</content>
<content>Unless, by some strange fatality, those vows were made under a total misapprehension.</content>
<content>You tell me Sister Seraphine expected a man to intervene?"</content>
<content>The Bishop sat up, of a sudden keenly alert.</content>
<content>His eyes, no longer humorous and tender, became searching and bright young still, but with the fire of youth, rather than its merriment.</content>
<content>As he leaned forward in his chair, his hands gripped his knees.</content>
<content>Looking at his ring the Prioress saw the stone the colour of red wine.</content>
<content>"What if, after all, I can help you in this," he said.</content>
<content>"What if I can throw light upon the whole situation, and find a cause for the little foolish bird's restless condition, proving to you that she may have heard something more than the mere neighing of a palfrey!</content>
<content>Listen!</content>
<content>"A Knight arrived in this city, rather more than a month ago; a very noble Knight, splendid to look upon; one of our bravest Crusaders.</content>
<content>He arrived here in sore anguish of heart.</content>
<content>His betrothed had been taken from him during his absence from England, waging war against the Turks in Palestine taken from him by a most dastardly and heartless plot.</content>
<content>He made many inquiries concerning this Nunnery and Order, rode north again on urgent business, but returned, with a large retinue, five days since."</content>
<content>The Prioress did not stir.</content>
<content>She maintained her quiet posture as an attentive listener.</content>
<content>But her face grew as white as her wimple, and she folded her hands to steady their trembling.</content>
<content>But the Bishop, now eagerly launched, had no interest in pallor, or possible palsy.</content>
<content>His vigorous words cut the calm atmosphere.</content>
<content>The gem on his finger sparkled like red wine in a goblet.</content>
<content>"I knew him of old," he said; "knew him as a high-spirited lad, yet loving, and much beloved.</content>
<content>He came to me, in his grief, distraught with anguish of heart, and told me this tale of treachery and wrong.</content>
<content>Never did I hear of such a network of evil device, such a tragedy of loving hearts sundered.</content>
<content>And when at last he returned to this land, he found that the girl whom he had thought false, thinking him so, had entered a Nunnery.</content>
<content>Also he seemed convinced that she was to be found among our White Ladies of Worcester.</content>
<content>Now tell me, dear Prioress, think you she could be Seraphine?"</content>
<content>The Prioress smiled; and truly it was a very creditable smile for a face which might have been carved in marble.</content>
<content>"From my knowledge of Sister Mary Seraphine," she said, "it seems unlikely that for loss of her, so noble a Knight as you describe would be distraught with anguish of heart."</content>
<content>"Nay, there I do not agree," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"It is ever opposites which attract.</content>
<content>The tall wed the short; the stout, the lean; the dark, the fair; the grave, the gay.</content>
<content>Wherefore my stern Crusader may be breaking his heart for your foolish little bird."</content>
<content>"I do not think so," said the Prioress, shortly; then hastened to add:
"Not that I would presume to differ from you, Reverend Father.</content>
<content>Doubtless you are better versed in such matters than I.</content>
<content>But if it be
as you suppose what measures do you suggest?</content>
<content>How am I to deal with
Sister Mary Seraphine?"</content>
<content>The Bishop leaned forward and whispered, though not another soul was within hearing; but at this juncture in the conversation, a whisper was both dramatic and effective.</content>
<content>Also, when he leaned forward, he could almost hear the angry beating of the heart of the Prioress.</content>
<content>The Bishop held the Prioress in high regard, and loved not to distress her.</content>
<content>But he did not think it right that a woman should have such complete mastery over herself, and therefore over others.</content>
<content>A fine quality in a man, may be a blemish in a woman.</content>
<content>For which reason the Bishop leaned forward and whispered.</content>
<content>"Let her fly, my daughter; let her fly.</content>
<content>If his arms await her, she will not have far to go, nor many dangers to face.</content>
<content>Her lover will know how to guard his own."</content>
<content>"My lord," said the Prioress, now flushed with anger, "you amaze me!</content>
<content>Am I to understand that you would have me open the Convent door, so that a renegade nun may escape to her lover?</content>
<content>Or perhaps, my lord, it would better meet your ideas if I bid the porteress stand wide the great gates, so that this high-spirited Knight may ride in and carry off the nun he desires, in sight of all!</content>
<content>My Lord Bishop!</content>
<content>You rule in Worcester and in the cities of the diocese.</content>
<content>But I rule in this Nunnery; and while I rule here, such a thing as this shall never be."</content>
<content>The Prioress flashed and quivered; rose to her feet and towered; flung her arms wide, and paced the floor.</content>
<content>"The Knight has bewitched you, my lord," she said.</content>
<content>"You forget the rules of our holy Church.</content>
<content>You fail in your trust toward the women who look to you as their spiritual Father and guide."</content>
<content>The Prioress walked up and down the cell, and each time she passed her chair she wheeled, and gripping the back with her strong fingers, shook it.</content>
<content>Not being able to shake the Bishop, she needs must shake something.</content>
<content>"You amaze me!"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"Truly, my lord, you amaze me!"</content>
<content>The Bishop put on his biretta.</content>
<content>Only once before, in his eventful life, had he made a woman as angry as this.</content>
<content>Very young he was, then; and the angry woman had seized him by his hair.</content>
<content>The Bishop did not really think the Prioress would do this; but it amused him to fancy he was afraid, and to put on his biretta.</content>
<content>Then, as he leaned back in his chair, and his finger tips met, the stone in his ring was blue again, and his eyes were more than ever the eyes of a merry schoolboy out on a holiday.</content>
<content>Yet, presently, he sought to calm the tempest he had raised.</content>
<content>"My daughter," he said, "I did but agree to that which you yourself suggested.</content>
<content>Did you not ask whether it would seem to me right or possible to grant absolution from her vows, tacitly to allow the opening of the cage door, that the little foolish bird might, if she wished it, escape?</content>
<content>Why this exceeding indignation, when I do but yield to your arguments and fall in with your suggestions?"</content>
<content>"I did not suggest that a lover's arms were awaiting one of my nuns," said the angry Prioress.</content>
<content>"You did not mention arms," replied the Bishop, gently; "but you most explicitly mentioned a voice.</content>
<content>'Supposing the voice of an earthly lover calls,' you said.</content>
<content>And having admitted that I am better versed in such matters than you you must forgive me, dear Prioress, if I amaze you further by acquainting you with the undoubted fact, recognised, in the outer world, as beyond dispute, that when a lover's voice calls, a lover's arms are likely to be waiting.</content>
<content>Earthly lovers, my daughter, by no means resemble those charming cherubs which you may have observed on the carved woodwork in our Cathedral.</content>
<content>Otherwise you might have just a voice, flanked by seraphic wings.</content>
<content>Some such fanciful creation must have been in your mind for Sister Mary Seraphine; for, until I made mention of the noble Knight who had arrived in Worcester distraught with anguish of heart by reason of his loss, you had decided leanings toward tacitly allowing flight.</content>
<content>Therefore it was not the fact of the broken vows, but the idea of Seraphine wedded to the brave Crusader, which so greatly roused your ire."</content>
<content>The Prioress stood silent.</content>
<content>Her hot anger cooled, enveloped in the chill mantle of self-revelation and self-scorn.</content>
<content>It seemed to her that the gentle words of the Bishop indeed expressed the truth far more correctly than he knew.</content>
<content>The thought of Hugh, consoling himself with some foolish, vain, unworthy, little Seraphine, had stung with intolerable pain.</content>
<content>Yet, how should she, the cause of his despair, begrudge him any comfort he might find in the love of another?</content>
<content>Then, suddenly, the Prioress knelt at the feet of the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Forgive me, most Reverend Father," she said.</content>
<content>"I did wrong to be angry."</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester extended his hand, and the Prioress kissed the ring.</content>
<content>As she withdrew her lips from the precious stone, she saw it blood-red and sparkling, as the juice of purple grapes in a goblet.</content>
<content>The Bishop laid his biretta once more upon the table, and smiled very tenderly on the Prioress, as he motioned her to rise from her knees and to resume her seat.</content>
<content>"You did right to be angry, my daughter," he said.</content>
<content>"You were not angry with me, nor with the brave Crusader, nor with the foolish Seraphine.</content>
<content>Your anger, all unconsciously, was aroused by a system, a method of life which is contrary to Nature, and therefore surely at variance with the will of God.</content>
<content>I have long had my doubts concerning these vows of perpetual celibacy for women.</content>
<content>For men, it is different.</content>
<content>The creative powers in a man, if denied their natural functions, stir him to great enterprise, move him to beget fine phantasies, creations of his brain, children of his intellect.</content>
<content>If he stamp not his image on brave sons and fair daughters, he leaves his mark on life in many other ways, both brave and fair.</content>
<content>But it is not so with woman; in the very nature of things it cannot be.</content>
<content>Methinks these Nunneries would serve a better purpose were they schools from which to send women forth into the world to be good wives and mothers, rather than store-houses filled with sad samples of Nature's great purposes deliberately unfulfilled."</content>
<content>The merry schoolboy look had vanished.</content>
<content>The Bishop's eyes were stern and searching; yet he looked not on the Prioress as he spoke.</content>
<content>Amazement was writ larger than ever, on her face; but she held herself well under control.</content>
<content>"Such views, my lord, if freely expressed and adopted, would change the entire monastic system."</content>
<content>"I know it," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"And I would not express them, saving to you and to one other, to whom I also talk freely.</content>
<content>But the older I grow, the more clearly do I see that systems are man-made, and therefore often mistaken, injurious, pernicious.</content>
<content>But Nature is Divine.</content>
<content>Those who live in close touch with Nature, who rule their lives by Nature's rules, do not stray far from the Divine plan of the Creator.</content>
<content>But when man takes upon himself to say 'Thou shalt,' or 'Thou shalt not,' quickly confusion enters.</content>
<content>A false premise becomes the starting-point; and the goal, if it stop short of perdition, is, at best, folly and failure."</content>
<content>The Bishop paused.</content>
<content>The eyes of the woman before him were dark with sorrow, regret, and the dawning of a great fear.</content>
<content>Presently she spoke.</content>
<content>"To say these things here, my lord, is to say them too late."</content>
<content>"It is never too late," replied Symon of Worcester.</content>
<content>"'Too late' tolls the knell of the coward heart.</content>
<content>If we find out a mistake while we yet walk the earth where we made it, it is not too late to amend it."</content>
<content>"Think you so, Reverend Father?</content>
<content>Then what do you counsel me to do with Seraphine?"</content>
<content>"Speak to her gently, and with great care and prudence.</content>
<content>Say to her much of that which you have said to me, and a little of that which I have said to you, but expressed in such manner as will be suited to a foolish mind.</content>
<content>You and I can hurl bricks at one another, my dear Prioress, and be the better for the exercise.</content>
<content>But we must not fling at little Seraphine aught harder than a pillow of down.</content>
<content>Empty heads, like empty eggshells, are soon broken.</content>
<content>Tell her you have consulted me concerning her desire to return to the world; and that I, being lenient, and holding somewhat wider views on this subject than the majority of prelates, also being well acquainted with the mind of His Holiness the Pope concerning those who embrace the religious life for reasons other than a true vocation, have promised to arrange the matter of a dispensation.</content>
<content>But add that there must be no possibility of any scandal connected with the Nunnery.</content>
<content>Since the Lady Wulgeova, mother of Bishop Wulstan, of blessed memory, took the veil here a century and a half ago, this house has ever been above reproach.</content>
<content>You will tacitly allow her to slip away; and, once away, I will set matters right for her.</content>
<content>But nothing must transpire which could stumble or scandalise the other members of the Community.</content>
<content>The peculiar circumstances which the Knight made known to me always, of course, without making any mention of the name of Seraphine can hardly have occurred in any other case.</content>
<content>It is not likely, for instance, that our worthy Sub-Prioress was torn by treachery from the arms of a despairing lover; and she would undoubtedly share your very limiting ideas of a lover's physical qualities and requirements; possibly not even allowing him a voice.</content>
<content>"Now I happen to know that the Knight daily spends the hour of Vespers in the Cathedral crypt, kneeling before the shrine of Saint Oswald beside a stretcher whereon lies one of his men, much bandaged about the head, swathed in linen, and covered with a cloak.</content>
<content>The Knight has my leave to lay the sick man before the holy relics, daily, for five days.</content>
<content>I asked of him what he expected would result from so doing.</content>
<content>He made answer: 'A great recovery and restoration.'"</content>
<content>The Bishop paused, as if meditating upon the words.</content>
<content>Then he slowly repeated them, taking evident pleasure in each syllable.</content>
<content>"A great recovery and restoration," said the Bishop, and smiled.</content>
<content>"Well?</content>
<content>The blessed relics can do much.</content>
<content>They may avail to mend a broken head.</content>
<content>Could they mend a broken heart?</content>
<content>I know not.</content>
<content>That were, of the two, the greater miracle."</content>
<content>The Bishop glanced at the Prioress.</content>
<content>Her face was averted.</content>
<content>"Well, my daughter, matters being as they are, you may inform Sister Mary Seraphine that, should she chance to lose her way among the hundred and forty-two columns, when passing through the crypt after Vespers, she will find a Knight, who will doubtless know what to do next.</content>
<content>If he can contrive to take her safely from the Cathedral and out of the Precincts, she will have to ride with him to Warwick, where a priest will be in readiness to wed them.</content>
<content>But it would be well that Sister Mary Seraphine should have some practice in mounting and riding, before she goes on so adventurous a journey.</content>
<content>She may remember the crimson trappings of her palfrey, and yet have forgotten how to sit him.</content>
<content>It is for us to make sure that the Knight's brave plans for the safe capture of his lady, do not fail for lack of any help which we may lawfully give."</content>
<content>The Bishop stretched out his hand and took up his biretta.</content>
<content>"When did the nuns last have a Play Day?"</content>
<content>he asked.</content>
<content>"Not a month ago," replied the Prioress.</content>
<content>"They made the hay in the river meadow, and carried it themselves.</content>
<content>They thought it rare sport."</content>
<content>The Bishop put on his biretta.</content>
<content>"Give them a Play Day, dear Prioress, in honour of my visit.</content>
<content>Tell them I asked that they should have it the day after to-morrow.</content>
<content>I will then send you my white palfrey, suitably caparisoned.</content>
<content>Brother Philip, who attends me when I ride, and who has the palfrey well controlled, shall lead him in.</content>
<content>The nuns can then ride in turns, in the river meadow; and our little foolish bird can try her wings, before she attempts the long flight from Worcester to Warwick."</content>
<content>The Bishop rose, crossed the cell, and knelt long, in prayer, before the crucifix.</content>
<content>When he turned toward the door, the Prioress said: "I pray you, give me your blessing, Reverend Father, before you go."</content>
<content>She knelt, and the Bishop extended his hand over her bowed head.</content>
<content>Expecting a Latin formula, she was almost startled when tender words, in the English tongue, fell softly from the Bishop's lips.</content>
<content>"The Lord bless thee, and keep thee; and grant unto thee grace and strength to choose and to do the harder part, when the harder part is His will for thee."</content>
<content>After which: "Benedictio Domini sit vobiscum," said the Bishop; and made the sign of the cross over the bowed head of the Prioress.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XX

HOLLY AND MISTLETOE

Symon, Bishop of Worcester, had bidden Sir Hugh d'Argent to sup with him at the Palace.</content>
<content>It was upon the second day after the Bishop's conversation with the
Prioress in the Convent at Whytstone; the evening of the Nun's Play
Day, granted in honour of his visit.</content>
<content>The Bishop and the Knight supped together, with much stately ceremony, in the great banqueting hall.</content>
<content>Knowing the Bishop's love of the beautiful, and his habit of being punctilious in matters of array and deportment, acquired no doubt during his lengthy sojourns in France and Italy, the Knight had donned his finest court suit white satin, embroidered with silver; jewelled collar, belt, and shoes; a small-sword of exquisite workmanship at his side.</content>
<content>A white cloak, also richly embroidered with silver, hung from his shoulders; white silk hose set off the shapely length of his limbs.</content>
<content>The blood-red gleam of the magnificent rubies on his breast, sword-belt, and shoe-buckles, were the only points of colour in his attire.</content>
<content>The Bishop's keen eyes noted with quiet pleasure how greatly this somewhat fantastically beautiful dress enhanced the dark splendour of the Knight's noble countenance, displayed his superb carriage, and shewed off the supple grace of his limbs, which, in his ordinary garb, rather gave the idea of massive strength alone.</content>
<content>The Bishop himself wore crimson and gold; and, just as the dark beauty of the Knight was enhanced by the fair white and silver of his dress, so did these gorgeous Italian robes set off the frail whiteness of the Bishop's delicate face, the silvery softness of his abundant hair.</content>
<content>And just as the collar of rubies gleamed like fiery eyes upon the Knight's white satin doublet, so from out the pallor of the Prelate's countenance the eyes shone forth, bright with the fires of eternal, youth, the gay joy of life, the twinkling humour of a shrewd yet kindly wit.</content>
<content>They supped at a round table of small size, in the very centre of the huge apartment.</content>
<content>It formed a point of light and brightness from which all else merged into shadow, and yet deeper shadow, until the eye reached the dark panelling of the walls.</content>
<content>The light seemed to centre in the Knight white and silver; the colour, in the figure of the Bishop crimson and gold.</content>
<content>In and out of the shadows, swift and silent, on sandalled feet, moved the lay-brothers serving the feast; watchful of each detail; quickly supplying every need.</content>
<content>At length they loaded the table with fruit; put upon it fresh flagons of wine, and finally withdrew; each black-robed figure merging into the black shadows, and vanishing in silence.</content>
<content>The Bishop's Chaplain appeared in a distant doorway.</content>
<content>"Benedicite," said Symon of Worcester, looking up.</content>
<content>"Deus," replied the Chaplain, making a profound obeisance.</content>
<content>Then he stood erect a grim, austere figure, hard features, hollow eyes, half-shrouded within his cowl.</content>
<content>He looked with sinister disapproval at the distant table, laden with fruit and flagons; at the Bishop and the Knight, now sitting nigh to one another; the Bishop in his chair of state facing the door, the Knight, on a high-backed seat at the Bishop's right hand, half-way round the table.</content>
<content>"Holly and Mistletoe," muttered the Chaplain, as he closed the great door.</content>
<content>"Yea, verily!</content>
<content>Mistletoe and Holly," he repeated, as he strode to his cell.</content>
<content>"The Reverend Father sups with the World, and indulges the Flesh.</content>
<content>Methinks the Devil cannot be far off."</content>
<content>Nor was he.</content>
<content>He was very near.</content>
<content>He had looked over the Chaplain's shoulder as he made his false obeisance in the doorway.</content>
<content>But he liked not the pure white of the Knight's dress, and he feared the clear light in the Prelate's eyes.</content>
<content>So, when the Chaplain closed the door, the Devil stayed on the outside, and now walked beside the Chaplain along the passage leading to his cell.</content>
<content>There is no surer way of securing the company of the Devil, than to make sure he is at that moment busy with another particularly if that other chance to be the most saintly man you know, and merely displeasing to you, at the moment, because he hath not bidden you to sup with him.</content>
<content>The Devil and the Chaplain made a night of it.</content>
<content>The Bishop's gentle "Benedicite" spread white wings and flew, like an affrighted dove, over the head of the bowing Chaplain, into the chill passage beyond.</content>
<content>But, just as the great door was closing, it darted in again, circled round the banqueting hall, and came back to rest in the safe nest of the kindly heart which had sent it forth.</content>
<content>No blessing, truly vitalised, ever ceases to live.</content>
<content>If the blessed be unworthy, it returns on swift wing to the blesser.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXI

SO MUCH FOR SERAPHINE!</content>
<content>A sense of peace fell upon the banqueting hall, with the closing of the door.</content>
<content>All unrest and suspicion seemed to have departed.</content>
<content>An atmosphere of confidence and serenity pervaded the great chamber.</content>
<content>It was in the Bishop's smile, as he turned to the Knight.</content>
<content>"At length the time has come when we may talk freely; and truly, my son, we have much to say."</content>
<content>The Knight glanced round the spacious hall, and his look implied that he would prefer to talk in a smaller chamber.</content>
<content>"Nay, then," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"No situation can be better for a private conversation than the very centre of a very large room.</content>
<content>Have you not heard it said that walls have ears?</content>
<content>Well, in a small room, they may use them to some purpose.</content>
<content>But here, we sit so far removed from the walls that, strain their ears as they may, they will hear nothing; even the very key-hole, opening wide its naughty eye, will see naught, neither will the adjacent ear hear anything.</content>
<content>We may speak freely."</content>
<content>The Bishop, signing to the Knight to help himself to fruit, moved the wine toward him.</content>
<content>At his own right hand stood a Venetian flagon and goblet of ruby glass, ornamented with vine leaves and clusters of grapes.</content>
<content>The Bishop drank only from this flagon, pouring its contents himself into the goblet which he held to the light before he drank from it, enjoying the rich glow of colour, and the beauty of the engraving.</content>
<content>His guests sometimes wondered what specially choice kind of wine the Bishop kept for his own, exclusive use.</content>
<content>If they asked, he told them.</content>
<content>"The kind used at the marriage feast at Cana in Galilee, when the supply of an inferior quality had failed.</content>
<content>This, my friends, is pure water, wholesome, refreshing, and not costly.</content>
<content>I drink it from glass which gives to it the colour of the juice of the grape, partly in order that my guests may not feel chilled in their own enjoyment of more gay and luscious beverage; partly because I enjoy the emblem.</content>
<content>"The gifts of circumstance, life, and nature, vary, not so much in themselves, as in the human vessels which contain them.</content>
<content>If the heart be a ruby goblet, the humblest form of pure love filling it, will assume the rich tint and fervour of romance.</content>
<content>If the mind be, in itself, a thing of vivid tints and glowing colours, the dullest thought within it will take on a lustre, a sparkle, a glow of brilliancy.</content>
<content>Thus, whensoever men or matters seem to me dull or wearisome, to myself I say: 'Symon!</content>
<content>Thou art this day, thyself, a pewter pot.'"</content>
<content>Then the Bishop would fill up his goblet and hold it to the light.</content>
<content>"Aye, the best wine!"</content>
<content>he would say.</content>
<content>"'Thou hast kept the best wine until now.'</content>
<content>The water of earth drawn by faithful servants, acting in unquestioning obedience to the commands of the blessed Mother of our Lord transmuted by the word and power of the Divine Son; outpoured for others, in loving service; this is ever 'the best wine.'"</content>
<content>The Knight filled his goblet and took some fruit.</content>
<content>Then, leaving both untouched, turned his chair sidewise, that he might the better face the Bishop, crossed his knees, leaned his right elbow on the table and his head upon his hand, pushing his fingers into his hair.</content>
<content>Thus, for a while, they sat in silence; the Knight's eyes searching the
Bishop's face; the Bishop, intent upon the colour of his ruby goblet.</content>
<content>At length Hugh d'Argent spoke.</content>
<content>"I have been through deep waters, Reverend Father, since last I supped with you."</content>
<content>The Bishop put down the goblet.</content>
<content>"So I supposed, my son.</content>
<content>Now tell me what you will, neither more nor less.</content>
<content>I will then give you what counsel I can.</content>
<content>On the one point concerning which you must not tell me more than I may rightly know, I will question you.</content>
<content>Have you contrived to see the woman you loved, and lost, and are now seeking to regain?</content>
<content>Tell me not how, nor when, nor where; but have you had speech with her?</content>
<content>Have you made clear to her the treachery which sundered you?</content>
<content>Have you pleaded with her to remember her early betrothal, to renounce these later vows, and to fly with you?"</content>
<content>The Knight looked straight into the Bishop's keen eyes.</content>
<content>At first he could not bring himself to answer.</content>
<content>This princely figure, with his crimson robes and golden cross, so visibly represented the power and authority of the Church.</content>
<content>His own intrusion into the Nunnery, his attempt to win away a holy nun, suddenly appeared to him, as the most appalling sacrilege.</content>
<content>With awe and consternation in his own, he met the Bishop's eyes.</content>
<content>At first they were merely clear and searching, and the Knight sat tongue-tied.</content>
<content>But presently there flicked into them a look so human, so tender, so completely understanding, that straightway the tongue of the Knight was loosed.</content>
<content>"My lord, I have," he said.</content>
<content>"All those things have I done.</content>
<content>I have been in heaven, Reverend Father, and I have been in hell "

"Sh, my son," murmured the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Methinks you have been in a place which is neither heaven nor hell; though it may, on occasion, approximate somewhat nearly to both.</content>
<content>How you got there, is a marvel to me; and how you escaped, without creating a scandal, an even greater wonder.</content>
<content>Yet I think it wise, for the present, not to know too much.</content>
<content>I merely required to be certain that you had actually found your lost betrothed, made her aware of your proximity, your discovery, and your desires.</content>
<content>I gathered that you had succeeded in so doing; for, two days ago, the Prioress herself sent to beg a private interview with me, in order to ask whether, under certain circumstances, I could approve the return of a nun to the world, and obtain absolution from her vows."</content>
<content>The rubies on the Knight's breast suddenly glittered, as if a bound of his heart had caused them all to leap together.</content>
<content>But, except for that quick sparkle, he sat immovable, and made no sign.</content>
<content>The Bishop had marked the gleam of the rubies.</content>
<content>He lifted his Venetian goblet to the light and observed it carefully, as he continued: "The Prioress a most wise and noble lady, of whom I told you on the day when you first questioned me concerning the Nunnery has been having trouble with a nun, by name Sister Mary Seraphine.</content>
<content>This young and lovely lady has, just lately, heard the world loudly calling on her own shewing, through the neighing of a palfrey bringing to mind past scenes of gaiety.</content>
