<content>But the Prioress suspicioned the voice of an earthly lover; and I, knowing how reckless and resolute an earthly lover was attempting to invade the Nunnery, we both the Prioress and I drew our own conclusions, and proceeded to face the problem with which we found ourselves confronted, namely: whether to allow or to thwart the flight of Seraphine."</content>
<content>The Knight, toying with walnuts, held at the moment four in the palm of his right hand.</content>
<content>They broke with a four-fold crack, which sounded but as one mighty crunch.</content>
<content>Then, all unconscious of what he did, the Knight opened his great hand and let fall upon the table, a little heap of crushed nuts, shells and white flesh inextricably mixed.</content>
<content>The Bishop glanced at the small heap.</content>
<content>The veiled twinkle in his eyes seemed to say; "So much for Seraphine!"</content>
<content>"I know not any lady of that name," said the Knight.</content>
<content>"Not by that name, my son.</content>
<content>The nuns are not known in the Convent by the names they bore before they left the world.</content>
<content>I happen to know that the Prioress, before she professed, was Mora, Countess of Norelle.</content>
<content>I know this because, years ago, I saw her at the Court, when she was a maid of honour to the Queen; very young and lovely; yet, even then remarkable for wisdom, piety, and a certain sweet dignity of deportment.</content>
<content>Sometimes now, when she receives me in the severe habit of her Order, I find myself remembering the flow of beautiful hair, soft as spun silk, bound by a circlet of gold round the regal head; the velvet and ermine; the jewels at her breast.</content>
<content>Yet do I chide myself for recalling things which these holy women have renounced, and doubtless would fain forget."</content>
<content>The Bishop struck a silver gong with his left hand.</content>
<content>At once a distant door opened in the dark panelling and two black-robed figures glided in.</content>
<content>"Kindle a fire on the hearth," commanded the Bishop; adding to his guest: "The evening air strikes chilly.</content>
<content>Also I greatly love the smell of burning wood.</content>
<content>It is pungent to the nostrils, and refreshing to the brain."</content>
<content>The monks hastened to kindle the wood and to fan it into a flame.</content>
<content>Presently, the fire blazing brightly, the Bishop rose, and signed to the monks to place the chairs near the great fireplace.</content>
<content>This they did; and, making profound obeisance, withdrew.</content>
<content>Thus the Bishop and the Knight, alone once more, were seated in the firelight.</content>
<content>As it illumined the white and silver doublet, and glowed in the rubies, the Bishop conceived the whimsical fancy that the Knight might well be some splendid archangel, come down to force the Convent gates and carry off a nun to heaven.</content>
<content>And the Knight, watching the leaping flame flicker on the Bishop's crimson robes and silvery hair, saw the lenient smile upon the saintly face and took courage as he realised how kindly was the heart, filled with most human sympathy, which beat beneath the cross of gold upon the Prelate's breast.</content>
<content>Leaning forward, the Bishop lifted the faggot-fork and moved one of the burning logs so that a jet of blue smoke, instead of mounting the chimney, came out toward them on the hearth.</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester sat back and inhaled it with enjoyment.</content>
<content>"This is refreshing," he said.</content>
<content>"This soothes and yet braces the mind.</content>
<content>And now, my son, let us return to the question of your own private concerns.</content>
<content>First, let me ask Hugh, dear lad, as friend and counsellor I ask it are you able now to tell me the name of the woman you desire to wed?"</content>
<content>"Nay, my dear lord," replied the Knight, "that I cannot do.</content>
<content>I guard her name, as I would guard mine honour.</content>
<content>If as may our Lady be pleased to grant she consent to fly with me, her name will still be mine to guard; yet then all men may know it, so they speak it with due respect and reverence.</content>
<content>But if as may our blessed Lady forbid she withhold herself from me, so that three days hence I ride away alone; then must I ride away leaving no shadow of reproach on her fair fame.</content>
<content>Her name will be forever in my heart; but no word of mine shall have left it, in the mind of any man, linked with broken vows, or a forsaken lover."</content>
<content>The Bishop looked long and earnestly at the Knight.</content>
<content>"That being so, my son," he said at length, "for want of any better name, I needs must call her by the name she bears in the Nunnery, and now speak with you of Sister Mary Seraphine."</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent frowned.</content>
<content>"I care not to hear of this Seraphine," he said.</content>
<content>"Yet I fear me you must summon patience to hear of Seraphine for a few moments," said the Bishop, quietly; 'seeing that I have here a letter from the Prioress herself, in which she sends you a message.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Ah!</content>
<content>I marvel not that you are taken by surprise, my dear Knight; but keep your seat, and let not your hand fly so readily to your sword.</content>
<content>To transfix the Reverend Mother's gracious epistle on your blade's keen point, would not tend to elucidate her meaning; nor could it alter the fact that she sends you important counsel concerning Sister Mary Seraphine."</content>
<content>The Bishop lighted a wax taper standing at his elbow, drew a letter from the folds of his sash, slowly unfolded and held it to the light.</content>
<content>The Knight sat silent, his face in shadow.</content>
<content>The leaping flame of the fire played on his sword hilt and on the rubies across his breast.</content>
<content>As the parchment crackled between the Bishop's fingers, the Knight kept himself well in hand; but he prayed he might not have need to speak, nor to meet the Bishop's eyes.</content>
<content>These the saints be praised were now intent upon the closely written page.</content>
<content>The light of the taper illumined the almost waxen whiteness of the gentle face, and gleamed upon the Bishop's ring.</content>
<content>The Knight, fixing his eyes upon the stone, saw it the colour of red wine.</content>
<content>At last the Bishop began to speak with careful deliberation, his eyes upon the letter, yet telling, instead of reading; a method ofttimes maddening to an anxious listener, eager to snatch the parchment and master its contents for himself; yet who must perforce wait to receive them, with due patience, from another.</content>
<content>"The Prioress relates to me first of all a conversation she had, by my suggestion, with Sister Mary Serephine, in which she told that lady much of what passed between herself and me when she consulted me upon the apparent desire of this nun to escape from the Convent, renounce her vows, and return to her lover and the world her lover who had come to save her."</content>
<content>The Bishop paused.</content>
<content>The Knight stirred uneasily in his seat.</content>
<content>A net seemed to be closing around him.</content>
<content>Almost he saw himself compelled to ride to Warwick in company with this most undesired and undesirable nun, Mary Seraphine.</content>
<content>The Bishop raised his eyes from the letter and looked pensively into the fire.</content>
<content>"A most piteous scene took place," he said, "on the day when Sister Seraphine first heard again the call of the outer world.</content>
<content>Most moving it was, as told me by the Prioress.</content>
<content>The distraught nun lay upon the floor of her cell in an abandonment of frantic weeping.</content>
<content>She imitated the galloping of a horse with her hands and feet, a ride of some sort evidently being in her mind.</content>
<content>At length she lifted a swollen countenance, crying that her lover had come to save her."</content>
<content>The Knight clenched his teeth, in despair.</content>
<content>Almost, he and this fearsome nun had arrived at Warwick, and she was lifting a swollen countenance to him that he might embrace it.</content>
<content>Yet Mora well knew that he had not come for any Seraphine!</content>
<content>Mora might deny herself to him; but she would not foist another upon him.</content>
<content>Only, alas!</content>
<content>this grave and Reverend Prioress of whom the Bishop spoke, hardly seemed one with the woman of his desire; she who, but three evenings before, had yielded her lips to his, clasping her arms around him; loving, even while she denied him.</content>
<content>The Bishop's eyes were again upon the letter.</content>
<content>"The Prioress," he said, "with her usual instinctive sense of the helpfulness of outward surroundings, and desiring, with a fine justice, to give Seraphine and her lover every possible advantage, arranged that the conversation should take place in the Nunnery garden, in a secluded spot where they could not be overheard, yet where the sunshine glinted, through overhanging branches, flecking, in golden patches, the soft turf; where birds carolled, and spread swift wings; where white clouds chased one another across the blue sky; in fact, my son," said the Bishop, suddenly looking up, "where all Nature sang aloud of liberty and nonrestraint."</content>
<content>The Knight's eyes, frowning from beneath a shading hand, were gloomy and full of sombre fury.</content>
<content>It mattered not to him in what surroundings this preposterous offer, that she should leave the Convent and fly with him to Warwick, had been made to Seraphine.</content>
<content>Her swollen countenance would be equally unattractive, whether lifted in cell or cloister, or where white clouds chased one another across the blue sky!</content>
<content>The Knight felt as if he were being chased, and by something more to be feared than a white cloud.</content>
<content>Grim Nemesis pursued him.</content>
<content>This reverend prelate, whom he had deemed so wise, was well-nigh witless.</content>
<content>Yet Mora knew the truth.</content>
<content>Would her kind hands deal him so base a blow?</content>
<content>The Bishop saw the brooding rage in the Knight's eyes, and he lowered his own to the letter, in time to hide their twinkling.</content>
<content>Even the best and bravest of Knights, for having forced his way into a Nunnery, pressed a suit upon a nun, and escaped unscathed, deserved some punishment at the hands of the Church!</content>
<content>"Which was generous in the Reverend Mother," said the Bishop, 'since she was inclined, upon the whole, to disapprove this offering of liberty to the restless nun.</content>
<content>You can well understand that, the responsibility for the good conduct of that entire Community resting upon the Prioress, she is bound to regard with disfavour any innovation which might tend to provoke a scandal."</content>
<content>The Bishop did not look up, or he would have seen dull despair displacing the Knight's anger.</content>
<content>"However she appears faithfully to have laid before Sister Mary Seraphine, my view of the matter, giving her to understand that I am inclined to be lenient concerning vows made under misapprehension; also that, when there is not a true vocation, and a worldly spirit chafes against the cloistered life, I regard its presence within the Community as more likely to be harmful to the common weal, than the short-lived scandal which might arise if those in power should connive at an escape."</content>
<content>The Knight moved impatiently in his seat.</content>
<content>"Could we arrive, my lord," he said, "at the Lady Prioress's message, of which you spoke?"</content>
<content>"We are tending thither, my son," replied the Bishop, unruffled.</content>
<content>"Curb your impatience.</content>
<content>We of the Cloister are wont to move slowly, with measured tread each step a careful following up of the step which went before not with the leaps and bounds and capers of the laity.</content>
<content>In due time we shall reach the message.</content>
<content>"Well, in this conversation the Prioress appears to have complied with my suggestions, excepting in the matter of one most important detail, concerning which she used her own discretion.</content>
<content>I distinctly advised her to tell Seraphine that we were aware of your arrival, and that to my certain knowledge you were in the crypt each afternoon at the hour when the White Ladies pass to and from Vespers.</content>
<content>In fact, my dear Knight, I even went so far as to suggest to the Reverend Mother to give Sister Mary Seraphine to understand that if she stepped aside, losing her way among the many pillars, you would probably know what to do next.</content>
<content>"But the Reverend Mother writes" at last the Bishop began to read: "'I felt so sure from your description of the noble Knight who came to you in his trouble, that he cannot be the lover of this shallow-hearted little Seraphine, that I deemed it wise not to tell her of his arrival, nor to mention your idea, that the woman he seeks is to be found in this Nunnery.'"</content>
<content>The smothered sound which broke from the Knight was a mixture of triumph, relief, and most bitter laughter.</content>
<content>"Now that is like the Prioress," said the Bishop; "thus to use her own judgment, setting at naught my superior knowledge of the facts, and flouting my authority!</content>
<content>A noble nature, Hugh, and most lovable; yet an imperious will, and a strength of character and purpose unusual in a woman.</content>
<content>Had she remained in the world and married, her husband would have found it somewhat difficult wholly to mould her to his will.</content>
<content>Yet to possess such a woman would have been worth adventuring much.</content>
<content>But I must not fret you, dear lad, by talking of the Prioress, when your mind is intent upon arriving at the decision of Seraphine.</content>
<content>"Well, I fear me, I have but sorry news for you.</content>
<content>The Reverend Mother writes: 'Sister Mary Seraphine expressed herself as completely satisfied with the cloistered life.</content>
<content>She declared that her desire to return to the world had been but a passing phase, of which she was completely purged by the timely discipline of Mother Sub-Prioress, and by the fact that she has been appointed, with Sister Mary Gabriel, to embroider the new altar-cloth for the Chapel.</content>
<content>She talked more eagerly about a stitch she is learning from Mary Gabriel, than about any of those by-gone memories, which certainly had seemed most poignantly revived in her; and I had no small difficulty in turning her mind from the all-absorbing question as to how to obtain the right tint for the pomegranates.</content>
<content>My lord, to a mind thus intent upon needle-work for the Altar of God, I could scarce have brought myself to mention the call of an earthly lover, even had I believed your Knight to be seeking Seraphine.</content>
<content>Her heart is now wedded to the Cloister.'"</content>
<content>The Bishop looked up.</content>
<content>"Therefore, my son, we must conclude that your secret interview, whenever or wherever it took place, had no effect will bear no lasting fruit."</content>
<content>The Bishop could not resist this allusion to the pomegranates of Seraphine.</content>
<content>But Hugh d'Argent, face to face with the suspended portcullis of his fate, trampled all such gossamer beneath impatient feet.</content>
<content>He moistened his dry lips.</content>
<content>"The message," he said.</content>
<content>The Bishop lifted the letter.</content>
<content>"'But,'" he read, "'if you still believe your noble Knight to be the lover of Seraphine, then I pray you to tell him this from me.</content>
<content>No nun worthy of a brave man's love, would consent to break her vows.</content>
<content>A nun who could renounce her vows to go to him, would wrong herself and him, bringing no blessing to his home.</content>
<content>Better an empty hearth, than a hearth where broods a curse.</content>
<content>I ask you, my lord, to give this as a message to that noble Knight from me the Prioress of this House and to bid him go in peace, praying for a heart submissive to the will of God.'"</content>
<content>The Bishop's voice fell silent.</content>
<content>He had maintained its quiet tones, yet perforce had had to rise to something of the dignity of this final pronouncement of the Prioress, and he spoke the last words with deep emotion.</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent leaned forward, his elbows on his knees; then dropped his head upon his hands, and so stayed motionless.</content>
<content>The portcullis had fallen.</content>
<content>Its iron spikes transfixed his very soul.</content>
<content>She was his, yet lost to him.</content>
<content>This final word of her authority, this speaking, through the Bishop's mouth, yet with the dignity of her own high office, all seemed of set intent, to beat out the last ray of hope within him.</content>
<content>As he sat silent, with bowed head, wild thoughts chased through his brain.</content>
<content>He was back with her in the subterranean way.</content>
<content>He knelt at her feet in the yellow circle of the lantern's light.</content>
<content>Her tender hands, her woman's hands, her firm yet gentle hands, fell on his head; the fingers moved, with soothing touch, in and out of his hair.</content>
<content>Then when his love and longing broke through his control came her surrender.</content>
<content>Ah, when she was in his arms, why did he loose her?</content>
<content>Or, when she had unlocked the door, and the dim, grey light, like a pearly dawn at sea, stole downward from the crypt, why, like a fool, did he mount the steps alone, and leave her standing there?</content>
<content>Why did he not fling his cloak about her, and carry her up, whether she would or no?</content>
<content>"Why?"</content>
<content>cried the demon of despair in his soul.</content>
<content>"Ah, why!"</content>
<content>But, even then, his own true heart made answer.</content>
<content>He had loosed her because he loved her too well to hold her to him when she had seemed to wish to stand free.</content>
<content>And he had gone alone, because never would he force a woman to come with him against her will.</content>
<content>His very strength was safeguard to her weakness.</content>
<content>Presently Hugh heard the Bishop folding the Prioress's letter.</content>
<content>He lifted his head and held out his hand.</content>
<content>The Bishop was slipping the letter into his sash.</content>
<content>He paused.</content>
<content>Those eyes implored.</content>
<content>That outstretched hand demanded.</content>
<content>"Nay, dear lad," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"I may not give it you, because it mentions the White Ladies by name, the Order, and poor little shallow, changeful Seraphine herself, But this much I will do: as you may not have it, none other shall."</content>
<content>With which the Bishop, unfolding the Prioress's letter, flung it upon the burning logs.</content>
<content>Together they watched it curl and blacken; uncurl again, and slowly flake away.</content>
<content>Long after the rest had fallen to ashes, this sentence remained clear: "Better an empty hearth; than a hearth where broods a curse."</content>
<content>The flames played about it, but still it remained legible; white letters, upon a black ground; then, letters of fire upon grey ashes.</content>
<content>Of a sudden the Knight, seizing the faggot-fork, dashed out the words with a stroke.</content>
<content>"I would risk the curse," he cried, with passion.</content>
<content>"By Pilate's water,
I would risk the curse!"</content>
<content>"I know you would, my son," said the Bishop, "and, by our Lady's crown, I would have let you risk it, believing, as I do, that it would end in blessing.</content>
<content>But listen, Hugh.</content>
<content>In asking what you asked, you scarce know what you did.</content>
<content>You need not say 'yea,' nor 'nay,' but I incline to think with the Reverend Mother, that the woman you sought was not foolish little Seraphine, turned one way by the neighing of a palfrey, another by the embroidering of a pomegranate.</content>
<content>There are women of finer mould in that Nunnery, any one of whom may be your lost betrothed.</content>
<content>But of this we may be sure: whosoever she be, the Prioress knows her, and knew of whom she wrote when she sent you that message.</content>
<content>She has the entire confidence of all in the Nunnery.</content>
<content>I verily believe she knows them better than does their confessor a saintly old man, but dim.</content>
<content>"Now, listen to me.</content>
<content>I said you knew not what you asked.</content>
<content>Hugh, my lad, if you had won your betrothed away, you would have had much to learn and much to unlearn.</content>
<content>Believe me, I know women, as only a priest of many years' standing can know them.</content>
<content>Women are either bad or good.</content>
<content>The bad are bad below man's understanding, because their badness is not leavened by one grain of honour; a fact the worst of men will ever fail to grasp.</content>
<content>The good are good above man's comprehension, because their perfect purity of heart causeth the spirit ever to triumph over the flesh; and their love-instinct is the instinct of self-sacrifice.</content>
<content>Every true woman is a Madonna in the home, or fain would be, if her man would let her.</content>
<content>To such a woman, each promise of a child is an Annunciation; our Lady's awe and wonder, whisper again in the temple of her inner being; for her love has deified the man she loves; and, it seems to her, a child of his and hers must be a holy babe, born into the world to help redeem it.</content>
<content>And so it would be, could she but have her way.</content>
<content>But too often the man fails to understand, and so spoils the perfect plan.</content>
<content>And she to whom love means self-sacrifice, sacrifices all even her noblest ideals sooner than fail a call upon her love.</content>
<content>Yet I say again, could the Madonna instinct have had full sway, the world would have been redeemed ere now to holiness, to happiness, to health.</content>
<content>"You looked high, my son, by your own shewing.</content>
<content>You loved high.</content>
<content>Your love was worthy, for you remained faithful, when you believed you had been betrayed.</content>
<content>Let your consolation now be the knowledge that she also was faithful, and that it is a double faithfulness which keeps her from responding to the call of your love.</content>
<content>Seek union with her on the spiritual plane, and some day in the Realm where all noble things shall attain unto full perfection you may yet give thanks that your love was not allowed to pass through the perilous pitfalls of an earthly union."</content>
<content>The Knight looked at the delicate face of the Bishop, with its wistful smile, its charm of extreme refinement.</content>
<content>Yes!</content>
<content>Here spoke the Prelate, the Idealist, the Mystic.</content>
<content>But the Knight was a man and a lover.</content>
<content>His dark face flushed, and his eyes grew bright with inward fires such as the Bishop could hardly be expected to understand.</content>
<content>"I want not spiritual planes," he said, "nor realms of perfection.</content>
<content>I want my own wife, in my own home; and, could I have won her there, I have not much doubt but that I could have lifted her over any perilous pitfalls that came in her way."</content>
<content>"True, my son," said the Bishop, at once gently acquiescent; for Symon of Worcester invariably yielded a point which had been misunderstood.</content>
<content>For over-rating a mind with which he conversed, this was ever his self-imposed penance.</content>
<content>"Your great strength would be fully equal to lifting ladies over pitfalls.</content>
<content>Which recalls to my mind a scene in this day's events, which I would fain describe to you before we part."</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXII

WHAT BROTHER PHILIP HAD TO TELL

The Bishop sat back in his chair, smiling, as at a mental picture which gave him pleasure, coupled with some amusement.</content>
<content>Ignoring the Knight's sullen silence, he began his story in the cheerful voice which takes for granted a willing and an interested listener.</content>
<content>"When the Prioress and myself were discussing your hopes, my son, and I was urging, in your interests, liberty of flight for Sister Mary Seraphine, I informed the Reverend Mother that the carrying out of your plans, carefully laid in order to keep any scandal concerning the White Ladies from reaching the city, would involve for Seraphine a ride of many hours to Warwick, almost immediately upon safely reaching the Star hostel.</content>
<content>This seemed as nothing to the lover who, by his own shewing, had ofttimes seen her 'ride like a bird, all day, on the moors.'</content>
<content>But to us who know the effect of monastic life and how quickly such matters as these become lost arts through disuse, this romantic ride in the late afternoon and on into the summer night, loomed large as a possible obstacle to the successful flight of Seraphine.</content>
<content>"Therefore, in order that our little bird might try her wings, regain her seat and mastery of a horse, and rid herself of a first painful stiffness, I persuaded the Reverend Mother to grant the nuns a Play Day, in honour of my visit, promising to send them my white palfrey, suitably caparisoned, in safe charge of a good lay-brother, so that all nuns who pleased, might ride in the river meadow.</content>
<content>You would not think it," said the Bishop, with a smile, "but the White Ladies dearly love such sport, when it is lawful.</content>
<content>They have an aged ass which they gleefully mount in turns, on Play Days, in the courtyard and in the meadow.</content>
<content>Therefore riding is not altogether strange to them, although my palfrey, Iconoklastes, is somewhat of an advance upon their mild ass, Sheba."</content>
<content>The Knight's sad face had brightened at mention of the beasts.</content>
<content>"Wherefore 'Iconoklastes'?"</content>
<content>he asked, with interest.</content>
<content>It struck him as a curious name for a palfrey.</content>
<content>"Because," replied the Bishop, 'soon after I had bought him he trampled to ruin, in a fit of misplaced merriment, some flower beds on which I had spent much precious time and care, and of which I was inordinately fond."</content>
<content>"Brute," said the Knight, puzzled, but unwilling to admit it.</content>
<content>"Methinks I should have named him 'Devil,' for the doing of such diabolic mischief."</content>
<content>"Nay," said the Bishop, gently.</content>
<content>"The Devil would have spared my flower beds.</content>
<content>They were a snare unto me."</content>
<content>"And wherefore 'Sheba'?"</content>
<content>queried the Knight.</content>
<content>"I named her so, when I gave her to the Prioress," said the Bishop, "in reply to a question put to me by the Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>The ass was elderly and mild, even then, but a handsome creature, of good breed.</content>
<content>The Prioress asked me whether she still had too much spirit to be easily managed by the lay-sisters.</content>
<content>I answered that her name was 'Sheba.'"</content>
<content>The Bishop paused and rubbed his hands softly over each other, in gleeful enjoyment of the recollection.</content>
<content>But the Knight again looked blank.</content>
<content>"Did that content the Prioress?"</content>
<content>he asked; but chiefly for love of mentioning her name.</content>
<content>"Perfectly," replied the Bishop.</content>
<content>"She smiled and said: 'That is well.'</content>
<content>And the name stuck to the ass, though the Reverend Mother and I alone understood its meaning."</content>
<content>"About the Play Day?"</content>
<content>suggested the Knight, growing restive.</content>
<content>"Ah, yes!</content>
<content>About the Play Day.</content>
<content>The time chosen was after noon on this day, in order that the Prioress might first accomplish her talk with Seraphine, thus clearing the way for our experiment.</content>
<content>Although written last evening, I had not received the Reverend Mother's decisive letter, when Iconoklastes set forth; and, I confess, I looked forward with keen interest, to questioning the lay-brother on his return.</content>
<content>As I have told you, I had doubts concerning Seraphine; but I knew the Prioress would see to it that my meaning and intention reached the member of the Community actually concerned, were she Seraphine or another; and I should have light, both on the identity of the lady and on her probable course of action, when report reached me as to which of the nuns had taken the riding seriously.</content>
<content>Therefore, with no little interest, I awaited the return of Iconoklastes, in charge of Brother Philip."</content>
<content>The Bishop lifted the faggot-fork and, bending over the hearth, began to build the logs, quickening the dying flame.</content>
<content>"Well?"</content>
<content>cried the Knight, chafing like a charger on the curb.</content>
<content>"Well, my lord?</content>
<content>And then?"</content>
<content>The Bishop stood the faggot-fork in its corner.</content>
<content>"I paused, my son, that you might say: 'Wherefore "Philip"?'"</content>
<content>"The names of men interest me not," said the Knight, with impatience.</content>
<content>"I care but to know the reason for the names of beasts."</content>
<content>"Quite right," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Adam named the beasts; Eve named the men.</content>
<content>Yet, I would like you to ask 'Wherefore "Philip,"' because the Prioress at once put that question, when she heard me call Brother Mark by his new name."</content>
<content>"Wherefore 'Philip'?"</content>
<content>asked the Knight, with averted eyes.</content>
<content>"Because 'Philip' signifies 'a lover of horses.'</content>
<content>I named the good brother so, when he developed a great affection for all the steeds in my stables.</content>
<content>"Well, at length Brother Philip returned, leading the palfrey.</content>
<content>I had been riding upon the heights above the town, on my comely black mare, Shulamite."</content>
<content>Again the Bishop paused, and shot a merry challenge at Hugh d'Argent; but realising at once that the Knight could brook no more delay, he hastened on.</content>
<content>"Riding into the courtyard, just as Philip led in the palfrey, I bade him first to see to Icon's comfort; then come to my chamber and report.</content>
<content>Before long the lay-brother appeared.</content>
<content>"Now Brother Philip is an excellent teller of stories.</content>
<content>He does not need to mar them by additions, because his quickness of observation takes in every detail, and his excellent memory lets nothing slip.</content>
<content>He has a faculty for recalling past scenes in pictures, and tells a story as if describing a thing just happening before his mental vision: the sole draw-back to so vivid a memory being, that if the picture grows too mirth provoking, Brother Philip is seized with spasms of the diaphragm, and further description becomes impossible.</content>
<content>On this occasion, I saw at once that the good brother's inner vision teemed with pictures.</content>
<content>I settled myself to listen.</content>
<content>"Aye, it had been a wonderful scene, and more merriment, so the lay-sisters afterwards told Brother Philip, than ever known before at any Play Day.</content>
<content>"Icon was led in state from the courtyard, down into the river meadow.</content>
<content>"At first the great delight was to crowd round him, pat him, stroke his mane, finger his trappings; cry out words of ecstatic praise and admiration, and attempt to feed him with all manner of unsuitable food.</content>
<content>"Icon, I gather, behaved much as most males behave on finding themselves the centre of a crowd of admiring women.</content>
<content>He pawed the ground, and swished his tail; arched his neck, and looked from side to side; munched cakes he did not want, winking a large and roguish eye at Brother Philip; and finally, ignoring all the rest, fixed a languorous gaze upon the Prioress, she being the only lady present who stood apart, regarding the scene, but taking no share in the general adulation.</content>
<content>"At length the riding began; Brother Philip keeping firm hold on Icon, while the entire party of nuns undertook to mount the nun who had elected to ride.</content>
<content>Each time Brother Philip attempted a description of this part of the proceedings he was at once seized with such spasms in the region of his girdle, that speech became an impossibility; he could but hold himself helplessly, looking at me from out streaming eyes, until a fresh peep at his mental picture again bent him double.</content>
<content>"Much as I prefer a story complete, from start to finish, I was constrained to command Brother Philip to pass on to scenes which would allow him some possibility of articulate speech.</content>
<content>"The sternness of my tones gave to the good brother the necessary assistance.</content>
<content>In a voice still weak and faltering, but gaining firmness as it proceeded, he described the riding.</content>
<content>"Most of the nuns rode but a few yards, held in place by so many willing hands that, from a distance, only the noble head of Icon could be seen above the moving crowd, surmounted by the terrified face of the riding nun; who, hastening to exclaim that her own delight must not cause her to keep others from participation, would promptly fall off into the waiting arms held out to catch her; at once becoming, when safely on her feet, the boldest encourager of the next aspirant to a seat upon the back of Icon.</content>
<content>"Sister Mary Seraphine proved a disappointment.</content>
<content>She had been wont to boast so much of her own palfrey, her riding, and her hunting, that the other nuns had counted upon seeing her gallop gaily over the field.</content>
<content>"The humble and short-lived attempts were all made first.</content>
<content>Then Sister Mary Seraphine, bidding the others stand aside, was swung by one tall sister, acting according to her instructions, neatly into the saddle.</content>
<content>"She gathered up the reins, as to the manner born," and bade Brother Philip loose the bridle.</content>
<content>But the palfrey, finding himself no longer hemmed in by a heated, pressing crowd, gave, for very gladness of heart, a gay little gambol.</content>
<content>"Whereupon, Sister Mary Seraphine, almost unseated, shrieked to Brother
Philip to hold the bridle, rating him soundly for having let go.</content>
<content>"He then led Icon about the meadow, the nuns following in procession; Sister Seraphine all the while complaining; first of the saddle, which gripped her where it should not, leaving an empty space there where support was needed; then of the palfrey's paces; then of a twist in her garments twice the procession stopped to adjust them; then of the ears of the horse which twitched for no reason, and presently pointed at nothing a sure sign of frenzy; and next of his eye, which rolled round and was vicious.</content>
<content>"At this, Mother Sub-Prioress, long weary of promenading, yet determined not to be left behind while others followed on, exclaimed that if the eye of the creature were vicious, then must Sister Mary Seraphine straightway dismount, and the brute be led back to the seat where the Prioress sat watching.</content>
<content>"To this Seraphine gladly agreed, and a greatly sobered procession returned to the top of the field.</content>
<content>"But gaiety was quickly restored by the old lay-sister, Mary Antony, who, armed with the Reverend Mother's permission, insisted on mounting.</content>
<content>"Willing hands, miscalculating the exceeding lightness of her aged body, lifted her higher than need be, above the back of the palfrey.</content>
<content>Whereupon Mary Antony, parting her feet, came down straddling!</content>
<content>"Firm as a limpet, she sat thus upon Icon.</content>
<content>No efforts of the nuns could induce her to shift her position.</content>
<content>Commanding Brother Philip, seeing 'the Lord Bishop' was now safely mounted, to lead on and not keep him standing, old Antony rode off in triumph, blessing the nuns right and left, as she passed.</content>
<content>"Never were heard such shrieks of merriment!</content>
<content>Even Mother Sub-Prioress sank upon a seat to laugh with less fatigue.</content>
<content>Sister Seraphine's fretful complaints were forgotten.</content>
<content>"Twice round the field went old Antony, with fingers uplifted.</content>
<content>Icon stepped carefully, arching his neck and walking as if he well knew that he bore on his back, ninety odd years of brave gaiety.</content>
<content>"Well, that made of the Play Day a success.</content>
<content>But the best of all was yet to come."</content>
<content>The Bishop took up the faggot-fork, and again tended the fire.</content>
<content>He seemed to find it difficult to tell that which must next be told.</content>
<content>The Knight was breathing quickly.</content>
<content>He sat immovable; yet the rubies on his breast glittered continuously, like so many eager, fiery eyes.</content>
<content>The Bishop went on, speaking rapidly, the faggot-fork still in his hand, his face turned to the fire.</content>
<content>"They had lifted Mary Antony down, and were crowding round Icon, patting and praising him, when a message came from the Reverend Mother, bidding Brother Philip to bring the palfrey into the courtyard; the nuns to remain in the field.</content>
<content>"They watched the beautiful creature pace through the archway and disappear, and none knew quite what would happen next.</content>
<content>Philip heard them discussing it later.</content>
<content>"Some thought the Bishop had sent for his palfrey.</content>
<content>Others, that the Reverend Mother had feared for the safety of the old lay-sister; or, lest her brave example should fire the rest to be too venturesome.</content>
<content>Yet all eyes were turned toward the archway, vaguely expectant.</content>
<content>"And then 

"They heard the hoofs of Icon ring on the flagstones of the courtyard.</content>
<content>"They heard the calm voice of the Prioress.</content>
<content>Could it be she who was coming?</content>
<content>"Out from the archway, into the sunshine, alone and fearless; the Prioress rode upon Icon.</content>
<content>On her face was the light of a purposeful radiance.</content>
<content>The palfrey stepped as if proud of the burden he carried.</content>
<content>"She smiled and would have cried out gaily to the groups as she passed.</content>
<content>But, with one accord, the nuns dropped to their knees, with clasped hands, and faces uplifted, adoring.</content>
<content>Always they loved her, revered her, and thought her beautiful.</content>
<content>But this vision of the Prioress, whom none had ever seen mounted, riding forth into the sunshine on the snow-white palfrey, filled their hearts with praise and with wonder.</content>
<content>"Brother Philip leaned against the archway, watching.</content>
<content>He knew his hand upon the bridle was no longer needed, from the moment when he saw the Reverend Mother gather up the reins in her left hand, lay her right gently on the neck of Icon, and, bending, speak low in his ear.</content>
<content>"She sat a horse said Philip as only they can sit, who have ridden from childhood.</content>
<content>"She walked him round the meadow once, then gently shook the reins, and he broke into a trot.</content>
<content>"The watching nuns, now on their feet again, shrieked aloud, with fright and glee.</content>
<content>"At the extreme end of the meadow, wheeling sharply, she let him out into a canter.</content>
<content>"The nuns at this were petrified into dumbness.</content>
<content>One and all held their breath; while Mother Sub-Prioress nobody quite knew why turned upon Sister Mary Seraphine, and shook her.</content>
<content>"And the next moment the Prioress was among them, walking the palfrey slowly, settling her veil, which had streamed behind her as she cantered, bending to speak to one and another, as she passed.</content>
<content>"And the light of new life was in her eyes.</content>
<content>Her cheeks glowed, she seemed a girl again.</content>

<content>"She paused and said: 'None need remain here longer than they will.</content>
<content>But, being up and mounted, and our Lord Bishop in no haste for the return of his palfrey, it is my intention to ride for an hour.'"</content>

<content>"For a full hour, in the sunshine, on the soft turf of the river meadow, THE PRIORESS TRIED HER WINGS."</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXIII

THE MIDNIGHT ARRIVAL

<content>CHAPTER XXIV

THE POPE'S MANDATE

The Bishop and Hugh d'Argent were once more alone.</content>
<content>It was characteristic of both that they sat for some minutes in unbroken silence.</content>
<content>Then the Bishop put out his hand, took up the packet from Rome, and looked at the Knight.</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent rose, walked over to the casement, and leaned out into the still, summer night.</content>
<content>He could hear the Bishop breaking the seals of the Pope's letter.</content>
<content>Below in the courtyard, all was quiet.</content>
<content>The great gates were barred.</content>
<content>He wondered whether the steaming horse had been well rubbed down, clothed, and given a warm mash mixed with ale.</content>
<content>He could hear the Bishop unfolding the parchment, which crackled.</content>
<content>The moon, in her first quarter, rode high in the heavens.</content>
<content>The towers of St. Mary's church looked black against the sky.</content>
<content>The Palace stood on the same side of the Cathedral as the main street of the city, in the direct route to the Foregate, the Tithing, and the White Ladies' Nunnery at Whytstone.</content>
<content>How strange to remember, that beneath him lay that mile-long walk in darkness; that just under the Palace, so near the Cathedral, she and he, pacing together, had known the end of their strange pilgrimage to be at hand.</content>
<content>Yet then 

He could hear the Bishop turning the parchment.</content>
<content>How freely the silvery moon sailed in this stormy sky, like a noble face looking calmly out, and ever out again, from amid perplexities and doubts.</content>
<content>In two nights' time, the moon would be well-nigh full.</content>
<content>Would he be riding to Warwick alone, or would she be beside him?</content>
<content>As the Bishop had said, he had described her as riding all day, like a bird, on the moors.</content>
<content>Yet now he loved best to picture her riding forth upon Icon into the river meadow, her veil streaming behind her; "on her face the light of a purposeful radiance."</content>
<content>Ah, would she come?</content>
<content>Would she come, or would she stay?</content>
<content>Would she stay, or would she come?</content>
<content>The moon was now hidden by a cloud; but he could see the edge of the cloud silvering.</content>
<content>If the moon sailed forth free, before he had counted to twelve, she would come.</content>
<content>He began to count, slowly.</content>
<content>At nine, the moon was still hidden; and the Knight's heart failed him.</content>
<content>But at ten, the Bishop called: "Hugh!"</content>
<content>and turning from the casement the Knight answered to the call.</content>
<content>The Bishop held in his hands the Pope's letter, and also a legal-looking document, from which seals depended.</content>
<content>"This doth closely concern you, my son," said the Bishop, with some emotion, and placed the parchment in the Knight's hands.</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent could have mastered its contents by the light of the wax taper burning beside the Bishop's chair.</content>
<content>But some instinct he could not have explained, caused him to carry it over to the table in the centre of the hall, whereon four wax candles still burned.</content>
<content>He stood to read the document, with his back to the Bishop, his head bent close to the flame of the candles.</content>
<content>Once, twice, thrice, the Knight read it, before his bewildered brain took in its full import.</content>
<content>Yet it was clear and unmistakable a dispensation, signed and sealed by the Pope, releasing Mora, Countess of Norelle, from all vows and promises taken and made when she entered the Nunnery of the White Ladies of Worcester, at Whytstone, in the parish of dairies, and later on when she became Prioress of that same Nunnery; and furthermore stating that this full absolution was granted because it had been brought to the knowledge of His Holiness that this noble lady had entered the cloistered life owing to a wicked and malicious plot designed to wrest her castle and estates from her, and also to part her from a valiant Knight, at that time fighting in the Holy Wars, to whom she was betrothed.</content>
<content>Furthermore the deed empowered Symon, Bishop of Worcester or any priest he might appoint, to unite in marriage the Knight Crusader, Hugh d'Argent, and Mora de Norelle, sometime Prioress of the White Ladies of Worcester.</content>
<content>The Knight walked back to the hearth and stood before the Bishop, the parchment in his hand.</content>
<content>"My Lord Bishop," he said, "do I dream?"</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester smiled.</content>
<content>"Nay, my son.</content>
<content>Surely no dream of thine was ever signed by His Holiness, nor bore suspended from it the great seal of the Vatican!</content>
<content>The document you hold will be sufficient answer to all questions, and will ensure your wife's position at Court and her standing in the outer world should she elect to re-enter it.</content>
<content>"But whether she shall do this, or no, is not a matter upon which the Church would give a decisive or even an authoritative pronouncement; and the Holy Father adds, in, his letter to me, further important instructions.</content>
<content>"Firstly: that it must be the Prioress's own wish and decision, apart from any undue pressure from without, to resign her office and to accept this dispensation, freeing her from her vows.</content>
<content>"Secondly; that she must leave the Nunnery and the neighbourhood, secretly; if it be possible, appearing in her new position, as your wife, without much question being raised as to whence she came.</content>
<content>"Thirdly: that when her absence becomes known in the Nunnery, I am authorized solemnly to announce that she has been moved on by me, secretly, with the knowledge and approval of the Holy Father, to a place where she was required for higher service."</content>
<content>The Bishop smiled as he pronounced the final words.</content>
<content>There was triumph in his eye.</content>
<content>The Knight still looked as if he felt himself to be dreaming; yet on his face was a great gladness of expectation.</content>
<content>"And, my lord," he exclaimed joyously, "what news for her!</content>
<content>Shall you send it, in the morn, or yourself take it to her?"</content>
<content>The Bishop's lips were pressed against his finger-tips.</content>
<content>"I know not," he answered, slowly; "I know not that I shall either take or send it."</content>
<content>"But, my lord, surely!</content>
<content>It will settle all doubts, solve all questions, remove all difficulties "

"Tut!</content>
<content>Tut!</content>
<content>Tut!"</content>
<content>exclaimed the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Good heavens, man!</content>
<content>Dare I wed you to a woman you know so little?</content>
<content>Not for one instant, into her consideration of the matter, will have entered any question as to what Church or State might say or do.</content>
<content>For her the question stands upon simpler, truer, lines, not involved by rule or dogma: 'Is it right for me, or wrong for me?</content>
<content>Is it the will of God that I should do this thing?'"</content>
<content>"But if you tell her, my lord, of the Holy Father's dispensation and permission; what will she then say?"</content>
<content>"What will she then say?"</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester softly laughed, as at something which stirred an exceeding tender memory.</content>
<content>"She will probably say: 'You amaze me, my lord!</content>
<content>Indeed, my lord, you amaze me!</content>
<content>His Holiness the Pope may rule at Rome; you, my Lord Bishop, rule in the cities of this diocese; but I rule in this Nunnery, and while I rule here, such a thing as this shall never be!'"</content>
<content>The Bishop gently passed his hands the one over the other, as was his habit when a recollection gave him keen mental pleasure.</content>
<content>"That is what the Prioress would probably say, my dear Knight, were I so foolish as to flaunt before her this most priceless parchment.</content>
<content>And yet I know not.</content>
<content>It may be wise to send it, or to show it without much comment, simply in order that she may see the effect upon the mind of the Holy Father himself, of a full knowledge of the complete facts of the case."</content>
<content>"My lord," said the Knight, with much earnestness, "how came that full knowledge to His Holiness in Rome?"</content>
<content>"When first you came to me," replied the Bishop, "with this grievous tale of wrong and treachery, I knew that if you won your way with Mora, we must be armed with highest authority for the marriage and for her return to the world, or sorrow and much trial for her might follow, with, perhaps, danger for you.</content>
<content>Therefore I resolved forthwith to lay the whole matter, without loss of time, before the Pope himself.</content>
<content>I know the Holy Father well; his openness of mind, his charity and kindliness; his firm desire to do justly, and to love mercy.</content>
<content>Moreover, his friendship for me is such, that he would not lightly refuse me a request.</content>
<content>Also he would, of his kindness, incline to be guided by my judgment.</content>
<content>"Wherefore, no sooner were all the facts in my possession, those you told me, those I already knew, and those I did for myself deduce from both, than I sent for young Roger de Berchelai, whose wits and devotion I could safely trust, gave him all he would need for board and lodging, boats and steeds, that he might accomplish the journey in the shortest possible time, and despatched him to Rome with a written account of the whole matter, under my private seal, to His Holiness the Pope."</content>
<content>The Knight stood during this recital, his eyes fixed in searching question upon the Bishop's face.</content>
<content>Then: "My lord," he said, 'such kindness on your part, passes all understanding.</content>
<content>That you should have borne with me while I told my tale, was much.</content>
<content>That you should tacitly have allowed me the chance to have speech with my betrothed, was more.</content>
<content>But that, all this time, while I was giving you half-confidence, and she no confidence at all, you should have been working, spending, planning for us, risking much if the Holy Father had taken your largeness of heart and breadth of mind amiss!</content>
<content>All this, you did, for Mora and for me!</content>
<content>That you were, as you tell me, a frequent guest in my childhood's home, holding my parents in warm esteem, might account for the exceeding kindness of the welcome you did give me.</content>
<content>But this generosity this wondrous goodness I stand amazed, confounded!</content>
<content>That you should do so great a thing to make it possible that I should wed the Prioress It passes understanding!"</content>
<content>When Hugh d'Argent ceased speaking, Symon of Worcester did not immediately make reply.</content>
<content>He sat looking into the fire, fingering, with his left hand, the gold cross at his breast, and drumming, with the fingers of his right, upon the carved lion's head which formed the arm of his chair.</content>
<content>It seemed as if the Bishop had, of a sudden, grown restive under the Knight's gratitude; or as if some train of thought had awakened within him, to which he did not choose to give expression, and which must be beaten back before he allowed himself to speak.</content>
<content>At length, folding his hands, he made answer to the Knight, still looking into the fire, a certain air of detachment wrapping him round, as with an invisible yet impenetrable shield.</content>
<content>"You overwhelm me, my dear Hugh, with your gratitude.</content>
<content>It had not seemed to me that my action in this matter would demand either thanks or explanation.</content>
<content>There are occasions when to do less than our best, would be to sin against all that which we hold most sacred.</content>
<content>To my mind, the most useful definition of sin, in the sacred writings, is that of the apostle Saint James, most practical of all the inspired writers, when he said: 'To him that knoweth to do good, and doeth it not, to him it is sin.'</content>
<content>I knew quite clearly the 'good' to be done in this case.</content>
<content>Therefore no gratitude is due to me for failing to fall into the sin of omission.</content>
<content>"Also, my son, many who seem to deserve the gratitude of others, would be found not to deserve it, if the entire inward truth of motive could be fully revealed.</content>
<content>"With me it is well-nigh a passion that all good things should attain unto full completeness.</content>
<content>"It may be I was better able to give full understanding to your tale because, for love of a woman, I dwelt seven years in exile from this land, fearing lest my great love for her, which came to me all unsought, should by becoming known to her lead her young heart, as yet fresh and unawakened, to respond.</content>
<content>There was never any question of breaking my vows; and I hold not with love-friendships between man and woman, there where marriage is not possible.</content>
<content>They are, at best, selfish on the part of the man.</content>
<content>They keep the woman from entering into her kingdom.</content>
<content>The crown of womanhood is to bear children to the man she loves to take her place in his home, as wife and mother.</content>
<content>The man who cannot offer this, yet stands in the way of the man who can, is a poor and an unworthy lover."</content>
<content>The Bishop paused, unclasped his hands, withdrew his steadfast regard from the fire, and sat back in his chair.</content>
<content>The stone in his ring gleamed blue, the colour of forget-me-nots beside a meadow brook.</content>
<content>Presently he looked at the silent Knight.</content>
<content>There was a kindly smile, in his eyes, rather than upon his lips.</content>
<content>"It may be, my dear Hugh, that this heart discipline of mine of which, by the way, I have never before spoken has made me quick to understand the sufferings of other men.</content>
<content>Also it may explain the great desire I always experience to see a truly noble woman come to the full completion of her womanhood.</content>
<content>"I returned to England not long after your betrothed had entered the cloistered life in the Whytstone Nunnery.</content>
<content>I was appointed to this See of Worcester, which appointment gave me the spiritual control of the White Ladies.</content>
<content>My friendship with the Prioress has been a source of interest, pleasure, and true helpfulness to myself and I trust to her also.</content>
<content>I think I told you while we supped that, many years ago, I had known her at the Court when I was confessor to the Queen, and preceptor to her ladies.</content>
<content>But no mention has ever been made between the Prioress and myself of any previous acquaintance.</content>
<content>I doubt whether she recognised, in the frail, white-haired, old prelate who arrived from Italy, the vigorous, bearded priest known to her, in her girlhood's days, as" the Bishop paused and looked steadily at the Knight "as Father Gervaise."</content>
<content>"Father Gervaise!"</content>
<content>exclaimed Hugh d'Argent, lifting his hand to cross himself as he named the Dead, yet arrested in this instinctive movement by something in those keen blue eyes.</content>
<content>"Father Gervaise, my lord, perished in a stormy sea.</content>
<content>The ship foundered, and none who sailed in her were seen again."</content>
<content>The Knight spoke with conviction; yet, even as he spoke, the amazing truth rushed in upon him, and struck him dumb.</content>
<content>Of a sudden he knew why the Bishop's eyes had instantly won his fearless confidence.</content>
<content>A trusted friend of his childhood had looked out at him from their dear depths.</content>
<content>Often he had searched his memory, since the Bishop had claimed knowledge of him in his boyhood, and had marvelled that no recollection of Symon as a guest in his parents' home came back to him.</content>
<content>Now in this moment of revelation how clearly he could see the figure of the famous priest, in brown habit, cloak, and hood, a cord at his waist, with tonsured head, full brown beard, and sandalled feet, pacing the great hall, standing in the armoury, or climbing the Cumberland hills to visit the chapel of the Holy Mount and the hermit who dwelt beside it.</content>
<content>As is the way with childhood's memories, the smallest, most trivial details leapt up vivid, crystal clear.</content>
<content>The present was forgotten, the future disregarded, in the sudden intimate dearness of that long-ago past.</content>
<content>The Bishop allowed time for this realisation.</content>
<content>Then he spoke.</content>
<content>"True, the ship foundered, Hugh; true, none who sailed in her were seen again.</content>
<content>And, if I tell you that one swimmer, after long buffeting, was flung up on a rocky coast, lay for many weeks sick unto death in a fisherman's humble cot, rose at last the frail shadow of his former self, to find that his hair had turned white in that desperate night, to find that none knew his name nor his estate, that leaving Father Gervaise and his failures at the bottom of the ocean he could shave his beard, and make his way to Rome under any name he pleased; if I tell you all this, I trust you with a secret, Hugh, known to one other only, during all these years His Holiness, the Pope."</content>
<content>"Father!"</content>
<content>exclaimed the Knight, with deep emotion; "Father" Then, his voice broke.</content>
<content>He dropped on one knee in front of the Bishop, and clasped the bands stretched out to him.</content>
<content>What strange thing had happened?</content>
<content>One, greatly loved and long mourned, had risen from the dead; yet she who had best loved and most mourned him, had herself passed to the Realm of Shadows, and was not here to wonder and to rejoice.</content>
<content>"Father," said Hugh, when he could trust his voice, "in her last words to me, my mother spoke of you.</content>
<content>I went to her chamber to bid her sleep well, and together we knelt before the crucifix.</content>
<content>'Let us repeat,' whispered my mother, 'those holy words of comfort which Father Gervaise ever bid his penitents to say, as they kneeled before the dying Redeemer.'</content>
<content>'Mother,' said I, 'I know them not.'</content>
<content>'Thou wert so young, my son,' she said, 'when Father Gervaise last was with us.'</content>
<content>'Tell me the words,' I said; 'I should like well to have them from thy lips.'</content>
<content>So, lifting her eyes to the dead Christ, my mother said, with awe and reverence in her voice and a deep gladness on her face: 'He ever liveth to make intercession for us.'</content>
<content>And, in the dawn of the new day, her spirit passed."</content>
<content>The Bishop laid his hand upon the Knight's bowed head.</content>
<content>"My son," he said, "of all the women I have known, thy gentle mother bore the most beautiful and saintly character.</content>
<content>I would there were more such as she, in our British homes."</content>
<content>"Father," said Hugh, brokenly, "knew you how much she had to bear?</content>
<content>My father's fierce feuds with all, shut her up at last to utter loneliness.</content>
<content>His anger against Holy Church and his contempt of Her priests, cost my mother the comfort of your visits.</content>
<content>His life-long quarrel with Earl Eustace de Norelle caused that our families, though dwelling within a three hours' ride, were allowed no intercourse.</content>
<content>Never did I enter Castle Norelle until I rode up from the South, with a message for Mora from the King.</content>
<content>And, to this day, Mora has never been within the courtyard of my home!</content>
<content>When we were betrothed, I dared not tell my parents though Earl Eustace and his Countess both were dead lest my father's wrath might reach Mora, when I had gone.</content>
<content>News of his death, chancing to me in a far-off land, brought me home.</content>
<content>And truly, it was home indeed, at last!</content>
<content>Peace and content, where always there had been turbulence and strain.</content>
<content>Father, I tell you this because I know my gentle mother feared you did not understand, and that you may have thought her love for you had failed."</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester smiled.</content>
<content>"Dear lad," he said, "I understood."</content>
<content>"Ah why," cried Hugh, with sudden passion, "why should a woman's whole life be spoiled, and other lives be darkened and made sad, just by the angry, churlish, sullen whims of "

"Hush, boy!"</content>
<content>said the Bishop, quickly.</content>
<content>"You speak of your father, and you name the Dead.</content>
<content>Something dies in the Living, each time they speak evil of the Dead.</content>
<content>I knew your father; and, though he loved me not, yet, to be honest, I must say this of him: Sir Hugo was a good man and true; upright, and a man of honour.</content>
<content>He carried his shield untarnished.</content>
<content>If he was feared by his friends, he was also feared by his foes.</content>
<content>Brave he was and fearless.</content>
<content>One thing he lacked; and often, alas, they who lack just one thing, lack all.</content>
<content>"Hugo d'Argent knew not love for his fellow-men.</content>
<content>To be a man, was to earn his frown; all things human called forth his disdain.</content>
<content>To view the same landscape, breathe the same air, in fact walk the same earth as he, was to stand in his way, and raise his ire.</content>
<content>Yet in his harsh, vexed manner he loved his wife, and loved his little son.</content>
<content>Nor had he any self-conceit.</content>
<content>He realised in himself his own worst foe.</content>
<content>Lest we fall into this snare, it is well daily to pray: 'O Lover of Mankind, grant unto me truly to love my fellow-men; to honour them, until they prove worthless; to trust them, until they prove faithless; and ever to expect better of them, than I expect of myself; to think better of them, than I think of myself.'</content>
<content>Let us go through life, my son, searching for good in others, not for evil; we may miss the good, if we search not for it; the evil, alas, will find us, quite soon enough, unsought."</content>
<content>Suddenly Hugh lifted his head.</content>
<content>"Father," he said, "the starling!</content>
<content>Mind you the starling with the broken wing, which you and I found in the woods and carried home; and you did set his wing, and tamed him, and taught him to say 'Hugh'?</content>
<content>Each time I brought him food, you said: 'Hugh!</content>
<content>Hugh!'</content>
<content>And soon the starling, seeing me coming, also said: 'Hugh!</content>
<content>Hugh!'</content>
<content>Do you remember, Father?"</content>
<content>"I do remember," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"I see thee now, coming across the courtyard, bread and meat in thy hands a little lad, bareheaded in the sunshine, glowing with pleasure because the starling ran to meet thee, shouting 'Hugh!'"</content>
<content>"Then listen, dear Father.</content>
<content>Ah, how often have I wished to tell you this!</content>
<content>Soon after you were gone, that starling rudely taught me a hard lesson.</content>
<content>Gaining strength, one day he left the courtyard, ran through the buttery, and wandered in the garden.</content>
<content>I followed, whistling and watching.</content>
<content>It greatly delighted the bird to find himself on turf.</content>
<content>There had been rain.</content>
<content>The grass was wet.</content>
<content>Presently a rash worm, gliding from its hole, adventured forth.</content>
<content>The starling ran to the worm, calling it 'Hugh.'</content>
<content>'Hugh!</content>
<content>Hugh!'</content>
<content>he cried, and tugged it from the earth.</content>
<content>'Hugh!</content>
<content>Hugh!'</content>
<content>and pecked it, where helpless it lay squirming.</content>
<content>Then, shouting 'Hugh!'</content>
<content>once more, gobbled it down.</content>
<content>I stood with heavy heart, for I had thought that starling loved me with a true, personal love, when he ran at my approach shouting my name.</content>
<content>Yet now I knew it was the food I carried, he called 'Hugh'; it was the food, not me, he loved.</content>
<content>Glad was I when, his wing grown strong, he flew away.</content>
<content>It cut me to the heart to hear the worms, the grubs, the snails, the caterpillars, all called 'Hugh'!"</content>
<content>The Bishop smiled, then sighed.</content>
<content>"Poor little eager heart," he said, "learning so hard a lesson, all alone!</content>
<content>Yet is it a lesson, lad, sooner or later learned in sadness by all generous hearts.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>And now, leaving the past, with all its memories, let us return to the present, and face the uncertain future.</content>
<content>Also, dear Knight, I must ask you to remember, even when we are alone, that your old friend, Father Gervaise, in his brown habit, lies at the bottom of the ocean; yet that your new friend, Symon of Worcester, holds you and your interests very near his heart."</content>
<content>The Bishop put out his hand.</content>
<content>Hugh seized and kissed it, knowing this was his farewell to Father
Gervaise.</content>
<content>Then he rose to his feet.</content>
<content>The Bishop said nothing; but an indefinable change came over him.</content>
<content>Again he extended his hand.</content>
<content>The Knight kneeled, and kissed the Bishop's ring.</content>
<content>"I thank you, my lord," he said, "for your great trust in me.</content>
<content>I will not prove unworthy."</content>
<content>With this he went back to his seat.</content>
<content>The Bishop, lifting the faggot-fork, carefully stirred and built up the logs.</content>
<content>"What were we saying, my dear Knight, when we strayed into a side issue?</content>
<content>Ah, I remember!</content>
<content>I was telling you of my appointment to the See of Worcester, and my belief that the Prioress failed to recognise in me, one she had known long years before."</content>
<content>The Bishop put by the faggot-fork and turned from the fire.</content>
<content>"I found the promise of that radiant girlhood more than fulfilled.</content>
<content>She was changed; she shewed obvious signs of having passed through the furnace; but pure gold can stand the fire.</content>
<content>The strength of purpose, the noble outlook upon life, the gracious tenderness for others, had matured and developed.</content>
<content>Even the necessary restrictions of monastic life could not modify the grand lines both mental, and physical on which Nature had moulded her.</content>
<content>"I endeavoured to think no thoughts concerning her, other than should be thought of a holy lady who has taken vows of celibacy.</content>
<content>Yet, seeing her so fitted to have made house home for a man, helping him upward, and to have been the mother of a fine race of sons and daughters, I felt it grievous that in leaving the world for a reason which in no sense could be considered a true vocation, she should have cut herself off from such powers and possibilities.</content>
<content>"So passed the years in the calm service of God and of the Church; yet always I seemed aware that a crisis would come, and that, when that crisis came, she would need me."</content>
<content>The Bishop paused and looked at the Knight.</content>
<content>Hugh's face was in shadow; but, as the Bishop looked at him, the rubies on his breast glittered in the firelight, as if some sudden thought had set him strongly quivering.</content>
<content>At sight of which, a flash of firm resolve, like the swift drawing of a sword, broke o'er the Bishop's calmness.</content>
<content>It was quick and powerful; it seemed to divide asunder soul and spirit, joints and marrow, and to discern the thoughts and intents of the heart.</content>
<content>And before that two-edged blade could sheathe itself again, swiftly the Bishop spoke.</content>
<content>"Therefore, my dear Hugh, when you arrived with your tale of wrong and treachery, all unconsciously to yourself, every word you spoke of your betrothed revealed her to the man who had loved her while you were yet a youth, with your spurs to win, and all life before you.</content>
<content>"I saw in your arrival, and in the strange tale you told, a wondrous chance for her of that fuller development of life for which I knew her to be so perfectly fitted.</content>
<content>"It had seemed indeed the irony of fate that, while I had fled and dwelt in exile lest my presence should hold her back from marriage, the treachery of others should have driven her into a life of celibacy.</content>
<content>"Therefore while, with my tacit consent, you went to work in your own way, I sent my messenger to Rome bearing to the Holy Father a full account of all, petitioning a dispensation from vows taken owing to deception, and asking leave to unite in the holy sacrament of marriage these long-sundered lovers, undertaking that no scandal should arise therefrom, either in the Nunnery or in the City of Worcester.</content>
<content>"As you have seen, my messenger this night returned; and we now find ourselves armed with the full sanction of His Holiness, providing the Prioress, of her own free will, desires to renounce the high position she has won in her holy calling, and to come to you."</content>
<content>The quiet voice ceased speaking.</content>
<content>The Knight rose slowly to his feet.</content>
<content>At first he stood silent.</content>
<content>Then he spoke with a calm dignity which proved him worthy of the Bishop's trust.</content>
<content>"I greatly honour you, my lord," he said; "and were our ages and conditions other than they are, so that we might fight for the woman we love, I should be proud to cross swords with you."</content>
<content>The Bishop sat looking into the fire.</content>
<content>A faint smile flickered at the corners of the sensitive mouth.</content>
<content>The fights he had fought for the woman he loved had been of sterner quality than the mere crossing of knightly swords.</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent spoke again.</content>
<content>"Profoundly do I thank you, Reverend Father, for all that you have done; and even more, for that which you did not do.</content>
<content>It was six years after her first sojourn at the Court that I met Mora, loved her, and won her; and well I know that the sweet love she gave to me was a love from which no man had brushed the bloom."</content>
<content>Hugh paused.</content>
<content>Those kindly and very luminous eyes were still bent upon the fire.</content>
<content>Was the Bishop finding it hard to face the fact that his life's secret had now, by his own act, passed into the keeping of another?</content>
<content>Hugh moved a pace nearer.</content>
<content>"And deeply do I love you, Reverend Father, for your wondrous goodness to her, and for her sake to me.</content>
<content>And I pray heaven," added Hugh d'Argent simply, "that if she come to me, she may never know that she once won the love of so greatly better a man than he who won hers."</content>
<content>With which the Knight dropped upon one knee, and humbly kissed the hem of the Bishop's robe.</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester was greatly moved.</content>
<content>"My son," he said, "we are at one in desiring her happiness and highest good.</content>
<content>For the rest, God, and her own pure heart, must guide her feet into the way of peace."</content>
<content>The Bishop rose, and went to the casement.</content>
<content>"The aurora breaks in the east.</content>
<content>The dawn is near.</content>
<content>Come with me, Hugh, to the chapel.</content>
<content>We pray for His Holiness, giving thanks for his gracious letter and mandate; we praise for the safe return of my messenger.</content>
<content>But we will also offer up devout petition that the Prioress may have clear light at this parting of the ways, and that our enterprise may be brought to a happy conclusion."</content>
<content>So, presently, in the dimly-lighted chapel, the Knight knelt alone; while, away at the high altar, remote, wrapt, absorbed in the supreme act of his priestly office, stood the Bishop, celebrating mass.</content>
<content>Yet one anxious prayer ascended from the hearts of both.</content>
<content>And, in the pale dawn of that new day, the woman for whom both the Knight and the Bishop prayed, kept vigil in her cell, before the shrine of the Madonna.</content>
<content>"Blessed Virgin," she said; "thou who lovedst Saint Joseph, being betrothed to him, yet didst keep thyself an holy shrine consecrate to the Lord and His need of thee oh, grant unto me strength to put from me this constant torment at the thought of his sufferings to whom once I gave my troth, and to reconsecrate myself wholly to the service of my Lord."</content>
<content>Thus these three knelt, as a new day dawned.</content>
<content>And the Knight prayed: "Give her to me!"</content>
<content>And the Bishop prayed: "Guide her feet into the way of peace."</content>
<content>And the Prioress, with hands crossed upon her breast and eyes uplifted, said: "Cause me to know the way wherein I should walk; for I lift up my soul unto Thee."</content>
<content>The silver streaks of the aurora paled before soaring shafts of gold, bright heralds of the rising sun.</content>
<content>Then from the Convent garden trilled softly the first notes, poignant but passing sweet, of the robin's song.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXV

MARY ANTONY RECEIVES THE BISHOP

The morning after the return from Rome of the Bishop's messenger, the old lay-sister, Mary Antony, chanced to be crossing the Convent courtyard, when there came a loud knocking on the outer gates.</content>
<content>Mary Antony, hastening, thrust aside the buxom porteress, and herself opened the guichet, and looked out.</content>
<content>The Lord Bishop, mounted upon his white palfrey, waited without;
Brother Philip in attendance.</content>
<content>What a bewildering surprise!</content>
<content>What a fortunate thing, thought old Antony, that she should chance to be there to deal with such an emergency.</content>
<content>Never did the Bishop visit the Nunnery, without sending a messenger beforehand to know whether the Prioress could see him, stating the exact hour of his proposed arrival; so that, when the great doors were flung wide and the Bishop rode into the courtyard, the Prioress would be standing at the top of the steps to receive him; Mother Sub-Prioress in attendance in the background; the other holy ladies upon their knees within the entrance; Mary Antony, well out of sight, yet where peeping was possible, because she loved to see the Reverend Mother kneel and kiss the Bishop's ring, rising to her feet again without pause, making of the whole movement one graceful, deep obeisance.</content>
<content>After which, Mary Antony, still peeping, greatly loved to see the Prioress mount the wide, stone staircase with the Bishop; each shewing a courtly deference to the other.</content>
<content>One of Mary Antony's most exalted dreams of heaven, was of a place where she should sit upon a jasper seat and see the Reverend Mother and the great Lord Bishop mounting together interminable flights of golden stairs; while Mother Sub-Prioress and Sister Mary Rebecca looked through black bars, somewhere down below, whence they would have a good view of Mary Antony on her jasper seat, but no glimpse of the golden stairs or of the radiant figures which she watched ascending.</content>
<content>So much for the usual visits of the Bishop, when everything was in readiness for his reception.</content>
<content>But now, all unexpected, the Bishop waited without the gate, and Mary
Antony had to deal with this emergency.</content>
<content>Crying to the porteress to open wide, she hastened to the steps.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>It was impossible to summon the Reverend Mother in time.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>The
Lord Bishop must not be kept waiting!</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Even now the great doors
were rolling back.</content>
<content>Mary Antony mounted the six steps; then turned in the doorway.</content>
<content>The Lord Bishop must be received.</content>
<content>There was nobody else to do it.</content>
<content>She would receive the Lord Bishop!</content>
<content>As she saw him riding in upon Icon, blessing the porteress as he passed, she remembered how she had ridden round the river meadow as the Bishop.</content>
<content>Now she must play her part as the Prioress.</content>
<content>So it came to pass that, as he rode up to the door and dismounted, flinging his rein to Brother Philip, the Bishop found himself confronted by the queer little figure of the aged lay-sister, drawn up to its full height and obviously upheld by a sense of importance and dignity.</content>
<content>As the Bishop reached the entrance, she knelt and kissed his ring; then tried to rise quickly, failed, and clutching at his hand, exclaimed: "Devil take my old knee-joints!"</content>
<content>Never before had the Bishop been received with such a formula!</content>
