<content>Aye, even before Symon had flung the stone; when, in reply to the doubt cast by him on our Lady's smile, the Knight had said: "I keep my trust in prayer," a joyous confidence had then and there awakened within him.</content>
<content>He had stretched out the right hand of his withered faith, and lo, it had proved strong and vital.</content>
<content>Yet as, in the heavy silence of the crypt, he heard the turning of the key in the lock, his heart stood still, and every emotion hung suspended, as the first veiled figure shadowy and ghostlike moved into view.</content>
<content>It was not she.</content>
<content>The Knight's pulses throbbed again.</content>
<content>His heart pounded violently as, keeping their measured distances, nine, ten, eleven, white figures passed.</content>
<content>Then twelfth: a tall nun, almost her height; yet not she.</content>
<content>Then thirteenth: Oh, blessed Virgin!</content>
<content>Oh, saints of God!</content>
<content>Mora!</content>
<content>She, herself.</content>
<content>Never could he fail to recognize her carriage, the regal poise of her head.</content>
<content>However veiled, however shrouded, he could not be mistaken.</content>
<content>It was Mora; and that she should be walking in this central position meant that she might with comparative safety, step aside.</content>
<content>Yet, even this 

But, at that moment, passing him, she turned her head, and for an instant her eyes met the eyes of the Knight looking out from the shadows.</content>
<content>Another moment and she had vanished up the winding stairway in the wall.</content>
<content>But that instant was enough.</content>
<content>As her eyes met his, Hugh d'Argent knew that his betrothed was once more his own.</content>
<content>His heart ceased pounding; his pulses beat steadily.</content>
<content>The calm of a vast, glad certainty enfolded him; a joy beyond belief.</content>
<content>Yet he knew now that he had been sure of it, ever since he came up from the depths of the Severn into the summer sunshine, grasping the white stone.</content>
<content>"I keep my trust in prayer.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Give her to me!</content>
<content>Give her to me!</content>
<content>Blessed Virgin, give her to me!</content>
<content>'A sculptured smile'?</content>
<content>Nay, my lord.</content>
<content>I keep my trust in prayer!"</content>
<content>The solemn chanting of the monks, stole down from the distant choir.</content>
<content>Vespers had begun.</content>
<content>The Knight strode to the altar, and knelt for some minutes, his hands clasped upon the crossed hilt of his sword.</content>
<content>Then he rose, and spoke in low tones to his men-at-arms.</content>
<content>"When a thrush calls, you will leave the crypt, and guard the entrance from without; allowing none, on any pretext, to pass within.</content>
<content>When a blackbird whistles you will return, lift the stretcher, and pass with it, as heretofore, from the Cathedral to the hostel."</content>
<content>Next the Knight, returning to the altar, bent over the bandaged man upon the stretcher.</content>
<content>"Martin," he said, speaking very low, so that his trusted foster-brother alone could hear him.</content>

<content>Our pilgrimage is about to end, as we have hoped, in a great recovery and restoration.</content>
<content>When the call of a curlew sounds, leap from the stretcher, leave the bandages beside it; go to the entrance, guarding it from within; but turn not thy head this way, until a blackbird whistles; upon which lose thyself among the pillars, letting no man see thee, until we have passed out.</content>
<content>After which, make thy way out, as best thou canst, and join me at the hostel, entering by the garden and window, without letting thyself be seen in the courtyard."</content>
<content>The keen eyes below the bandage, smiled assent.</content>
<content>Stooping, the Knight lifted the cloak, fastened it to his left shoulder, and drew it around him, holding the greater part of it in many folds in his right hand.</content>
<content>Then he moved back into the shadow of the pillar.</content>
<content>Above, the monks sang Nunc Dimittis.</content>
<content>By and by the voices fell silent.</content>
<content>Vespers were over.</content>
<content>Careful, shuffling feet were coming down the stairs within the wall.</content>

<content>But at sound of the turning key, the woman in his arms shivered, the slow, cold shudder of a soul in pain; and suddenly he knew that in coming to him she had chosen that which now seemed to her the harder part.</content>
<content>With the first revulsion of feeling occasioned by this knowledge, came a strong impulse to put her from him, to leap down the stairway, force open the heavy door, thrust her into the passage leading to her Nunnery, and shut the door upon her; then go out himself into the world to seek, in one wild search, every possible form of sin and revelry.</content>
<content>But this ungoverned impulse lasted but for the moment in which his passionate joy, recoiling upon himself, struck him a blinding, a bewildering blow.</content>
<content>In ten seconds he had recovered.</content>
<content>His arms tightened more securely around her.</content>
<content>She had come to him.</content>
<content>Whatever complex emotions might now be stirring within her, this fact was beyond question.</content>
<content>Also, she had come of her own free will.</content>
<content>The foot which had dared to stamp upon the torn fragments of the Pope's mandate, had, with an equal courage, stepped aside from the way of convention and had brought her within the compass of his arms.</content>
<content>He could not put her from him.</content>
<content>She was his to hold and keep.</content>
<content>But she was his also to shield and guard; aye, to shield not from outward dangers only, but from anything in himself which might cause her pain or perplexity, thus making more difficult her noble act of self-surrender.</content>
<content>Words spoken by the Bishop, in the banqueting hall, came back to him with fuller significance.</content>
<content>A joy arose within him, deeper far than the rapture of passion; the joy of a faithful patience, of a strong man's mastery over the strongest thing in himself, of a lover's comprehension, by sure instinct, of that which no words, however clear and forcible, could have succeeded in making plain.</content>
<content>His love arose, a kingly thing, crowned by her trust in him.</content>
<content>As he folded back the cloak, he stood with eyes uplifted to the arched roof above his head.</content>
<content>And the vision he saw, in the dim pearly light, was a vision of the Madonna in his home.</content>
<content>The shelter of the cloak removed, the Prioress looked around with startled eyes, full of an unspeakable shrinking; then upward to the face of her lover, and saw it transfigured by the light of holy purpose and of a great resolve.</content>
<content>But, even as she looked, he took his arm from about her, stepped a pace forward, leaving her in the shadow, and whistled thrice the Do-it-now call of the thrush.</content>
<content>Instantly the men-at-arms leapt to their feet, and making quickly for the entrance to the Cathedral from the crypt, stood to hold it from without, against all comers.</content>
<content>As their running feet rang on the steps, softly there sounded through the crypt the plaintive call of the curlew.</content>
<content>The man lying upon the stretcher rose, leaving his bandages behind; and, without glancing to right or left, passed quickly in and out amongst the forest of columns, and was lost to view.</content>
<content>The entrance he had to guard from within, was out of sight of the altar.</content>
<content>To all intents and purposes, the two who still stood motionless in the shadow, were now alone.</content>
<content>Then the Knight turned to the Prioress, took her right hand with his left, and led her forward to the altar.</content>
<content>There he loosed her hand as they knelt side by side; he clasping his upon the crossed hilt of his sword; she crossing hers upon her breast.</content>

<content>Divining her desire, he rose, laid the ring upon the altar, then knelt again.</content>
<content>Then rising, he took the ring, kissed it reverently, and slipped it upon the little finger of his own left hand.</content>
<content>The sad eyes of the Prioress, watching him, said to this neither "yea" nor "nay."</content>
<content>Rising she waited meekly to know his will for her.</content>
<content>The Knight, the blue cloak over his arm, turned to the stretcher, picked up the bandages, then, spoke, very low, without looking at the Prioress.</content>
<content>"Lay thyself down thereon," he said.</content>
<content>"I grieve to ask it of thee,
Mora; but there is no other way of taking thee hence, unobserved."</content>
<content>The Prioress took two steps forward, and stood beside the stretcher.</content>
<content>It was many years since she had lain in any human presence.</content>
<content>Standing, walking, sitting, kneeling, she had been seen by the nuns; but lying never.</content>
<content>Though her cross of office and sacred ring were gone, her dignity and authority seemed still to belong to her while she stood, stately and tall, upon her feet.</content>
<content>She hesitated.</content>
<content>The apologetic tone the Knight had used, seemed warrant for her hesitancy, and rendered compliance more difficult.</content>
<content>Each moment it became more impossible to place herself upon the stretcher.</content>
<content>"Lie down," said the Knight, sternly.</content>
<content>At the curt word of command, the Prioress shuddered again; but, without a word, she laid herself down upon the stretcher, closing her eyes, and crossing her hands upon her breast.</content>
<content>So white she was, so still, so rigid; as Hugh d'Argent, the bandages in his hand, stood looking down upon her, she seemed the marble effigy of a recumbent Prioress, graven upon a tomb; save that, as the Knight looked upon that beautiful, proud face, two burning tears forced their way from beneath the closed lids and rolled helplessly down the pale cheeks.</content>
<content>She did not see the look of tender compunction, of adoring love, in
Hugh's eyes.</content>
<content>Her shame, her utter humiliation, seemed complete.</content>
<content>Not when she took off her jewelled cross, and placed it upon our Lady's hand; not when she stepped aside and allowed herself to be hidden by the cloak; not even when she removed her ring and handed it to Hugh, did she cease to be Prioress of the White Ladies of Worcester; but when she laid herself down before the shrine of Saint Oswald, full length upon the stretcher, at her lover's feet.</content>
<content>Hugh stooped, and hid the bandages beside her.</content>
<content>He could not bring himself to touch or to disguise that lovely head.</content>
<content>Instead, he covered her completely with the cloak; saying, in deep tones of infinite tenderness:

"Our Lady be with thee.</content>
<content>It will not be for long."</content>
<content>Then, shrill through the silent crypt, rang the dear call of the blackbird.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXXII

A GREAT RECOVERY AND RESTORATION

Symon, Bishop of Worcester, attended by his Chaplain, chanced to be walking through the Precincts on his way from the Priory to the Palace, just as the men-at-arms bearing the stretcher came through the great door of the Cathedral.</content>
<content>Father Benedict, cowled, and robed completely in black, a head and shoulders taller than the Bishop, walked behind him, a somewhat sinister figure.</content>
<content>The Bishop stopped.</content>
<content>"Precede me to the Palace, Father Benedict," he said.</content>
<content>"I wish to have speech with yonder Knight who, I think, comes this way."</content>
<content>The Chaplain stood still, made deep obeisance, jerked his cowl more closely over his face, and strode away.</content>
<content>The Bishop waited, a radiant figure, in the afternoon sunshine.</content>
<content>His silken cassock, his silvery hair, his blue eyes, so vivid and searching, not only made a spot on which light concentrated, but almost seemed themselves to give forth light.</content>
<content>The steady tramp of the men-at-arms drew nearer.</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent walked beside the stretcher, head erect, eyes shining, his hand upon the hilt of his sword.</content>
<content>When the Bishop saw the face of the Knight, he moved to meet the little procession as it approached.</content>
<content>He held up his hand, and the men-at-arms halted.</content>
<content>"Good-day to you, Sir Hugh," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Hath your pilgrimage to the shrine of the blessed Saint Oswald worked the recovery you hoped?"</content>
<content>"Aye, my lord," replied the Knight, "a great recovery and restoration.</content>
<content>We start for Warwick in an hour's time."</content>
<content>"Wonderful!"</content>
<content>said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Our Lady and the holy Saint be praised!</content>
<content>But you are wise to keep the patient well covered.</content>
<content>However complete the restoration, great care is required at first, and over-exertion must be avoided."</content>
<content>"Your blessing for the patient, Reverend Father," said the Knight, uncovering.</content>
<content>The Bishop moved nearer.</content>
<content>He laid his hand upon the form beneath the blue and silver cloak.</content>
<content>"Benedictio Domini sit vobiscum," he said.</content>
<content>Then added, in a lower tone: "Be not afraid, neither be thou dismayed.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Go in peace."</content>

<content>It was the Knight who had smiled, and there was glory in his eyes, and triumph in the squaring of his broad shoulders, the swing of his stride, and the proud poise of his head.</content>
<content>The Bishop was white to the lips.</content>
<content>His hands trembled as he walked.</content>
<content>He feared he feared sorely this that they had accomplished.</content>
<content>It was one thing to theorize, to speculate, to advise, when the Prioress was safe in her Nunnery.</content>
<content>It was quite another, to know that she was being carried through the streets of Worcester, helpless, upon a stretcher; that when that blue pall was lifted, she would find herself in a hostel, alone with her lover, surrounded by men, not a woman within call.</content>
<content>The heart of a nun was a thing well known to the Bishop, and he trembled at thought of this, which he had helped to bring about.</content>
<content>Also he marvelled greatly that the Prioress should have changed her mind; and he sought in vain to conjecture the cause of that change.</content>
<content>Arrived in the courtyard of the Palace, he called for Brother Philip.</content>
<content>"Saddle me Shulamite," he said.</content>
<content>"Also mount Jasper on our fastest nag, with saddle-bags.</content>
<content>We ride to Warwick; and must start within a quarter of an hour."</content>
<content>A portion of that time the Bishop spent writing in the library.</content>
<content>When he was mounted, he stooped from the saddle and spoke to Brother
Philip.</content>
<content>"Philip," he said, "a very noble lady, betrothed to Sir Hugh d'Argent, has just arrived at the Star hostel, where for some days he has awaited her.</content>
<content>She rides with the Knight forthwith to Warwick, where they will join me at the Castle.</content>
<content>It is my wish to lend Iconoklastes to the lady.</content>
<content>Therefore I desire thee to saddle the palfrey precisely as he was saddled when he went to the Convent of the White Ladies for their pleasuring and play.</content>
<content>Lead him, without delay, to the hostel; deliver him over to the men-at-arms of Sir Hugh d'Argent, and see that they hand this letter at once to the Knight, that he may give it to his lady.</content>
<content>Lose not a moment, my good Philip.</content>
<content>Look to see me return to-morrow."</content>
<content>The Bishop gathered up the reins, and started out, at a brisk pace, for the Warwick road.</content>
<content>The letter he had intrusted to Brother Philip, sealed with his own signet, was addressed to Sir Hugh d'Argent.</content>
<content>But within was written:

Will the Countess of Norelle be pleased to accept of the palfrey Iconoklastes as a marriage gift from her old friend Symon Wygorn.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXXIII

MARY ANTONY HOLDS THE FORT

Mary Antony awaited in the cloisters the return of the White Ladies from Vespers.</content>
<content>The old lay-sister was not in the mood for gay chatter to the robin, nor even for quaint converse with herself.</content>
<content>She sat upon the stone seat, looking very frail, and wearing a wistful expression, quite unlike her usual alert demeanour.</content>
<content>As she sat, she slowly dropped the twenty-five peas from her right hand, to her left, and back again.</content>
<content>A wonderful thing had happened on that afternoon, just before the White
Ladies set forth to the Cathedral.</content>
<content>All were assembling in the cloisters, when word arrived that the
Reverend Mother wished to speak, in her cell, with Sister Mary Antony.</content>
<content>Hastening thither she found the Reverend Mother standing, very white and silent, very calm and steadfast, looking out from the oriel window.</content>
<content>At first she did not turn; and Mary Antony stood waiting, just within the doorway.</content>
<content>Then she turned, and said: "Ah, dear Antony!"</content>
<content>in tones which thrilled the heart of the old lay-sister.</content>
<content>"Come hither, Antony," she said; and even as she said it, moved to meet her.</content>
<content>A few simple instructions she gave, concerning matters in the Refectory and kitchen.</content>
<content>Then said: "Now I must go.</content>
<content>The nuns wait."</content>
<content>Then of a sudden she put her arms about the old lay-sister.</content>
<content>"Good-bye, my Antony," she said.</content>
<content>"Thy love and devotion have been very precious to me.</content>
<content>The Presence of the Lord abide with thee in blessing, while we are gone."</content>
<content>And, stooping, she kissed her gently on the brow; then passed from the cell.</content>
<content>Mary Antony stood as one that dreamed.</content>
<content>It was so many years since any touch of tenderness had reached her.</content>
<content>And now those gracious arms around her; those serene eyes looking upon her with love in their regard, and a something more, which her old heart failed to fathom; those lips, whose every word of command she and the whole Community hastened to obey, leaving a kiss upon her brow!</content>
<content>Long after the White Ladies had formed into procession and left the cloisters, Mary Antony stood as one that dreamed.</content>
<content>Then, remembering her duties, she hurried to the cloisters, but found them empty; down the steps to the crypt passage; the door was locked on the inside; the key gone.</content>
<content>The procession had started, and Mary Antony had failed to be at her post.</content>
<content>The White Ladies had departed uncounted.</content>
<content>Mary Antony had not been there to count them.</content>
<content>Never before had the Reverend Mother sent for her when she should have been on duty elsewhere.</content>
<content>Hastening to remedy her failure, Mary Antony drew the bag of peas from her wallet, opened it, and hurrying from cell to cell, took out a pea at each, as she verified its emptiness; until five-and-twenty peas lay in her hand.</content>
<content>So now she waited, her error repaired; yet ever with her then, as she ran, and now, as she waited she felt the benediction of the Reverend Mother's kiss, the sense of her encircling arms, the wonder of her gracious words.</content>
<content>"The Presence of the Lord abide with thee in blessing."</content>
<content>Yes, a heavenly calm was in the cloisters.</content>
<content>The Devil had stayed away.</content>
<content>Heaven seemed very near.</content>
<content>Even that little vain man, the robin, appeared to be busy elsewhere.</content>
<content>Mary Antony was quite alone.</content>
<content>"While we are gone."</content>
<content>But they would not now be long.</content>
<content>Mary Antony could tell by the shadows on the grass, and the slant of the sunshine through a certain arch, that the hour of return drew near.</content>
<content>She would kneel beside the topmost step, and see the Reverend Mother pass; she would look up at that serene face which had melted into tenderness; would see the firm line of those beautiful lips 

Suddenly Mary Antony knew that she would not be able to look.</content>
<content>Not just yet could she bear to see the Reverend Mother's countenance, without that expression of wonderful tenderness.</content>
<content>And even as she realised this, the key grated in the lock below.</content>
<content>Taking up her position at the top of the steps, the five-and-twenty peas in her right hand, Mary Antony quickly made up her mind.</content>
<content>She could not lift her eyes to the Reverend Mother's face.</content>
<content>She would count the passing feet.</content>
<content>The young lay-sister who carried the light, stumped up the steps, and set down the lantern with a clatter.</content>
<content>She plumped on to her knees opposite to Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"Sister Mary Rebecca leads to-day," she chanted in a low voice, "and all the way hath stepped upon my heels."</content>
<content>But Mary Antony took no notice of this information, which, at any other time, would have delighted her.</content>
<content>Head bowed, eyes on the ground, she awaited the passing feet.</content>
<content>They came, moving slow and sedate.</content>
<content>They passed stepping two by two, out of her range of vision; moving along the cloister, dying away in the distance.</content>
<content>All had passed.</content>
<content>Nay!</content>
<content>Not all?</content>
<content>Another comes!</content>
<content>Surely, another comes?</content>
<content>Sister Abigail, lifting the lantern, rose up noisily.</content>
<content>"What wait you for, Sister Antony?</content>
<content>The holy Ladies have by now entered their cells."</content>
<content>Mary Antony lifted startled eyes.</content>
<content>The golden bars of sunlight fell across an empty cloister.</content>
<content>A few white figures in the passage, seen in the distance through the open door, were vanishing, one by one, into their cells.</content>
<content>Mary Antony covered her dismay with indignation.</content>
<content>"Be off, thou impudent hussy!</content>
<content>Hold thy noisy tongue and hang thy rattling lantern on a nail; or, better still, hold thy lantern, and hang thyself, holding it, upon the nail.</content>
<content>If I am piously minded to pray here until sunset, that is no concern of thine.</content>
<content>Be off, I say!"</content>
<content>Left alone, Mary Antony slowly opened her right hand, and peered into the palm.</content>
<content>One pea lay within it.</content>
<content>She went over to the seat and counted, with trembling fingers, the peas from her left hand.</content>
<content>Twenty-four!</content>
<content>One holy Lady had therefore not returned.</content>
<content>This must be reported at once to the Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>In her excitement, Mary Antony forgot the emotion which had so recently possessed her.</content>
<content>Bustling down the steps, she drew the key from the door, paused one moment to peep into the dank darkness, listening for running footsteps or a voice that called; then closed the door, locked it, drew forth the key, and hurried to the Reverend Mother's cell.</content>
<content>The door stood ajar, just as she had left it.</content>
<content>She knocked, but entered without waiting to be bidden, crying: "Oh, Reverend Mother!</content>
<content>Twenty-five holy Ladies went to Vespers, and but twenty-four have" 

Then her voice died away into silence.</content>
<content>The Reverend Mother's cell was empty.</content>
<content>Stock-still stood Mary Antony, while her world crumbled from beneath her old feet and her heaven rolled itself up like a scroll, from over her head, and departed.</content>
<content>The Reverend Mother's cell was empty.</content>
<content>It was the Reverend Mother who had not returned.</content>
<content>"Good-bye, my Antony.</content>
<content>The Presence of the Lord abide with thee in blessing, while we are gone."</content>
<content>Ah, gone!</content>
<content>Never to return!</content>
<content>Once again the old lay-sister stood as one that dreamed; but this time instead of beatific joy, there was a forlorn pathos in the dreaming.</content>
<content>Presently a door opened, and a step sounded, far away in the passage beyond the Refectory stairs.</content>
<content>Instantly a look of cunning and determination replaced the helpless dismay on the old face.</content>
<content>She quickly closed the cell door, hung up the crypt key in its accustomed place; then kneeling before the shrine of the Madonna: "Blessed Virgin," she prayed, with clasped hands uplifted; "be pleased to sharpen once again the wits of old Mary Antony."</content>
<content>Rising, she found the key of the Reverend Mother's cell, passed out, closing the door behind her; locked it, and slipped the key into her wallet.</content>
<content>The passage was empty.</content>
<content>All the nuns were spending in prayer and meditation the time until the Refectory bell should ring.</content>
<content>Mary Antony appeared in the kitchen, only a few minutes later than usual.</content>
<content>"Prepare you the evening meal," she said to her subordinates.</content>
<content>"I care not what the holy Ladies feed upon this even, nor how badly it be served.</content>
<content>Reverend Mother again elects to spend the night in prayer and fasting.</content>
<content>So Mother Sub-Prioress will spit out a curse upon the viands; or Sister Mary Rebecca will miaul over them like an old cat that sees a tom in every shadow, though all toms have long since fled at her approach.</content>
<content>Serve at the usual hour; and let Abigail ring the Refectory bell.</content>
<content>I am otherwise employed.</content>
<content>And remember.</content>
<content>Reverend Mother is on no account to be disturbed."</content>
<content>The porteress, at the gate, jumped well-nigh out of her skin when, turning, she found Mary Antony at her elbow.</content>
<content>"Beshrew me, Sister Antony!"</content>
<content>she exclaimed.</content>
<content>"Wherefore" 

"Whist!"</content>
<content>said Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"Speak not so loud.</content>
<content>Now listen, Mary Mark.</content>
<content>Saw you the great Lord Bishop yesterday, a-walking with Mary Antony?</content>
<content>Ha, ha!</content>
<content>Yea, verily!</content>
<content>'Worthy Mother,' his lordship called me.</content>
<content>'Worthy Mother,' with his hand upon his heart.</content>
<content>And into the gardens he walked with Mary Antony.</content>
<content>Wherefore, you ask?</content>
<content>Wherefore should the great Lord Bishop walk in the Convent garden with an old lay-sister, who ceased to be a comely wench more than half a century ago?</content>
<content>Because, Sister Mark, if you needs must know, the Lord Bishop is full of anxious fears for the Reverend Mother, and knoweth that Mary Antony, old though she be, is able to tend and watch over her.</content>
<content>The Lord Bishop and the Worthy Mother both fear that the Reverend Mother fasts too often, and spends too many hours in vigil.</content>
<content>The Reverend Father has therefore deputed the Worthy Mother to watch in this matter, and to let him know at once if the Reverend Mother imperils her health again, by too lengthy a fast or vigil.</content>
<content>And, lo!</content>
<content>this very day, the Reverend Mother purposes not coming to the evening meal, and intends spending the whole night in prayer and vigil, before our Lady's shrine.</content>
<content>Therefore the Worthy Mother I, myself must start at once to fetch the great Lord Bishop; and you, Sister Mary Mark, must open the gate and let me be gone."</content>
<content>The porteress gazed, round-eyed and amazed.</content>
<content>"Nay, Sister Mary Antony, that can I not, without an order from the
Reverend Mother herself.</content>
<content>And even then, you could not walk so far as
to the Lord Bishop's Palace.</content>
<content>I doubt if you would even reach the
Fore-gate."</content>
<content>"That I should, and shall!"</content>
<content>cried Mary Antony.</content>
<content>"And, if my old legs fail me, many a gallant will dismount and offer me his horse.</content>
<content>Thus in fine style shall I ride into Worcester city.</content>
<content>Didst thou not see me bestride the Lord Bishop's white palfrey on Play Day?"</content>
<content>Sister Mary Mark broke into laughter.</content>
<content>"Aye," she said, "my sides have but lately ceased aching.</content>
<content>I pray you,
Sister Antony, call not that sight again into my mind."</content>
<content>"Then open the door, Mary Mark, and let me go."</content>
<content>"Nay, that I dare not do."</content>
<content>"Then, if I fail to do as bidden by the great Lord Bishop, I shall tell his lordship that thou, and thine obstinacy, stood in the way of the fulfilment of my purpose."</content>
<content>The porteress wavered.</content>
<content>"Bring me leave from the Reverend Mother, Sister Antony."</content>
<content>"Nay, that can I not," said Mary Antony, "as any fool might see, when I go without the Reverend Mother's knowledge to report to the Lord Bishop by his private command.</content>
<content>Even the Reverend Mother herself obeys the commands of the Lord Bishop."</content>
<content>Sister Mary Mark hesitated.</content>
<content>She certainly had seen the Lord Bishop
pass under the rose-arch, and enter the garden, in close converse with
Sister Mary Antony.</content>
<content>Yet her trust at the gate was given to her by the
Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>"See here, Mary Mark," said Sister Antony.</content>
<content>"I must send a message forthwith to Mother Sub-Prioress.</content>
<content>You shall take it, leaving me in charge of the gate, as often I am left, by order of the Reverend Mother, when you are bidden elsewhere.</content>
<content>If, on your return and you need not to hurry you find me gone, none can blame you.</content>
<content>Yet when the Lord Bishop rides in at sunset, he will give you his blessing and, like enough, something besides."</content>
<content>Mary Mark's hesitation vanished.</content>
<content>"I will take your message, Sister Antony," she said meekly.</content>
<content>"Go, by way of the kitchens and the Refectory stairs, to the cell of Mother Sub-Prioress.</content>
<content>Say that the Reverend Mother purposes passing the night in prayer and vigil, will not come to the evening meal, and desires Mother Sub-Prioress to take her place.</content>
<content>Also that for no cause whatever is the Reverend Mother to be disturbed."</content>
<content>Sister Mary Mark, being thus given a legitimate reason for leaving her post and gaining the Bishop's favour without giving cause for displeasure to the Prioress, departed, by way of the kitchens, to carry Mary Antony's message.</content>
<content>No sooner was she out of sight, than Mary Antony seized the key, unlocked the great doors, pulled them apart, and left them standing ajar, the key in the lock; then hastened back across the courtyard, passed under the rose-arch, and creeping beneath the shelter of the yew hedge, reached the steps up to the cloisters; slipped unobserved through the cloister door, and up the empty passage; unlocked the Reverend Mother's cell, entered it, and softly closed and locked the door behind her.</content>
<content>Then in order to make it impossible to yield to any temptation to open the door she withdrew the key, and flung it through the open window, far out into the shrubbery.</content>
<content>Thus did Mary Antony prepare to hold the fort, until the coming of the
Bishop.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXXIV

MORA DE NORELLE

Symon, Bishop of Worcester, chid himself for restlessness.</content>
<content>Surely for once his mind had lost control of his limbs.</content>
<content>No sooner did he decide to walk the smooth lawns around the Castle, than he found himself mounting to the battlements; and now, though he had installed himself for greatly needed repose in a deep seat in the hall chamber, yet here he was, pacing the floor, or moving from one window to another.</content>
<content>By dint of hard riding he had reached Warwick while the sun, though already dipped beneath the horizon, still flecked the sky with rosy clouds, and spread a golden mantle over the west.</content>
<content>The lord of the Castle was away, in attendance on the King; but all was in readiness for the arrival of the Bishop, and great preparations had been made for the reception of Sir Hugh d'Argent.</content>
<content>His people, having left Worcester early that morning, were about in the courtyard, as the Bishop rode in.</content>
<content>As he passed through the doorway, an elderly woman, buxom, comely, and of motherly aspect, whom he easily divined to be the tire-woman of whom the Knight had spoken, came forward to meet him.</content>
<content>"Good my lord," she said, her eagerness allowing of scant ceremony, "comes Sir Hugh d'Argent hither this night?"</content>
<content>"Aye," replied the Bishop, looking with kindly eyes upon Mora's old nurse.</content>
<content>"Within two hours, he should be here."</content>
<content>"Comes he alone, my lord?"</content>
<content>asked Mistress Deborah.</content>
<content>"Nay," replied the Bishop, "the Countess of Norelle, a very noble lady to whom the Knight is betrothed, rides hither with him."</content>
<content>"The saints be praised!"</content>
<content>exclaimed the old woman, and turned away to hide her tears.</content>
<content>Whilst his body-servant prepared a bath and laid out his robes, the Bishop mounted to the ramparts and watched the gold fade in the west.</content>
<content>He glanced at the river below, threading its way through the pasture land; at the billowy masses of trees; at the gay parterre, bright with summer flowers.</content>
<content>Then he looked long in the direction of the city from which he had come.</content>
<content>During his strenuous ride, the slow tramp of the men-at-arms, had sounded continually in his ears; the outline of that helpless figure, lying at full length upon the stretcher, had been ever before his eyes.</content>
<content>He could not picture the arrival at the hostel, the removal of the covering, the uprising of the Prioress to face life anew, enfolded in the arms of her lover.</content>
<content>As in a weary dream, in which the mind can make no headway, but returns again and yet again to the point of distress, so, during the entire ride, the Bishop had followed that stretcher through the streets of Worcester city, until it seemed to him as if, before the pall was lifted, the long-limbed, graceful form beneath it would have stiffened in death.</content>
<content>"A corpse for a bride!</content>
<content>A corpse for a bride!"</content>
<content>the hoofs of the black mare Shulamite had seemed to beat out upon the road.</content>
<content>"Alas, poor Knight!</content>
<content>A corpse for a bride!"</content>
<content>The Bishop came down from the battlements.</content>
<content>When he left his chamber an hour later, he had donned those crimson robes which he wore on the evening when the Knight supped with him at the Palace.</content>
<content>As he paced up and down the lawns, the gold cross at his breast gleamed in the evening light.</content>
<content>A night-hawk, flying high overhead and looking downward as it flew, might have supposed that a great scarlet poppy had left its clump in the flower-beds, and was promenading on the turf.</content>
<content>A steward came out to ask when it would please the Lord Bishop to sup.</content>
<content>To the hovering hawk, a blackbird seemed to have hopped out, confronting and arresting the promenading poppy.</content>
<content>The Bishop said he would await the arrival of Sir Hugh; but he turned and followed the man into the Castle.</content>
<content>And now he sat in the great hall chamber.</content>
<content>Two hours had passed since his arrival.</content>
<content>Unless something unforeseen had occurred the Knight's cavalcade must be here before long.</content>
<content>He had planned to start within the hour; and, though the Bishop had ridden fast, they could scarcely have taken more than an hour longer to do the distance.</content>
<content>But supposing the Prioress had faltered at the last, and had besought to be returned to the Nunnery?</content>
<content>Would the chivalry of the Knight have stood such a test?</content>
<content>And, having left in secret, how could she return openly?</content>
<content>Would the way through the crypt be possible?</content>
<content>The Bishop began to wish that he had ridden to the Star hostel and awaited developments there, instead of hastening on before.</content>
<content>The hall chamber was in the centre of the Castle.</content>
<content>Its casements looked out upon the gardens.</content>
<content>Thus it came about that he did not hear a cavalcade ride into the courtyard.</content>
<content>He did not hear the shouting of the men, the ring of hoofs on the paving stones, the champing of horses.</content>
<content>He sat in a great carved chair beside the fireplace in the hall chamber, forcing himself to stillness, yet tormented by anxiety; half minded to order a fresh horse and to ride back to Worcester.</content>
<content>Suddenly, without any warning, the door, leading from the ante-chamber at the further end of the hall, opened.</content>
<content>Framed in the doorway appeared a vision, which for a moment led Symon of Worcester to question whether he dreamed, so beautiful beyond belief was the woman in a green riding-dress, looking at him with starry eyes, her cheeks aglow, a veil of golden hair falling about her shoulders.</content>
<content>Oh, Mora, child of delight!</content>
<content>Has the exquisite promise of thy girlhood indeed fulfilled itself thus?</content>
<content>Have the years changed thee so little -and yet so greatly?</content>
<content>"The captive exile hasteneth"; exile, long ago, for thy sake; seeking to be free, yet captive still, caught once and forever in the meshes of that golden hair.</content>
<content>Oh, Mora, child of delight!</content>
<content>Must all this planning for thy full development and perfecting of joy, involve the poignant anguish of thus seeing thee again?</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester rose and stood, a noble figure in crimson and gold, at the top of the hall.</content>
<content>But for the silver moonlight of his hair, he might have been a man in his prime so erect was his carriage, so keen and bright were his eyes.</content>
<content>The tall woman in the doorway gave a little cry; then moved quickly forward.</content>
<content>"You?"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"You!</content>
<content>The priest who is to wed us?</content>
<content>You!"</content>
<content>He stood his ground, awaiting her approach.</content>
<content>"Yes, I," he said; "I."</content>
<content>Half-way across the hall, she paused.</content>
<content>"No," she said, as if to herself.</content>
<content>"I dream.</content>
<content>It is not Father
Gervaise.</content>
<content>It is the Bishop."</content>
<content>She drew nearer.</content>
<content>Earnestly he looked upon her, striving to see in her the Prioress of
Whytstone the friend of all these happy, peaceful, blessed years.</content>
<content>But the Prioress had vanished.</content>
<content>Mora de Norelle stood before him, taller by half a head than he, flushed by long galloping in the night breeze; nerves strung to breaking point; eyes bright with the great unrest of a headlong leap into a new world.</content>
<content>Yet the firm sweet lips were there, unchanged; and, even as he marked them, they quivered and parted.</content>
<content>"Reverend Father," she said, "I have chosen, even as you prayed I might do, the harder part."</content>
<content>She flung aside the riding-whip she carried; and folding her hands, held them up before him.</content>
<content>"For Christ's sake, my Lord Bishop, pray for me!"</content>
<content>He took those folded hands in his, gently parted them, and held them against the cross upon his heart.</content>
<content>"You have chosen rightly, my child," he said; "we will pray that grace and strength may be vouchsafed you, so that you may continue, without faltering, along the pathway of this fresh vocation."</content>
<content>She looked at him with searching gaze.</content>
<content>The kind and gentle eyes of the Bishop met hers without wavering; also without any trace of the fire the keen brightness which had startled her as she stood in the doorway.</content>
<content>"Reverend Father," she said, and there was a strange note of bewildered question in her voice: "I pray you, tell me what you bid penitents to remember as they kneel in prayer before the crucifix?"</content>
<content>The Bishop looked full into those starry grey eyes bent upon him, and his own did not falter.</content>
<content>His mild voice took on a shade of sternness as befitted the solemn subject of her question.</content>
<content>"I tell them, my daughter, to remember, the sacred Wounds that bled and the Heart that broke for them."</content>
<content>She drew her hands from beneath his, and stepped back a pace.</content>
<content>"The Heart that broke?"</content>
<content>she said.</content>
<content>"That broke?</content>
<content>Do hearts break?"</content>
<content>she cried.</content>
<content>"Nay, rather, they turn to stone."</content>
<content>She laughed wildly, then caught her breath.</content>
<content>The Knight had entered the hall.</content>
<content>With free, glad step, and head uplifted, Hugh d'Argent came to them, where they stood.</content>
<content>"My Lord Bishop," he said, "you have been too good to us.</content>
<content>I sent Mora on alone that she might find you here, not telling her who was the prelate who had so graciously offered to wed us, knowing how much it would mean to her that it should be you, Reverend Father."</content>
<content>"Gladly am I here for that purpose, my son," replied the Bishop, "having as you know, the leave and sanction of His Holiness for so doing.</content>
<content>Shall we proceed at once to the chapel, or do you plan first to sup?"</content>
<content>"Nay, Father," said the Knight.</content>
<content>"My betrothed has ridden far and needs food first, and then a good night's rest.</content>
<content>If it will not too much delay your return to Worcester, I would pray you to wed us in the morning."</content>
<content>Knowing how determined Hugh had been, in laying his plans, to be wed at once on reaching Warwick, the Bishop looked up quickly, wishing to understand what had wrought this change.</content>
<content>He saw on the Knight's face that look of radiant peace which the Prioress had seen, when first the cloak was turned back in the crypt; and the Bishop, having passed that way himself, knew that to Hugh had come the revelation which comes but to the true, lover the deepest of all joys, that of putting himself on one side, and of thinking, first and only, of the welfare of the beloved.</content>
<content>And seeing this, the Bishop let go his fears, and in his heart thanked
God.</content>
<content>"It is well planned, Hugh," he said.</content>
<content>"I am here until the morning."</content>
<content>At which the Knight turning, strode quickly to the door, and beckoned.</content>
<content>Then back he came, leading by the hand the buxom, motherly old dame, seen on arrival by the Bishop.</content>
<content>Who, when the Lady Mora saw, she gave a cry, and ran to meet her.</content>
<content>"Debbie!"</content>
<content>she cried, "Oh, Debbie!</content>
<content>Let us go home!"</content>
<content>And with that the tension broke all on a sudden, and with her old nurse's arms around her, she sobbed on the faithful bosom which had been the refuge of her childhood's woes.</content>
<content>"There, my pretty!"</content>
<content>said Deborah, as best she could for her own sobs.</content>
<content>"There, there!</content>
<content>We are at home, now we are together.</content>
<content>Come and see the chamber in which we shall sleep, just as we slept long years ago, when you were a babe, my dear."</content>
<content>So, with her old nurse's arms about her, she, who had come in so proudly, went gently out in a soft mist of tears.</content>
<content>The Bishop turned away.</content>
<content>"Love never faileth," he murmured, half aloud.</content>
<content>Hugh turned with him, and laughed; but in his laughter there was no vexation, no bitterness, no unrest.</content>
<content>It was the happy laugh of a heart aglow with a hope amounting to certainty.</content>
<content>"There were two of us the other night, my dear lord," he said; "but now old Debbie has appeared, methinks there are three!"</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXXV

IN THE ARBOUR OF GOLDEN ROSES

The next day dawned, clear and radiant; a perfect summer morning.</content>
<content>Mora awoke soon after five o'clock.</content>
<content>Notwithstanding the fatigue of the previous day, the strain and stress of heart, and the late hour at which she had at length fallen asleep, the mental habit of years overcame the physical need of further slumber.</content>
<content>Her first conscious thought was for the rope which worked over a pulley through a hole in the wall of her cell, enabling her from, within to ring the great bell in the passage, thus rousing the entire community.</content>
<content>It had been her invariable habit to do this herself.</content>
<content>She liked the nuns to feel that the call to begin a new day came to them from the hand of their Prioress.</content>
<content>Realising the difficulty of early rising, especially after night vigils, it pleased her that her nuns should know that the fact of the bell resounding through the Convent proved that the Reverend Mother was already on her feet.</content>
<content>Yet now, looking toward the door, she could see no rope.</content>
<content>And what meant those sumptuous tapestry hangings?</content>
<content>She leapt from her couch, and gazed around her.</content>
<content>Why fell her hair about her, as a golden cloud?</content>
<content>that beautiful hair, which in some Orders would have been shorn from her head; and, in this, must ever be closely braided, covered, and never seen.</content>
<content>Still half-bewildered, she flung it back; gazing at the unfamiliar, yet well-remembered, garments laid ready for her use.</content>
<content>Sometimes she had had such dreams as this dreams in which she was back in the world, wearing its garments, tasting its pleasures, looking again upon forbidden things.</content>
<content>Why should she not now be dreaming?</content>
<content>Then a sound fell upon her ear; a sound, long forgotten, yet so familiar that as she heard it, she felt herself a child at home again the soft, contented snoring of old Debbie, fast asleep.</content>
<content>Sound is ever more convincing than sight.</content>
<content>The blind live in a world of certainties.</content>
<content>Not so, the deaf.</content>
<content>Mora needed not to turn and view the comely countenance of her old nurse sleeping upon a couch in a corner.</content>
<content>At sound of that soft purring snore, she knew all she needed to know knew she was no longer Prioress, knew she had renounced her vows; knew that even now the Convent was waking and wondering, as last night it must have marvelled and surmised, and to-morrow would question and condemn; knew that this was her wedding morn; that this robe of softest white, with jewelled girdle, and jewelled circlet to crown her hair, were old Debbie's choice for her of suitable attire in which to stand beside her bridegroom at the altar.</content>
<content>Passing into an alcove, she bathed and clothed herself, even putting on the jewelled band to clasp the shining softness of her hair.</content>
<content>Debbie's will on these points had never been disputed, and truly it mattered little to Mora what she wore, since wimple and holy veil were forever laid aside.</content>
<content>She passed softly from the chamber, without awakening the old nurse, made her way down a winding stair, out through a postern door, and so into the gardens bathed in early morning sunshine.</content>
<content>Seeking to escape observation from the Castle walls or windows, she made her way through a rose-garden to where a high yew hedge surrounded a bowling-green.</content>
<content>At the further end of this secluded place stood a rustic summer-house, now a veritable bower of yellow roses.</content>
<content>Bending her head, Mora passed through an archway of yew, down three stone steps, and so on to the lawn.</content>
<content>Then, out from the arbour stepped the Bishop, in his violet cassock and biretta, his breviary in his hand.</content>
<content>If this first sight of Hugh's bride, in bridal array, on her wedding morning, surprised or stirred him, he gave no sign of unusual emotion.</content>
<content>As he came to meet her, his lips smiled kindly, and in his eyes was that half whimsical, half tender look, she knew so well.</content>
<content>He might have been riding into the courtyard of the Nunnery, and she standing on the steps to receive him, so natural was his greeting, so wholly as usual did he appear.</content>
<content>"You are up betimes, my daughter, as I guessed you would be; also you have come hither, as I hoped you might do.</content>
<content>Am I the first to wish you joy, on this glad day?"</content>
<content>"The first," she said.</content>
<content>"Even my good Deborah slept through my rising.</content>
<content>I woke at the accustomed hour, to ring the Convent bell, and found myself Prioress no longer, but bride an earthly bride expected to deck herself with jewels for an earthly bridal."</content>
<content>"'Even the ornament of a meek and quiet spirit, which is in the sight of God of great price,'" quoted, the Bishop, a retrospective twinkle in his eye.</content>
<content>"Alas, my lord, I fear that ornament was never mine."</content>
<content>"Yet you must wear it now, my daughter.</content>
<content>I have heard it is an ornament greatly admired by husbands."</content>
<content>Standing in the sunlight, all unconscious of her wondrous beauty, she opened startled eyes on him; then dropped to her knees upon the turf.</content>
<content>"Your blessing, Reverend Father," she said, and there was a wild sob in her voice.</content>
<content>"Oh, I entreat your blessing, on this my bridal day!"</content>
<content>The Bishop laid his hands upon the bright coronet of her hair, and blessed her with the threefold Aaronic blessing; then raised her, and bade her walk with him across the turf.</content>
<content>Into the arbour he led her, beneath a cascade of fragrant yellow roses.</content>
<content>There, upon a rustic table was spread a dainty repast new milk, fruit freshly gathered, white rolls, and most golden pats of butter, the dew of the dairy yet upon them.</content>
<content>"Come, my daughter," said Symon of Worcester, gaily.</content>
<content>"We of the Church, who know the value of these early hours, let us break our fast together."</content>
<content>"Is it magic, my lord?"</content>
<content>she asked, suddenly conscious of unmistakable hunger.</content>
<content>"Nay," said the Bishop, "but I was out a full hour ago.</content>
<content>And the dairy wench was up before me.</content>
<content>So between us we contrived this simple repast."</content>
<content>So, while the bridegroom and old Deborah still slumbered and slept, the bride and the Bishop broke their fast together in a bower of roses; and his eyes were the eyes of a merry schoolboy out on a holiday; and the colour came back to her cheeks and she smiled and grew light-hearted, as always in their long friendship, when he came to her in this gay mood.</content>
<content>Yet, presently, when she had eaten well, and seemed strengthened and refreshed, the Bishop leaned back in his seat, saying with sudden gravity:

"And now, my daughter, will you tell me how it has come to pass that you have been led to feel it right to take this irrevocable step, renouncing your vows, and keeping your troth to Hugh?</content>
<content>When last we spoke together you declared that naught would suffice but a clear sign, vouchsafed you from our Lady herself, making it plain that your highest duty was to Hugh, and that Heaven absolved you from your vows.</content>
<content>Was such a sign vouchsafed?"</content>
<content>"Indeed it was, my lord, in wondrous fashion, our Lady choosing as the mouthpiece of her will, by means of a most explicit and unmistakable revelation, one so humble and so simple, that I could but exclaim: 'Thou hast hid these things from the wise and prudent, and hast revealed them unto babes.'"</content>
<content>"And who," asked the Bishop, his eyes upon a peach which he was peeling with extreme care; "who, my daughter, was the babe?"</content>
<content>"The old lay-sister, Mary Antony."</content>
<content>"Ah," murmured the Bishop, "an ancient babe.</content>
<content>Yet truly, a most worthy babe.</content>
<content>Almost, I should be inclined to say, a wise and prudent babe."</content>
<content>"Nay, my Lord Bishop," cried Mora, with a sharp decision of tone which made it please him to imagine that, should he look up from the peach, he would see the severe lines of the wimple and scapulary: "you and I were the wise and prudent, arguing for and against, according to our own theories and reason.</content>
<content>But to this babe, our Lady vouchsafed a clear vision."</content>
<content>"Tell me of it," said the Bishop, splitting his peach and removing the stone which he carefully washed, and slipped into his sash.</content>
<content>The Bishop always kept peach stones, and planted them.</content>
<content>She told him.</content>
<content>She began at the beginning, and told him all, to the minutest detail; the full description of Hugh the amazingly correct repetition, in the vision, of the way in which she and Hugh had actually kneeled together before the shrine of the blessed Virgin, of their very words and actions; and, finally, the sublime and gracious tenderness of our Lady's pronouncement, clearly heard at the close of the vision, by the old lay-sister: "Take her; she hath been ever thine.</content>
<content>I have but kept her for thee."</content>
<content>"What say you to that, Reverend Father?"</content>
<content>exclaimed Mora, concluding.</content>
<content>"I scarce know what to say," replied the Bishop.</content>
<content>"For lack of anything better, I fall back upon my favourite motto, and I say: 'Love never faileth.'"</content>
<content>Now, generally, she delighted in the exceeding aptness of the Bishop's quotations; but this time it seemed to Mora that his favourite motto bore no sort of relevance.</content>
<content>She felt, with a chill of disappointment and a sense of vexation, that the Bishop's mind had been so intent upon the fruit, that he had not fully taken in the wonder of the vision.</content>
<content>"It has naught to do with love, my lord," she said, rather coldly; "unless you mean the divine lovingkindness of our blessed Lady."</content>
<content>"Precisely," replied the Bishop, leaning back in his seat, and at length looking straight into Mora's earnest eyes.</content>
<content>"The divine lovingkindness of our blessed Lady never faileth."</content>
<content>"You agree, my lord, that the vision shed a clear light upon all my perplexities?"</content>
<content>"Absolutely clear," replied the Bishop.</content>
<content>"The love which arranged the vision saw to that.</content>
<content>Revelations, my daughter, are useless unless they are explicit.</content>
<content>Had our Lady merely waved her marble hand, instead of stooping to take yours and place it in that of the Knight, you might have thought she was waving him away, and bidding you to remain.</content>
<content>If her marble hand moved at all, it is well that it moved in so definite and practical a manner."</content>
<content>"It seems to me, Reverend Father," said Mora, leaning upon the table, her face framed in her hands, and looking with knitted brows at the Bishop; "it almost seems to me that you regard the entire vision with a measure of secret incredulity."</content>
<content>"Nay, my daughter, there you mistake.</content>
<content>On the contrary I am fully convinced, by that which you tell me, that the ancient babe, Mary Antony, was undoubtedly permitted to see you and your knightly lover kneeling hand in hand before our Lady's shrine; also I praise our blessed Lady that by vouchsafing this sight to Mary Antony, and by allowing her to hear words which you yourself know to have been in very deed actually spoken, your mind has been led to accept as the divine will for you, this return to the world and union with your lover, which will, I feel sure, be not only for your happiness and his, but also a fruitful source of good to many.</content>
<content>Yet, I admit "

The Bishop paused, and considered; as if anxious to say just so much, and neither more nor less.</content>
<content>Continuing, he spoke slowly, weighing each word.</content>
<content>"Yet, I frankly admit, I would sooner for mine own guidance listen for the Voice of God within, or learn His will from the written Word, than ask for miraculous signs, or act upon the visions of others.</content>
<content>"No doubt you read, in the Chronicle I lately lent you, how 'in the year of our Lord eleven hundred and thirty-seven that time of many sorrows, of burning, pillaging, rapine and torture, when the city of York was burned together with the principal monastery; the city of Rochester was consumed; also the Church of Bath, and the city of Leicester; when owing to the absence of King Stephen abroad and the mildness of his rule when at home, the barons greatly oppressed and ill-used the Church and the people while many were standing at the Celebration of Mass at Windsor, they beheld the Crucifix, which was over the altar, moving and wringing its hands, now the right hand with the left, now the left with the right, after the manner of those who are in distress.'</content>
<content>"This wondrous sight convinced those who saw it that the crucified
Redeemer sympathised with the grievous sorrows of the land.</content>
<content>"But no carven crucifix, wringing its hands before a gazing crowd, could so deeply convince me of the sympathy of the Redeemer as to sit alone in mine own chamber and read from the book of Isaiah the Prophet: 'Surely He hath borne our griefs, and carried our sorrows.'"</content>
<content>Mora's brow cleared.</content>
<content>"I think I understand, my lord; and that you should so feel, helps me to confess to you a thing which I have scarce dared admit to myself.</content>
<content>I found it difficult in mine own soul to attach due weight to our blessed Lady's words as heard by Mary Antony.</content>
<content>Mine own test the robin's flight, straight from the hand of the Madonna to the world without spoke with more sense of truth to my heart.</content>
<content>I blame myself for this; but so it is.</content>
<content>Yet it was the vision which decided me as to my clear path of duty."</content>
<content>"Doubtless," remarked the Bishop, "the medium of Mary Antony took from the solemnity of the pronouncement.</content>
<content>There would be a twist of quaintness in even the holiest vision, as described by the old lay-sister."</content>
<content>"Nay, my lord," said Mora.</content>
<content>"Truth to tell, it was not so.</content>
<content>Once fairly started on the telling, she seemed lifted into a strange sublimity of utterance.</content>
<content>I marvelled at it, and at the unearthly radiance of her face.</content>
<content>At the end, I thought she slept; but later I heard from the Sub-Prioress that she was found swooning before the crucifix and they had much ado to bring her round.</content>
<content>"My lord, my heart fails me when I think to-day of my empty cell, and of the sore perplexity of my nuns.</content>
<content>How soon will it be possible that you see them and put the matter right, by giving the Holy Father's message?"</content>
<content>"So soon as you are wed, my daughter, I ride back to Worcester.</content>
<content>I shall endeavour to reach the Convent before the hour when they leave for Vespers."</content>
<content>"May I beg, my lord, that you speak a word of especial kindness to old Antony, whose heart will be sore at my departure?</content>
<content>I had thought to bid her be silent concerning the vision; but as she declares the shining Knight was Saint George or Saint Michael, the nuns, in their devout simplicity, will doubtless hold the vision to have been merely symbolic of my removal to 'higher service.'"</content>
<content>"I will seek old Antony," said the Bishop, "and speak with her alone."</content>
<content>"Father," said Mora, with deep emotion, "during all these years, you have been most good to me; kind beyond words; patient always.</content>
<content>I fear I ofttimes tried you by being too firmly set on my own will and way.</content>
<content>But, I pray you to believe, I ever valued your counsel and could scarce have lived without your friendship.</content>
<content>Last night, on first entering the Castle, I fear I spoke wildly and acted strangely.</content>
<content>I was sore overwrought.</content>
<content>I came in, out of the night, not knowing whom I should find in the hall chamber; and for a moment, my lord, for one wild, foolish moment I took you not for yourself but for another."</content>
<content>"For whom did you take me, my daughter?"</content>
<content>asked the Bishop.</content>
<content>"For one of whom you have oft reminded me, my lord, if I may say so without offence, seeing I speak of a priest who was the ideal of my girlhood's dreams.</content>
<content>Knew you, many years ago, one Father Gervaise, held in high regard at the Court, confessor to the Queen and her ladies?"</content>
<content>The Bishop smiled, and his blue eyes looked into Mora's with an expression of quiet interest.</content>
<content>"Father Gervaise?"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>"Preacher at the Court?</content>
<content>Indeed, I knew him, my daughter; and more than knew him.</content>
<content>Father Gervaise and I had the same grandparents."</content>
<content>"Ah," cried Mora, eagerly, "then that accounts for a resemblance which from the first has haunted me, making of our friendship, at once, so sweetly intimate a thing.</content>
<content>The voice and the eyes alone were like but, ah, so like!</content>
<content>Father Gervaise wore a beard, which hid his mouth and chin; but his blue eyes had in them that kindly yet searching look, though not merry as yours oft are, my lord; and your voice has ever made me think of his.</content>
<content>"And once just once his eyes looked at me, across the Castle hall at Windsor, with a deep glow of fire in them; a look which made me feel called to an altar whereon, if I could but stand the test of fire, I should be forever purified, uplifted, blest as was never earthly maid before, save only our blessed Lady.</content>
<content>All that night I dreamed of it, and my whole soul was filled with it, yet never again did I see Father Gervaise.</content>
<content>The next morning he left the Court, and soon after sailed for Spain; and on the passage thither the ship foundered in a great storm, and he, with all on board, perished.</content>
<content>Heard you of that, my lord?"</content>
<content>"I heard it," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"All believed it, and mourned him; for by all he was beloved.</content>
<content>But never could I feel that he was dead.</content>
<content>Always for me it seemed that he still lived.</content>
<content>And last night when I entered across the great hall chamber, it seemed as if, once more, the eyes of Father Gervaise looked upon me, with that glowing fire in them, which called me to an altar."</content>
<content>The Bishop smiled again, and there was in his look a gentle merriment.</content>
<content>"You were over-strained, my daughter.</content>
<content>When you drew near, you found instead of a ghostly priest with eyes of fire, drowned many years ago, off the coast of Spain your old friend, Symon of Worcester, who had stolen a march on you, by reason of the swift paces of his good mare, Shulamite."</content>
<content>Mora leaned forward, and laid her hand on his.</content>
<content>"Mock not, my friend," she said.</content>
<content>"There was a time when Father Gervaise stood to me for all my heart held dearest.</content>
<content>Yet I loved him, not as a girl loves a man, but rather as a nun loves her Lord.</content>
<content>He stood to me for all that was noblest and best; and, above all, for all that was vital and alive in life and in religion; strong to act; able to endure.</content>
<content>He confessed me once, and told me, when I kneeled before the crucifix, to say of Him Who hangs thereon: 'He ever liveth to make intercession for us.'</content>
<content>Never have I forgotten it.</content>
<content>And sometimes when I say the sacred words, and, saying them, my mind turns to Father Gervaise, an echo seems to whisper to my spirit: 'He, also, liveth.'"</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester rose.</content>
<content>"My daughter," he said, "the sun is high in the heavens.</content>
<content>We must not linger here.</content>
<content>Hugh will be seeking his bride, and Mistress Deborah be waxing anxious over the escape of her charge.</content>
<content>The morning meal will be ready in the banqueting hall; after which we must to the chapel, for the marriage.</content>
<content>Then, without delay, I ride to Worcester to make all right at the Nunnery.</content>
<content>Let us go."</content>
<content>As Mora walked beside him across the sunny lawn, "Father," she said, "think you the heart of a nun can ever become again as the heart of other women?"</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXXVI

STRONG TO ACT; ABLE TO ENDURE

Back to Worcester rode the Bishop.</content>
<content>Gallop!</content>
<content>Gallop!</content>
<content>along the grassy rides, beside the hard highway.</content>
<content>Hasten good Shulamite, black and comely still, though flecked with foam.</content>
<content>Important work lies ahead.</content>
<content>Every moment is precious.</content>
<content>If Mother Sub-Prioress should send to the Palace, mischief will be done, which it will not be easy to repair.</content>
<content>If news of the flight of the Prioress reaches the city of Worcester, a hundred tongues, spiteful, ignorant, curious, or merely idle, will at once start wagging.</content>
<content>Gallop, gallop, Shulamite!</content>
<content>How impossible to overtake a rumour, if it have an hour's start of you.</content>
<content>As well attempt to catch up the water which first rushed through the sluice-gates, opened an hour before you reached the dam.</content>
<content>How impossible to remake a reputation once broken.</content>
<content>Before the priceless Venetian goblet fell from the table on to the flagged floor, one hand put forth in time might have hindered its fall.</content>
<content>But failing that timely hand when, a second later, it lies in a hundred pieces, the hands of the whole world are powerless to make it again as it was before it fell.</content>
<content>Faster, faster, Shulamite!</content>
<content>When the messenger of Mother Sub-Prioress reports the absence of the Bishop, he will most certainly be sent in haste to Father Benedict, who will experience a sinister joy at the prospect of following his long nose into the Prioress's empty cell, who will scent out scandal where there is but a fragrance of lilies, and tear to pieces Mora's reputation, with as little compunction as a wolf tears a lamb.</content>
<content>Gallop, gallop, Shulamite!</content>
<content>If no hand be put forth to save it, between Mother Sub-Prioress and Father Benedict, this crystal bowl will be broken into a hundred pieces.</content>
<content>At length the Bishop drew rein, and walked his mare a mile.</content>
<content>He had left Warwick ten miles behind him.</content>
<content>He would soon be half-way to Worcester.</content>
<content>He had left Warwick behind him!</content>
<content>It seemed to the Bishop that, ever since he had first known Mora de
Norelle, he had always been riding away and leaving behind.</content>
<content>For her sake he rode away, leaving behind the Court, his various offices, his growing influence and popularity.</content>
<content>For her sake he left his identity as Father Gervaise at the bottom of the ocean, taking up his life again, in Italy, under his other name.</content>
<content>For her sake, when he heard that she had entered the Convent of the White Ladies, he obtained the appointment to the see of Worcester, leaving the sunny land he loved, and the prospect of far higher preferment there.</content>
<content>And now for her sake he rode away from Warwick as fast as steed could carry him, leaving her the bride of another, in whose hand he had himself placed hers, pronouncing the Church's blessing upon their union.</content>
<content>Riding away leaving behind; leaving behind riding away.</content>
<content>This was what his love had ever brought him.</content>
<content>Yet he felt rich to-day, finding himself in possession of the certain knowledge that he had been right in judging necessary, that first departure into exile long years ago.</content>
<content>For had not Mora told him little dreaming to whom she spoke that there was a time when he had stood to her for all her heart held dearest; yet that she had loved him, not as a girl loves a man, but rather as a nun loves her Lord.</content>
<content>But surely a man would need to be divine to be so loved, and to hold such love aright.</content>
<content>And, even then, when that other man arrived who would fain woo her to love him as a girl loves a man, would her heart be free to respond to the call of nature?</content>
<content>Nay.</content>
<content>To all intents and purposes, her heart would be a cloistered thing; yet would she be neither bride of Christ nor bride of man.</content>
<content>The fire in his eyes would indeed have called her to an altar, and the sacrifice laid thereon would be the full completion of her womanhood.</content>
<content>"I did well to pass into exile," said the Bishop, reviewing the past, as he rode.</content>
<content>Yet deep in his heart was the comfort of those words she had said: that once he had stood to her for all her heart held dearest.</content>
<content>Mora, the girl, had felt thus; Mora, the woman, remembered it; and the Bishop, as he thought of both, offered up a thanksgiving that neither he nor Father Gervaise had done aught which was unworthy of the ideal of her girlhood's dream.</content>
<content>Gathering up the reins, he urged Shulamite to a rapid trot.</content>
<content>There must be no lingering by the way.</content>
<content>Hasten, Shulamite!</content>
<content>Even now the sluice-gates may be opening.</content>
<content>Even now the crystal bowl may be slipping from its pedestal, presently to lie in a hundred fragments on the ground.</content>
<content>Nay, trotting will scarce do.</content>
<content>Gallop, gallop, brave black mare!</content>
<content>The city walls are just in sight.</content>
<content>Well done!</content>
<content>Not far from the Convent gate, the Bishop chanced, by great good fortune, upon Brother Philip, trying in the meadows the paces of a young horse, but lately purchased.</content>
<content>The Bishop bade the lay-brother ride with him to the Nunnery and, so soon as he should have dismounted, lead Shulamite to the Palace stables, carefully feed and tend her; then bring him out a fresh mount.</content>
<content>As they rode forward: "Hath any message arrived at the Palace from the
Convent, Philip?"</content>
<content>inquired the Bishop.</content>
<content>"None, my lord."</content>
<content>"Or at the Priory?"</content>
<content>"Nay, my lord.</content>
<content>But I did hear, at the Priory, a strange rumour" 

"Rumours are rarely worth regarding or repeating, Brother Philip."</content>
<content>"True, my lord.</content>
<content>Yet having so lately aided her to ride upon Icon" 

"'Her'?</content>
<content>With whom then is rumour making free?</content>
<content>And what saith this
Priory rumour concerning 'her'?"</content>
<content>"They say the old lay-sister, Mary Antony, hath fled the Convent."</content>
<content>"Mary Antony!"</content>
<content>exclaimed the Bishop, and his voice held the most extraordinary combination of amazement, relief, and incredulity.</content>
<content>"But, in heaven's name, good brother, wherefore should the old lay-sister leave the Convent?"</content>
<content>"They say she was making her way into the city in search of you, my lord; but she hath not reached the Palace."</content>
<content>"Any other rumour, Philip?"</content>
<content>"Nay, my lord, none; save that the Prioress is distraught with anxiety concerning the aged nun, and has commanded that the underground way to the Cathedral crypt be searched; though, indeed, the porteress confesses to having let Sister Mary Antony out at the gate."</content>
<content>"Rumour again," said the Bishop, "and not a word of truth in it, I warrant.</content>
<content>Deny it, right and left, my good Philip; and say, on my authority, that the Reverend Mother hath most certainly not caused the crypt way to be searched.</content>
<content>I would I could lay hands on the originator of these foolish tales."</content>
<content>The Bishop spoke with apparent vexation, but his heart had bounded in the upspring of a great relief.</content>
<content>Was he after all in time to save with outstretched hand that most priceless crystal bowl?</content>
<content>The Bishop dismounted outside the Convent gate.</content>
<content>He took Shulamite's nose into his hand, and spoke gently in her ear.</content>
<content>Then: "Lead her home, Philip," he said, "and surround her with tenderest care.</content>
<content>Her brave heart hath done wonders this day.</content>
<content>It is for us to see that her body doth not pay the penalty.</content>
<content>Here!</content>
<content>Take her rein, and go."</content>
<content>Mary Mark looked out through the wicket, in response to a knocking on the door.</content>
<content>She gasped when she saw the Lord Bishop, on foot, without the gate.</content>
<content>Quickly she opened, wide, and wider; hiding her buxom form behind the door.</content>
<content>But the Bishop had no thought for Mary Mark, nor inclination to play hide-and-seek with a conscience-stricken porteress.</content>
<content>Avoiding the front entrance, he crossed the courtyard to the right, passed beneath the rose-arch, along the yew walk, and over the lawn, to the seat under the beech, where two days before he had awaited the coming of the Prioress.</content>
<content>Here he paused for a moment, looking toward the silent cloisters, and picturing her tall figure, her flowing veil and stately tread, advancing toward him over the sunny lawn.</content>
<content>Yet no.</content>
<content>Even in these surroundings he could not see her now as Prioress.</content>
<content>Even across the Convent lawn there moved to meet him the lovely woman with jewelled girdle, white robe, and coronet of golden hair the bride of Hugh.</content>
<content>Perhaps this was the hardest moment to Symon of Worcester, in the whole of that hard day.</content>
<content>It was the one time when he thought of himself.</content>
<content>"I have lost her!"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>"Holy Jesu Thou Whose heart did break after three hours of darkness and of God-forsaken loneliness have pity!</content>
<content>The light of my life is gone from me, yet must I live."</content>
<content>Overwhelmed by this sudden realisation of loss, worn out in mind and exhausted in body, the Bishop sank upon the seat.</content>
<content>Mora was safe with Hugh.</content>
<content>That much had been accomplished.</content>
<content>For the rest, things must take their own course.</content>
<content>He could do no more go no further.</content>
<content>Then he heard again her voice in the arbour of golden roses, saying, in those low sweet tones which thrilled his very soul: "He stood to me for all that was vital and alive, in life and in religion; strong to act; able to endure."</content>
<content>During five minutes the Bishop sat, eyes closed, hands firmly clasped.</content>
<content>So still he sat, that the little Knight of the Bloody Vest, watching, with bright eyes, from the tree overhead, almost made up his mind to drop to the other end of the seat.</content>
<content>He was missing Sister Mary Antony, who had not appeared at all that morning.</content>
<content>This meant neither crumbs nor cheese, and the "little vain man" was hungry.</content>
<content>But at the end of five minutes the Bishop rose, calm and purposeful; moved firmly up the lawn, mounted the steps, and passed into the cloisters.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XXXVII

WHAT MOTHER SUB-PRIORESS KNEW

Mother Sub-Prioress had applied her eye, for the fiftieth time, to the keyhole; but naught could she see in the Prioress's cell, save a portion of the great wooden cross against the opposite wall.</content>
<content>Sister Mary Rebecca, mounted upon a stool, attempted to spy through the hole over the rope and pulley by means of which the Reverend Mother rang the Convent bell.</content>
<content>But all Sister Mary Rebecca saw, after bumping her head upon a beam, and her nose on the wall, owing to the impossibility of getting it out of the way of her eye, was a portion of the top of the Reverend Mother's window.</content>
<content>She cried out, as a great discovery, that the curtains were drawn back; upon which, Mother Sub-Prioress, exclaiming, tartly, that that had been long ago observed from the garden below, pushed the stool in her anger, and sent Sister Mary Rebecca flying.</content>
<content>Jumping to save herself, she alighted heavily on the feet of Sister Teresa, striking Mary Seraphine full in the face with her elbow, and scattering, to right and left, the crowd around the door.</content>
