<content>A few moments later he was riding out at the gates, and making for the northward road.</content>
<content>Had Brother Philip chanced to be at hand, he could not have failed to note that the swarthy stranger was mounted upon the fastest nag in the Bishop's stable.</content>
<content>For a life of lawlessness, rapine, and robbery, does not debar a man from keeping an oath sworn, out of honest gratitude, in cleaner, better days.</content>
<content>Left alone, Mora passed on to the terrace and, in the clearer light, examined this soiled and much inscribed missive.</content>
<content>To her amazement she recognised the well-known script of Symon, Bishop of Worcester.</content>
<content>How many a letter had reached her hands addressed in these neat characters.</content>
<content>Yet Hugh had left her, and gone upon this ride of many days to Worcester in order to see the Bishop, because he had received a letter telling him, without sufficient detail, a matter of importance.</content>
<content>Probably the letter she now held in her hands should have reached him first.</content>
<content>Doubtless had he received it, he need not have gone.</content>
<content>Pondering this matter, and almost unconscious that she did so, Mora broke the seals.</content>
<content>Then paused, even as she began to unfold the parchment, questioning whether to read it or to let it await Hugh's return.</content>
<content>But not long did she hesitate.</content>
<content>It was upon a matter which closely concerned her.</content>
<content>That much Hugh had admitted.</content>
<content>It might be imperative to take immediate action concerning this first letter, which by so strange a mishap had arrived after the other.</content>
<content>Unless she mastered its contents, she could not act.</content>
<content>Ascending the turret stairway, Mora stepped again on to the battlements.</content>
<content>The golden ramparts in the west had faded; but a blood-red banner still floated above the horizon.</content>
<content>The sky overhead was clear.</content>
<content>Sitting upon the seat on which she had sat while telling Hugh of old Mary Antony's most blessed and wondrous vision, Mora unfolded and read the Bishop's letter.</content>
<content>CHAPTER XLIX

TWICE DECEIVED

The blood-red banner had drooped, dipped, and vanished.</content>
<content>The sky overhead had deepened to purple, and opened starry eyes upon the world beneath.</content>
<content>Each time the silent woman, alone upon the battlements, lifted a sorrowful face to the heavens, yet another bright eye seemed to spring wide and gaze down upon her.</content>
<content>At length the whole expanse of the sky was studded with stars; the planets hung luminous; the moon, already waning, rose large and golden from behind the firs, growing smaller and more silvery as she mounted higher.</content>
<content>Mora covered her face with her hands.</content>
<content>The summer night was too full of scented sweetness.</content>
<content>The stars sang together.</content>
<content>The moon rode triumphant in the heavens.</content>
<content>In this her hour of darkness she must shut out the brilliant sky.</content>
<content>She let her face sink into her hands, and bowed her head upon her knees.</content>
<content>Blow after blow had fallen upon her from the Bishop's letter.</content>
<content>First that the Bishop himself was plotting to deceive her, and seemed to take Hugh's connivance for granted.</content>
<content>Then that she had been hoodwinked by old Mary Antony, on the evening of Hugh's intrusion into the Nunnery; that this hoodwinking was known to the Bishop, and appeared but to cause him satisfaction, tempered by a faint amusement.</content>
<content>Then the overwhelming news that Mary Antony's vision had been an imposition, devised and contrived by the almost uncannily shrewd wits of the old woman; and that the Bishop advised the Knight to praise heaven for those wits, and to beware lest any chance word of his should lead her Mora to doubt the genuineness of the vision, and to realise that she had been hocussed, hoodwinked, outwitted!</content>
<content>In fact the Bishop and her husband were to become, and to continue indefinitely, parties to old Antony's deception.</content>
<content>She now understood the full significance of the half-humorous, half-sceptical attitude adopted by the Bishop, when she recounted to him the history of the vision.</content>
<content>No wonder he had called Mary Antony a "most wise and prudent babe."</content>
<content>But even as her anger rose, not only against the Bishop, but against the old woman she had loved and trusted and who had so deceived her, she came upon the news of the death of the aged lay-sister and the account of her devoted fidelity, even to the end.</content>
<content>Mary Antony living, was often a pathetic figure; Mary Antony dead, disarmed anger.</content>
<content>And, after all, the old lay-sister and her spurious vision faded into insignificance in view of the one supreme question: What course would Hugh take?</content>
<content>Would he keep silence and thus tacitly become a party to the deception; or would he, at all costs, tell her the truth?</content>
<content>It was evidence of the change her love had wrought in her, that this one point was so paramount, that until it was settled, she could not bring herself to contemplate other issues.</content>
<content>She remembered, with hopeful comfort, his scrupulous honesty in the matter of Father Gervaise.</content>
<content>Yet wherefore had he gone to consult with the Bishop unless he intended to fall in with the Bishop's suggestions?</content>
<content>Not until she at last sought her chamber and knelt before the shrine of the Madonna, did she realise that her justification in leaving the Convent was gone, if there had been no vision.</content>
<content>"Blessed Virgin," she pleaded, with clasped hands uplifted; "I, who have been twice deceived tricked into entering the Cloister, and tricked into leaving it I beseech thee, by the sword which pierced through thine own soul also, grant me now a vision which shall be, in very deed, a VISION OF TRUTH."</content>
<content>CHAPTER L

THE SILVER SHIELD

The Bishop sat at the round table in the centre of the banqueting hall, sipping water from his purple goblet while the Knight dined.</content>
<content>They were not alone.</content>
<content>Lay-brethren, with sandalled feet, moved noiselessly to and fro; and Brother Philip stood immovable behind the Reverend Father's chair.</content>
<content>The Bishop discoursed pleasantly of many things, watching Hugh the while, and blessing the efficacy of the bath.</content>
<content>It had, undoubtedly, cleansed away much beside travel-stains.</content>
<content>The thunder-cloud had lifted from the Knight's brow; his eyes, though tired, were no longer sombre; his manner was more than usually courteous and deferential, as if to atone for the defiant brusquerie of his first appearance.</content>
<content>He listened in absolute silence to the Bishop's gentle flow of conversation; but this was a trait the Bishop had observed in him before; and, after all, a lapse into silence could be easily understood when a man had travelled far, on meagre fare, and found himself seated at a well-spread board.</content>
<content>Yet the Knight ate but sparingly of the good cheer, so lavishly provided; and the famous Italian wine, he scarce touched at all.</content>
<content>The meal over, the Bishop dismissed Brother Philip and the attendant monks, and, rising, went to his chair near the hearth, motioning the Knight to the one opposite.</content>
<content>Thus they found themselves seated again as they had sat on the night of the arrival of the Pope's messenger; save that now no fire burned upon the hearth; no candles were lighted on the table.</content>
<content>Instead, the summer sunshine poured in through open casements.</content>
<content>"Well, my dear Hugh," said the Bishop, 'suppose you now tell me the reason which brings you hither.</content>
<content>It must surely be a matter of grave importance which could cause so devoted a lover and husband to leave his bride, and go a five days' journey from her, within two weeks of the bridal day."</content>
<content>"I have come, my lord," said the Knight, speaking slowly and with evident effort, "to learn from your lips the entire truth concerning that vision which caused the Prioress of the White Ladies to hold herself free to renounce her vows, leave her Nunnery, and give herself in marriage where she had been betrothed before entering the Cloister."</content>
<content>"Tut!"</content>
<content>said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"The White Ladies have no Prioress.</content>
<content>Mother Sub-Prioress doth exercise the functions of that office until such time as the Prior and myself shall make a fresh appointment.</content>
<content>We are not here to talk of prioresses, my son, but of that most noble and gracious lady who, by the blessing of God and our Lady's especial favour, is now your wife.</content>
<content>See to it that you continue to deserve your great good fortune."</content>
<content>The Knight made no protest at the mention of our Lady; but his left hand moved to the medallion hanging by a gold chain from his neck, covered it and clasped it firmly.</content>
<content>The Bishop paused; but finding that the Knight had relapsed into silence, continued:

"So you wish the entire history of the inspired devotion of the old lay-sister, Mary Antony may God rest her soul."</content>
<content>Both men crossed themselves devoutly, as the Bishop named the Dead.</content>
<content>"Shall I give it you now, my son, or will you wait until the morrow, when a good night's rest shall fit you better to enjoy the recital?"</content>
<content>"My lord," said Hugh, "ere this sun sets, I hope to be many miles on my homeward way."</content>
<content>"In that case," said the Bishop, "I must tell you this moving story, without further delay."</content>
<content>So, beginning with her custom of counting the White Ladies by means of the dried peas, the Bishop gave the Knight the whole history of Mary Antony's share in the happenings in the Nunnery on the day of his intrusion, and those which followed; laying especial stress on her devotion to Mora, and her constant prayers to our Lady to sharpen her old wits.</content>
<content>The Bishop had undoubtedly intended to introduce into the recital somewhat more of mysticism and sublimity than the actual facts warranted.</content>
<content>But once launched thereon, his sense of humour could not be denied its full enjoyment in this first telling of the entire tale.</content>
<content>Full justice he did to the pathos, but he also shook with mirth over the ludicrous.</content>
<content>As he quoted Mary Antony, the old lay-sister's odd manner and movements could be seen; her mumbling lips, and cunning wink.</content>
<content>And here was Mother Sub-Prioress, ferret-faced and peering; and here Sister Mary Rebecca, long-nosed, flat-footed, eager to scent out and denounce wrong doing.</content>
<content>And at last the Bishop told of his talk with Mora in the arbour of golden roses; and lo, there was Mora, devout, adoring, wholly believing.</content>
<content>"Thou hast hid these things from the wise and prudent and hast revealed them unto babes"; and here, the Bishop himself, half amused, half incredulous: "An ancient babe!</content>
<content>Truly, a most wise and prudent babe."</content>
<content>Then the scene outside the Prioress's cell when the Bishop unlocked the door; the full confession and the touching death of old Mary Antony.</content>
<content>To it all the Knight listened silently, shading his face with his right hand.</content>
<content>"Therefore, my son," concluded Symon of Worcester, "when on a sudden I remembered our conversation on the lawn, and that I had told you of my belief that the old lay-sister knew of your visit to the Convent and had seen you in Mora's cell, I hastened to send you a warning, lest you should, unwittingly, mention this fact to Mora, and raise a doubt in her mind concerning the genuineness of the vision, thus destroying her peace, and threatening her happiness and your own.</content>
<content>Hath she already told you of the vision?"</content>
<content>Still shielding his face the Knight spoke, very low:

"The evening before the messenger arrived, bringing your letter, my lord, Mora told me of the vision."</content>
<content>"Said you aught concerning my words to you?"</content>
<content>"So soon as she mentioned the name of Mary Antony, I said that I seemed to recall that you, my lord, had told me she alone knew of my visit to the Convent.</content>
<content>But Mora at once said nay, that it was she herself who had told me so, even while I stood undiscovered in her cell; but that afterward the lay-sister had confessed herself mistaken.</content>
<content>This seemed to me to explain the matter, therefore I said no more; nor did I, for a moment, doubt the truth and wonder of the vision."</content>
<content>"For that, the saints be praised," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Then no harm is done.</content>
<content>You and I, alone, know the entire story; and you and I, who would safeguard Mora's happiness with our lives, must see to it that she never has cause for misgivings."</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent lifted his head, and looked full at the Bishop.</content>
<content>"My lord," he said, "had there been no vision, no message from our Lady, no placing by her of Mora's hand in mine, think you she would have left the Nunnery and come to me?"</content>
<content>"Nay, dear lad, that I know she would not.</content>
<content>On that very morning, as I told you, she set her foot upon the Pope's mandate, and would accept no absolving from her vows.</content>
<content>Naught would suffice, said she, but a direct vision and revelation from our Lady herself."</content>
<content>"But," said the Knight, slowly, "was there a vision, my lord?</content>
<content>Was there a revelation?</content>
<content>Was there a spoken message or a given sign?"</content>
<content>The Bishop met the earnest eyes, full of a deep searching.</content>
<content>He stirred uneasily; then smiled, waving a deprecatory hand.</content>
<content>"Between ourselves, my dear Hugh though even so, it is not well to be too explicit between ourselves of course nothing well miraculous happened, beyond the fact that our Lady most certainly sharpened the wits of old Antony.</content>
<content>Therefore is it, that you undoubtedly owe your wife to those same wits, and may praise our Lady for sharpening them."</content>
<content>Then it was that the Knight rose to his feet.</content>
<content>"And I refuse," he said, "to owe my wife to sacrilege, fraud, and falsehood."</content>
<content>The Bishop leaned forward, gripping with both hands the arms of his chair.</content>
<content>His face was absolutely colourless; but his eyes, like blue steel, seemed to transfix the Knight, who could not withdraw his regard from those keen points of light.</content>
<content>The Bishop's whisper, when at length he spoke, was more alarming than if he had shouted.</content>
<content>"Fool!"</content>
<content>he said.</content>
<content>"Ungrateful, unspeakable fool!</content>
<content>What mean you by such words?"</content>
<content>"Call me fool if you will, my Lord Bishop," said the Knight, 'so long as I give not mine own conscience cause to call me knave."</content>
<content>"What mean you by such words?"</content>
<content>persisted the Bishop.</content>
<content>"I mean, my lord, that if the truth opened out an abyss which plunged me into hell, I would sooner know it than attempt to enter Paradise across the flimsy fabric of a lie."</content>
<content>Now during many days, Symon of Worcester had worked incessantly, suffered much, accomplished much, surrendered much, lost much.</content>
<content>Perhaps it is hardly to be wondered at, that, at this juncture, he lost his temper.</content>
<content>"By Saint Peter's keys!"</content>
<content>he cried, "I care not, Sir Knight, whether you drop to hell or climb to Paradise.</content>
<content>But it is my business to see to it that you do not disturb the peace of mind of the woman you have wed.</content>
<content>Therefore I warn you, that if you ride from here set upon so doing, you will not reach your destination alive."</content>
<content>The Knight smiled.</content>
<content>The film of weariness lifted as if by magic from his eyes, and they shone bright and serene.</content>
<content>"I cannot draw my sword upon threats, my Lord Bishop; but let those threats take human shape, and by Saint George, I shall find pleasure in rendering a good account of them.</content>
<content>With this same sword I once did hew my way through a score of Saracens.</content>
<content>Think you a dozen Worcester cut-throats could keep me from reaching my wife?"</content>
<content>Something in the tone with which the Knight spoke these final words calmed the Bishop; something in the glance of his eye quelled the angry Prelate.</content>
<content>In the former he recognised a depth of love such as he had not hitherto believed possible to Hugh d'Argent; in the latter, calm courage, nay, a serene joy at the prospect of danger, against which his threats and fury could but break themselves, even as stormy waves against the granite rocks of the Cornish coast.</content>
<content>The Bishop possessed that somewhat rare though valuable faculty, the ability to recognise instantly, and instantly to accept, the inevitable.</content>
<content>Also when he had made a false move, he knew it, and was preparing to counteract it almost before his opponent had perceived the mistake.</content>
<content>So rarely was the Bishop angry, that his anger now affected him physically, with a sickening sense of faintness.</content>
<content>With closed eyes, he leaned his head against the back of the chair.</content>
<content>His face, always white and delicate, now appeared as if carved in ivory.</content>
<content>His lips fell apart, but no breath issued from them.</content>
<content>Except for a slight twitching of the eyelids, the Bishop's countenance was lifeless.</content>
<content>Startled and greatly alarmed, Hugh looked around for some means whereby he might summon help, but could see none.</content>
<content>Hastening to the table, he poured wine into the Venetian goblet, brought it back, and moistened the Bishop's lips.</content>
<content>Then kneeling on one knee loosed the cold fingers from their grip.</content>
<content>Presently the Bishop opened his eyes no longer points of blue steel, but soft and dreamy like a mist of bluebells on distant hills.</content>
<content>He looked, with unseeing gaze, into the anxious face on a level with his own; then turned his eyes slowly upon the ruby goblet which the Knight had lifted from the floor and was trying to hold to his lips.</content>
<content>Waving it away, the Bishop slipped the finger and thumb of his left hand into his sash, and drew out a small gold box of exquisite workmanship, set with emeralds.</content>
<content>At this he gazed for some time, as if uncertain what to do with it; then touched a spring and as the lid flew open, sat up and took from the box a tiny white tablet.</content>
<content>This he dropped into the wine.</content>
<content>The Knight, watching with anxious eyes, saw it rapidly dissolve as it sank to the bottom.</content>
<content>But all consciousness of the tablet, the wine, or the kneeling Knight, appeared to have instantly faded from the Bishop's mind.</content>
<content>He lay back gazing dreamily at a banner which, for no apparent reason, stirred and wafted to and fro, as it hung from an oaken beam, high up among the rafters.</content>
<content>"Wherefore doth it waft?"</content>
<content>murmured the Bishop, thereby adding greatly to the Knight's alarm.</content>
<content>"Wherefore?</content>
<content>Wherefore?</content>
<content>Wherefore doth it waft?"</content>
<content>"Drink this, Reverend Father," urged the Knight.</content>
<content>"I implore you, my dear lord, raise yourself and drink."</content>
<content>"Methinks there must be a draught," mused the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Yea, truly," said the Knight, "of your famous Italian wine.</content>
<content>Father, I pray you drink."</content>
<content>"Among the rafters," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>But he sat up, took the goblet from the Knight's hand, and slowly sipped its contents.</content>
<content>Almost at once, a faint tinge of colour shewed in his cheeks and on his lips; his eyes grew bright.</content>
<content>He smiled at the Knight, as he placed the empty goblet on the table beside him.</content>
<content>"Ah, my dear Hugh," he said, extending his hand; "it is good to find you here.</content>
<content>Let us continue our conversation, if you are sufficiently rested and refreshed.</content>
<content>I have much to say to you."</content>
<content>In the reaction of a great relief, Hugh d'Argent seized the extended hand and fervently kissed the Bishop's ring.</content>
<content>It was the reverent homage of a loyal heart.</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester, as with a Benedicite he graciously acknowledged it, suffered a slight twinge of conscience; almost as unusual an experience as the ebullition of temper.</content>
<content>He took up the conversation exactly at that point to which it best suited him to return, namely, there where he had made the first false step.</content>
<content>"Therefore, my dear Hugh, I have now given you in detail the true history of the vision, making it clear that we owe it, alas!</content>
<content>to earthly devotion, rather than to Divine interposition though indeed the one may well be the means used by the other.</content>
<content>It remains for us to consider, and to decide upon, the best line to take with Mora in order to safeguard most surely her peace of mind, and permanently to secure her happiness."</content>
<content>"I have considered, Reverend Father," said the Knight, simply; "and I have decided."</content>
<content>"What have you decided to do, my son?"</content>
<content>questioned Symon of Worcester, in his smoothest tones.</content>
<content>"To make known to Mora, so soon as I return, the entire truth."</content>
<content>The Bishop cast his eyes upward, to see whether the banner still waved.</content>
<content>It did.</content>
<content>Undoubtedly there must be a current of air among the rafters.</content>
<content>"And what effect do you suppose such a communication will have, my son, upon the mind of your wife?"</content>
<content>"I am not called to face suppositions, Reverend Father; I am simply confronted by facts."</content>
<content>"Precisely, my son, precisely," replied the Bishop, pressing his finger-tips together, and raising them to his lips.</content>
<content>"Yet even while dealing with causes, it is well sometimes to consider effects, lest they take us wholly unawares.</content>
<content>Do you realise that, as your wife felt justified in leaving the Nunnery and wedding you, solely by reason of our Lady's miraculously accorded permission, when she learns that that permission was not miraculous, she will cease to feel justified?"</content>
<content>"I greatly fear it," said the Knight.</content>
<content>"Do you yourself now consider that she was not justified?"</content>
<content>"Nay!"</content>
<content>answered the Knight, with sudden vehemence.</content>
<content>"Always, since I learned how we had been tricked by her sister, I have held her to be rightfully mine.</content>
<content>Heaven knew, when she made her vows, that I was faithful, and she therefore still my betrothed.</content>
<content>Heaven allowed me to discover the truth, and to find her alive, and still unwed.</content>
<content>To my thinking, no Divine pronouncement was required; and when the Holy Father's mandate arrived bringing the Church's sanction, why then indeed naught seemed to stand between us.</content>
<content>But Mora thought otherwise."</content>
<content>A tiny gleam came into the Bishop's eyes; an exceedingly refined edition of the look of cunning which used to peep out of old Mary Antony's.</content>
<content>"Have you ever heard tell, my son, that two negatives make an affirmative?</content>
<content>Think you not that, in something the same way, two deceptions may make a truth.</content>
<content>Mora was deceived into entering the Convent, and deceived into leaving it; but from out that double deception arises the great truth that she has, in the sight of Heaven, been all along yours.</content>
<content>The first deception negatives the second, and the positive fact alone remains that Mora is wedded to you, is yours to guard and shield from sorrow; and those whom God hath joined together, let no man put asunder."</content>
<content>Hugh d'Argent passed his hand across his brow.</content>
<content>"I trust the matter may appear thus to Mora," he said.</content>
<content>The banner still wafted, gently.</content>
<content>The Bishop gave himself time to ponder whence that draught could come.</content>
<content>Then: "It will not so appear," he said.</content>
<content>"My good Hugh, when your wife learns from you that she was tricked by Mary Antony, she will go back in mind to where she was before the spurious vision, and will feel herself to be still Prioress of the White Ladies."</content>
<content>"I have so felt her, since the knowledge reached me," agreed the Knight.</content>
<content>The efficacy of the soothing drug taken by the Bishop was strained to its utmost.</content>
<content>"And what then do you propose to do, my son, with this wedded Prioress?</content>
<content>Do you expect her to remain with you in your home, content to fulfil her wifely duties?"</content>
<content>"I fear," said the Knight sadly, "that she will leave me."</content>
<content>"And I am certain she will leave you," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"It was largely this fear for the future which brought me at once to you, my lord.</content>
<content>If Mora desires, as you say, to consider herself as she was, before she was tricked into leaving the Convent, will you arrange that she shall return, unquestioned, to her place as Prioress of the White Ladies of Worcester?"</content>
<content>"Impossible!"</content>
<content>said the Bishop, shortly.</content>
<content>"It is too late.</content>
<content>We can have no Madonna groups in Nunneries, saving those carven in marble or stone."</content>
<content>To which there followed a silence, lasting many minutes.</content>
<content>Then the Knight said, with effort, speaking very low: "It is not too late."</content>
<content>Instantly the keen eyes were searching his face.</content>
<content>A line of crimson leapt to the Bishop's cheek, as if a whip-lash had been drawn across it.</content>
<content>Presently: "Fool!"</content>
<content>he whispered, but the word savoured more of pitying tenderness than of scorn.</content>
<content>Alas!</content>
<content>was there ever so knightly a fool, or so foolish a knight!</content>
<content>"What was the trouble, boy?</content>
<content>Didst find that after all she loved thee not?"</content>
<content>"Nay," said Hugh, quickly, "I thank God, and our Lady, that my wife loves me as I never dreamed that such as I could be loved by one so perfect in all ways as she.</content>
<content>But at first all was so new and strange to her.</content>
<content>It was wonder enough to be out in the world once more, free to come and go; to ride abroad, looking on men and things.</content>
<content>I put her welfare first.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Nay, it was easy, loving her as I loved, also greatly desiring the highest and the best.</content>
<content>Father, I wanted what you spoke of as the Madonna in the Home.</content>
<content>Therefore 'twas I who made the plan we agreed that, the wedding having of necessity been so hurried, the courtship should follow, and we would count ourselves but betrothed, even after reaching Castle Norelle, for just so many days or weeks as she should please; until such time as she herself should tell me she was wishful that I should take her home.</content>
<content>But each day of the ride northward had been more perfect than that which went before; each hour of each day, sweeter than the preceding.</content>
<content>Thus it came to pass that on the very evening of our arrival at Mora's home, after parting for the night at the door of her chamber, we met again on the battlements, where years before we had said farewell; and there, seated in the moonlight, she told me the wonder of our Lady's grace in the vision; and, afterwards, in words of perfect tenderness, the even greater wonder of her love, and that she was ready on the morrow to ride home with me.</content>
<content>So we parted in a rapture so deep and pure, that sleep came, for very joy of it.</content>
<content>But early in the morning I was wakened by a rapping at my door, and there stood Brother Philip, holding your letter, Reverend Father."</content>
<content>"Alas!"</content>
<content>said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Would that I had known she would have whereby to explain away thy memory of that which I had said."</content>
<content>Yet the Bishop spoke perfunctorily; he spoke as one who, even while speaking, muses upon other matters.</content>
<content>For, within his secret soul, he was fighting the hardest temptation yet faced by him, in the whole history of his love for Mora.</content>
<content>By rapid transition of mind, he was back on the seat in the garden of the White Ladies' Nunnery, left there by Mary Antony while she went to fetch the Reverend Mother.</content>
<content>He was looking up the sunny lawn toward the cloisters, from out the shade of the great beech tree.</content>
<content>Presently he saw the Prioress coming, tall and stately, her cross of office gleaming upon her breast, her sweet eyes alight with welcome.</content>
<content>And at once they were talking as they always talked together he and she each word alive with its very fullest meaning; each thought springing to meet the thought which matched it.</content>
<content>Next he saw himself again on that same seat, looking up the lawn to the sunlit cloisters; realising that never again would the Prioress come to greet him; facing for the first time the utter loneliness, the irreparable loss to himself, of that which he had accomplished for Hugh and Mora.</content>
<content>The Bishop's immeasurable loss had been Hugh's infinite gain.</content>
<content>And now that Hugh seemed bent upon risking his happiness, the positions were reversed.</content>
<content>Would not his loss, if he persisted, be the Bishop's gain?</content>
<content>How easy to meet her on the road, a few miles from Worcester; to proceed, with much pomp and splendour, to the White Ladies' Nunnery; to bid them throw wide the great gates; to ride in and, then and there, reinstate Mora as Prioress, announcing that the higher service upon which the Holy Father had sent her had been duly accomplished.</content>
<content>Picture the joy in the bereaved Community!</content>
<content>But, above and beyond all, picture what it would mean to have her there again; to see her, speak with her, sit with her, when he would.</content>
<content>No more loneliness of soul, no more desolation of spirit; and Mora's conscience at rest; her mind content.</content>
<content>But at that, being that it concerned the woman he loved, the true soul of him spoke up, while his imaginative reason fell silent.</content>
<content>Never again could the woman who had told Hugh d'Argent, in words of perfect tenderness, the wonder of her love, and that she was ready on the morrow to ride home with him, be content in the calm of the Cloister.</content>
<content>If Hugh persisted in this folly of frankness and disturbed her peace, she might leave him.</content>
<content>If the Bishop made the way easy, she might return to the Nunnery.</content>
<content>But all the true life of her would be left behind with her lover.</content>
<content>She would bring to the Cloister a lacerated conscience, and a broken heart.</content>
<content>Surely the two men who loved her, if they thrust away all thought of self, and thought only of her, could save her this anguish.</content>
<content>At once the Bishop resolved to do his part.</content>
<content>"My dear Hugh," he said, "you did well to come to me in order to consult over these plans before taking the irrevocable step which should set them in motion.</content>
<content>I, alone, could reinstate your wife as Prioress of the White Ladies; moreover my continued presence here would be essential, to secure her comfort in that reinstatement.</content>
<content>And I shall not be here.</content>
<content>I am shortly leaving Worcester, leaving this land and returning to my beauteous Italy.</content>
<content>The Holy Father has been pleased to tell me privately of high preferment shortly to be offered me.</content>
<content>I have to-day decided to accept it.</content>
<content>I return to Italy a Cardinal of Holy Church."</content>
<content>Hugh rose to his feet and bowed.</content>
<content>An immense scorn blazed in his eyes.</content>
<content>"My Lord High Cardinal, I congratulate you!</content>
<content>That a cardinal's hat should tempt you from your cathedral, from this noble English city, from your people who love you, from the land of your birth, may perhaps be understood.</content>
<content>But that, for the sake of Church preferment, however high, you should willingly depart, leaving Mora in sorrow, Mora in difficulty, Mora needing your help "

The Knight paused, amazed.</content>
<content>The Bishop, who seldom laughed aloud, was laughing.</content>
<content>Yet no!</content>
<content>The Bishop, who never wept, seemed near to weeping.</content>
<content>The scales fell from Hugh's eyes, even before the Bishop spoke.</content>
<content>He realised a love as great as his own.</content>
<content>"Ah, foolish lad!"</content>
<content>said Symon of Worcester; "bent upon thine own ways, and easy to deceive.</content>
<content>When I spoke of going, I said it for her sake, hoping the prospect of my absence might hold you from your purpose.</content>
<content>But now truly am I convinced that you are bent upon risking your own happiness, and imperilling hers.</content>
<content>Therefore will I devise some means of detaining the Holy Father's messenger, so that my answer need not be given until two weeks are past.</content>
<content>You will reach Mora, at longest, five days from this.</content>
<content>As soon as she decides what she will do, send word to me by a fast messenger.</content>
<content>Should she elect to return to the Nunnery, state when and where, upon the road, I am to meet her.</content>
<content>Her habit as Prioress, and her cross of office, I have here.</content>
<content>The former you returned to me, from the hostel; the latter I found in her cell.</content>
<content>You must take them with you.</content>
<content>If she returns, she must return fully robed.</content>
<content>If, on the other hand, she should decide to remain with you; if as may God grant she is content, and requires no help from me, send me this news by messenger.</content>
<content>I can then betake myself to that fair land to which I first went for her sake; left for her sake, and to which I shall most gladly return, if her need of me is over.</content>
<content>The time I state allows a four days' margin for vacillation."</content>
<content>"My lord," said the Knight, humbly, "forgive the wrong I did you.</content>
<content>Forgive that I took in earnest that which you meant in jest; or rather,
I do truly think, that which you hoped would turn me from my purpose.</content>
<content>Alas, I would indeed that I might rightly be turned therefrom."</content>
<content>"Hugh," said the Bishop, eagerly, "you deemed her justified in coming to you, apart from any vision."</content>
<content>"True," replied the Knight, "but I cannot feel justified in taking her, and all she would give me, knowing she gives it, with a free heart, because of her faith in the vision.</content>
<content>Moments of purest joy would be clouded by my secret shame.</content>
<content>Being aware of the deception, I too should be deceiving her; I, whom she loves and trusts."</content>
<content>"To withhold a truth is not to lie," asserted the Bishop.</content>
<content>"My lord," replied Hugh d'Argent, rising to his feet and standing erect, his hand upon his sword, "I cannot reason of these things; I cannot define the difference between withholding a truth and stating a lie.</content>
<content>But when mine Honour sounds a challenge, I hear; and I ride out to do battle against myself, if need be; or, if it must so be, against another.</content>
<content>On Eastern battle-fields, in Holy War, I won a name known throughout all the camp, known also to the enemy: 'The Knight of the Silver Shield.'</content>
<content>Our name is Argent, and we ever have the right to carry a pure silver shield.</content>
<content>But I won the name because my shield was always bright; because not once in battle did it fall in the dust; because it never was allowed to tarnish.</content>
<content>So bright it was, that as I rode, bearing it before me, reflecting the rays of the sun, it dazzled and blinded the enemy.</content>
<content>My lord, I cannot tarnish my silver shield by conniving at falsehood, or keeping silence when mine Honour bids me speak."</content>
<content>Looking at the gallant figure before him, the Bishop's soul responded to the noble words, and he longed to praise them and applaud.</content>
<content>But he thought of Mora's peace of mind, Mora's awakened heart and dawning happiness.</content>
<content>For her sake he must make a final stand.</content>
<content>"My dear Hugh," he said, "all this talk, of a silver shield and of the challenge of honour, is well enough for the warrior on the battle-field.</content>
<content>But the lover has to learn the harder lesson; he has to give up Self, even the Self which holds honour dear.</content>
<content>When you polished your silver shield, keeping it so bright, what saw you reflected therein?</content>
<content>Why, your own proud face.</content>
<content>Even so, now, you fear the faintest tarnish on your sense of honour, but you will keep that silver shield bright at Mora's expense, riding on proudly alone in your glory, reflecting the sun, dazzling all beholders, while your wife who loved and trusted you, Mora, who told you the sweet wonder of her love in words of deepest tenderness, lies desolate in the dark, with a shattered life, and a broken heart.</content>
<content>Hugh, I would have you think of the treasure of her golden heart, rather than of the brightness of your own selfish, silver shield."</content>
<content>"Selfish!"</content>
<content>cried the Knight.</content>
<content>"Selfish!</content>
<content>Is it selfish to hold honour dear?</content>
<content>Is it selfish to be ashamed to deceive the woman one loves?</content>
<content>Have I, who have so striven in all things to put her welfare first, been selfish towards my wife in this hour of crisis?"</content>
<content>He sat down, heavily; leaned his elbows on his knees, and dropped his head into his hands.</content>
<content>This attitude of utter dejection filled the Bishop with thankfulness.</content>
<content>Was he, in the very moment when he had given up all hope of winning, about to prove the victor?</content>
<content>"Perilously selfish, my dear Hugh," he said.</content>
<content>"But, thank Heaven, no harm has yet been done.</content>
<content>Listen to me and I will shew you how you may keep your honour safely untarnished, yet withhold from Mora all knowledge which might cause her disquietude of mind, thus securing her happiness and your own."</content>
<content>CHAPTER LI

TWO NOBLE HEARTS GO DIFFERENT WAYS

On that same afternoon, an hour before sunset, the two men who loved
Mora faced one another, for a final farewell.</content>
<content>The Bishop had said all he had to say.</content>
<content>Without interruption, his words had flowed steadily on; eloquent, logical, conciliatory, persuasive.</content>
<content>At first he had talked to the top of the Knight's head, to the clenched hands, to the arms outstretched across the table.</content>
<content>He had wondered what thoughts were at work beneath the crisp thickness of that dark hair.</content>
<content>He had wished the rigid attitude of tense despair might somewhat relax.</content>
<content>He had used the most telling inflexions of his persuasive voice in order to bring this about, but without success.</content>
<content>He had wished the Knight would break silence, even to rage or to disagree.</content>
<content>To that end he had cast as a bait an intentional slip in a statement of facts; and, later on, a palpable false deduction in a weighty argument.</content>
<content>But the Knight had not risen to either.</content>
<content>After a while Hugh had lifted his head, and leaned back in his chair; fixing his eyes, in his turn, upon the banner hanging from the rafters.</content>
<content>It had ceased to wave gently to and fro.</content>
<content>Probably Father Benedict had closed the trap-door, concealed behind an upright beam, through which he was wont to peer down into the banqueting hall below, in order to satisfy himself that all was well and that the Reverend Father needed naught.</content>
<content>Let it be here recorded that this exceeding vigilance, on the part of Father Benedict, met with but scant reward.</content>
<content>For, having deduced a draught, and its reason, from the slight stirring of the banner during his conversation with the Knight, the Bishop gave certain secret instructions to Brother Philip, with the result that the next time the Chaplain peered down upon a private conference he found, at its close, the door by which he had gained access to the roof chamber barred on the outside, and, forcing it, he was in no better case, the ladder which connected it with another disused chamber below having been removed.</content>
<content>Thereafter Father Benedict watched the Bishop, and his guest, partake of three meals, before he could bring himself to make known his predicament, and beg to be released.</content>
<content>And, even then, the Bishop was amazingly slow in locating the place from which issued the agitated voice imploring assistance.</content>
<content>Several brethren were summoned to help; so that quite a little crowd stood gazing up at the pallid countenance of Father Benedict, framed in the trap-door as, lying upon his very empty stomach, he called down replies to the Bishop's questions; vainly striving to give a plausible reason for the peculiar situation in which he was discovered.</content>
<content>But, to return to the interview which brought about this later development.</content>
<content>The Knight had lifted his head, yet had still remained silent and impassive.</content>
<content>Where at length the Bishop had paused, awaiting comment of some kind,
Hugh d'Argent, removing his eyes from the rafters, had asked:
"When, my lord, do you propose to meet the Prioress, should my wife, upon learning the truth, elect to return to the Nunnery?"</content>
<content>Thus had the Bishop been forced to realise that the flow of his eloquence, the ripple of his humour, the strong current of his arguments, the gentle lapping of his tenderness, the breakers of his threats, and the thunderous billows of his denunciations, had alike expended themselves against the rock of the Knight's unshakable resolve, and left it standing.</content>
<content>Whereupon, in silence, the Bishop had risen, and had led the way to the library.</content>
<content>Here they now faced one another in final farewell.</content>
<content>Each knew that his loss would be the other's gain; his gain, the other's irreparable loss.</content>
<content>Yet, at that moment, each thought only of Mora's peace of soul.</content>
<content>They did but differ in their conception of the way in which that peace might best be preserved and maintained.</content>
<content>"I must take her cross of office, my Lord Bishop," said the Knight, with decision.</content>
<content>The Bishop went to a chest, standing in one corner of the room, opened it, and bent over it, his back to Hugh d'Argent; then, slipping his hand into his bosom drew therefrom a cross of gold gleaming with emeralds.</content>
<content>Shutting down the massive lid of the chest, he returned, and placed the cross in the outstretched hand of the Knight.</content>
<content>"I entrust it to you, my dear Hugh, only on one condition: that it shall without fail return to me in two weeks' time.</content>
<content>Should you decide to tell your wife the true history of the vision, I must see this cross of office upon her breast when I meet her riding back to Worcester, once more Prioress of the White Ladies.</content>
<content>If, on the other hand, wiser counsel prevails, and you decide not to tell her, you must, by swift messenger, at once return it to me in a sealed packet."</content>
<content>"I shall tell her," said the Knight.</content>
<content>"If she elects to leave me, you will see the cross upon her breast, my lord.</content>
<content>If she elects to stay, you shall receive it by swift messenger."</content>
<content>"She will leave you," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"If you tell her, she will leave you."</content>
<content>"She loves me," said the Knight; and he said it with a tender reverence, and such a look upon his face, as a man wears when he speaks of his faith in God.</content>
<content>"Hugh," said the Bishop, sadly; "Hugh, my dear lad, you have but little experience of the heart of a nun.</content>
<content>The more she loves, the more determined will she be to leave you, if you yourself give her reason to think her love unjustified.</content>
<content>The very thing which is now a cause of bliss will instantly become a cause for fear.</content>
<content>She will flee from joy, as all pure hearts flee from sin; because, owing to your folly, her joy will seem to her to be sinful.</content>
<content>My son!"</content>
<content>the Bishop stretched out his hands; a passion of appeal was in his voice "God and Holy Church have given you your wife.</content>
<content>If you tell her this thing, you will lose her."</content>
<content>"I must take with me the dress she left behind," said the Knight, 'so that, should she decide to go, she may ride back fully robed."</content>
<content>The Bishop went again to the chest, raised the heavy lid, and lifted out the white garments rolled together.</content>
<content>At sight of them both men fell silent, as in presence of the dead; and the Knight felt his heart grow cold with apprehension, as he received them from the Bishop's hand.</content>
<content>They passed together through the doorway leading to the river terrace, and so down the lawn, under the arch, and into the courtyard.</content>
<content>There Brother Philip waited, mounted, while another lay-brother held the Knight's horse.</content>
<content>As they came in sight of the horses: "Philip will see you a few miles on your way," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"I thank you, Father," replied the Knight, "but it is not needful.</content>
<content>The good Brother has had many long days in the saddle."</content>
<content>"It is most needful," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Let Philip ride beside you until you have passed through the Monk's Wood, and are well on to the open ground beyond.</content>
<content>There, if you will, you may bid him turn back."</content>
<content>"Is this to ensure the safety of the Worcester cut-throats, my lord?"</content>
<content>The Bishop smiled.</content>
<content>"Possibly," he said.</content>
<content>"Saracens may be hewn in pieces, with impunity.</content>
<content>But we cannot allow our Worcester lads rashly to ride to such a fate.</content>
<content>Also, my dear Hugh, you carry things of so great value that we must not risk a scuffle.</content>
<content>These are troublous times, and dangers lurk around the city.</content>
<content>Three miles from here you may dismiss Brother Philip, and ride forward alone."</content>
<content>Arrived at the horses, the Knight put away safely, that which he carried, into his saddle-bag.</content>
<content>Then he dropped on one knee, baring his head for the Bishop's blessing.</content>
<content>Symon of Worcester gave it.</content>
<content>Then, bending, added in low tones: "And may God and the blessed Saints aid thee to a right judgment in all things."</content>
<content>"Amen," said Hugh d'Argent, and kissed the Bishop's ring.</content>
<content>Then he mounted; and, without one backward look, rode out through the
Palace gates, closely followed by Brother Philip.</content>
<content>CHAPTER LII

THE ANGEL-CHILD

Symon of Worcester turned, walked slowly across the courtyard, made his way to the parapet above the river, and stood long, with bent head, watching the rapid flow of the Severn.</content>
<content>His eyes rested upon the very place where the Knight had cleft the
water in his impulsive dive after the white stone, made, by the
Bishop's own words, to stand to him for his chances of winning the
Prioress.</content>
<content>Yet should that sudden leap be described as "impulsive"?</content>
<content>The Bishop, ever a stickler for accuracy in descriptive words, considered this.</content>
<content>Nay, not so much "impulsive" as "prompt."</content>
<content>Even as the warrior who, having tested his trusty sword, knowing its readiness in the scabbard and the strength of his own right arm, draws, on the instant, when surprised by the enemy.</content>
<content>Prompt, not impulsive.</content>
<content>A swift action, based upon an assured certainty of power, and a steadfast determination, of long standing, to win at all costs.</content>
<content>The Bishop's hand rested upon the parapet.</content>
<content>The stone in his ring held neither blue nor purple lights.</content>
<content>Its colour had paled and faded.</content>
<content>It shone as the Prioress had once seen it shine like a large tear-drop on the Bishop's finger.</content>
<content>Deep dejection was in the Bishop's attitude.</content>
<content>With the riding away of the Knight, something strong and vital seemed to have passed out of his life.</content>
<content>A sense of failure oppressed him.</content>
<content>He had not succeeded in bending Hugh d'Argent to his will, neither had he risen to a frank appreciation of the loyal chivalry which would not enjoy happiness at the expense of honour.</content>
<content>While his mind refused to accept the Knight's code, his soul yearned to rise up and acclaim it.</content>
<content>Yet, working to the last for Mora's peace of mind, he had maintained his tone of scornful disapproval.</content>
<content>He would never again have the chance to cry "Hail!"</content>
<content>to the Silver Shield.</content>
<content>The deft fingers of his sophistry had striven to loosen the Knight's shining armour.</content>
<content>How far they had succeeded, the Bishop could not tell.</content>
<content>But, as he watched the swiftly moving river, he found himself wishing that his task had been to strengthen, rather than to weaken; to gird up and brace, rather than subtly to unbuckle and disarm.</content>
<content>Yet by so doing, would he not have been ensuring his own happiness, bringing back the joy of life to his own heart, at the expense of the two whom he had given to be each other's in the Name of the Divine Trinity?</content>
<content>If Hugh persisted in his folly, he would lose his bride, yet would the Bishop meet and reinstate the Prioress with a clear conscience, having striven to the very last to dissuade the Knight.</content>
<content>If, on the other hand, Hugh, growing wiser as he rode northward, decided to keep silence, why then the sunny land he loved, and the Cardinal's office, for Symon, Bishop of Worcester.</content>
<content>But meanwhile, two weeks of uncertainty; and the Bishop could not abide uncertainty.</content>
<content>He turned from the river and began to pace the lawn slowly from end to end, his head bent, his hands clasped behind him.</content>
<content>Each time he reached the wall between the garden and the courtyard, he found himself confronted by two rose trees, a red and a white, climbing so near together that their branches intertwined, crimson blooms resting their rich petals against the fragrant fairness of their white neighbours.</content>
<content>Presently these roses became symbolic to the Bishop the white, of the fair presence of the Prioress; the red, of the high honour awaiting him in Rome.</content>
<content>He was seized by the whimsical idea that, were he to close his eyes, beseech the blessed Saint Joseph to guide his hand, take three steps forward, and pluck the first blossom his fingers touched, he might put an end to this tiresome uncertainty.</content>
<content>But he smiled at the childishness of the fancy.</content>
<content>It savoured of the old lay-sister, Mary Antony, playing with her peas and confiding in her robin.</content>
<content>Moreover the Bishop never did anything with his eyes shut.</content>
<content>He would have slept with them open, had not Nature decreed otherwise.</content>
<content>Once again he paced the full length of the lawn, his hands clasped behind his back, his eyes looking beyond the river to the distant hills.</content>
<content>"Will she come, or shall I go?</content>
<content>Shall I depart, or will she return?"</content>
<content>As he turned at the parapet, a voice seemed to whisper with insistence:
"A white rose for her pure presence in the Cloister.</content>
<content>A red rose for
Rome."</content>
<content>And, as he reached the wall again, the bright eyes of a little maiden peeped at him through the archway.</content>
<content>He stood quite still and looked at her.</content>
<content>Never had he seen so lovely an elf.</content>
<content>A sunbeam had made its home in each lock of her tumbled hair.</content>
<content>Her little brown face had the soft bloom of a ripe nectarine; her eyes, the timid glance of a startled fawn.</content>
<content>The Bishop smiled.</content>
<content>The bright eyes lost their look of fear, and sparkled responsive.</content>
<content>The Bishop beckoned.</content>
<content>The little maid stole through the archway; then, gaining courage flew over the turf, and stood between the Bishop and the roses.</content>
<content>"How camest thou here, my little one?"</content>
<content>questioned Symon of Worcester, in his softest tones.</content>
<content>"The big gate stood open, sir, and I ran in."</content>
<content>"And what is thy name, my little maid?"</content>
<content>"Verity," whispered the child, shyly, blushing to speak her own name.</content>
<content>"Ah," murmured the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Hath Truth indeed come in at my open gate?"</content>
<content>Then, smiling into the little face lifted so confidingly to his: "Dost thou want something, Angel-child, that I can give thee?"</content>
<content>One little bare, brown foot rubbed itself nervously over the other.</content>
<content>Five little brown, bare toes wriggled themselves into the grass.</content>
<content>"Be not afraid," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Ask what thou wilt and I will give it thee, unto the half of my kingdom.</content>
<content>Yea, even the head of Father Benedict, in a charger."</content>
<content>"A rose," said the child, eagerly ignoring the proffered head of Father
Benedict and half the Bishop's kingdom.</content>
<content>"A rose from that lovely tree!</content>
<content>Their pretty faces looked at me over the wall."</content>
<content>The Bishop's lips still smiled; but his eyes, of a sudden, grew grave.</content>
<content>"Blessed Saint Joseph!"</content>
<content>he murmured beneath his breath, and crossed himself.</content>
<content>Then, bending over the little maid, he laid his hand upon the tumbled curls.</content>
<content>"Truly, my little Verity," he said, "thou shalt gather thyself a rose, and thou shall gather one for me.</content>
<content>I leave thee free to make thy choice.</content>
<content>See!</content>
<content>I clasp my hands behind me thus.</content>
<content>Then I shall turn and walk slowly up the lawn.</content>
<content>So soon as my back is turned, pluck thou two roses.</content>
<content>Fly with those little brown feet after me, and place one of the roses whichever thou wilt in my hands.</content>
<content>Then run home thyself, with the other.</content>
<content>Farewell, little Angel-child.</content>
<content>May the blessing of Bethlehem's purple hills be ever thine."</content>
<content>The Bishop turned and paced slowly up the lawn, head bent, hands clasped behind him.</content>
<content>The small bare feet made no sound on the turf.</content>
<content>But before the Bishop was half-way across the lawn, the stem of a rose was thrust between his fingers.</content>
<content>As they closed over it, a gay ripple of laughter sounded behind him, fading fleetly into the distance.</content>
<content>The Angel-child had made her choice, and had flown with her own rose, leaving the Bishop's destiny in his clasped hands.</content>
<content>Without pausing or looking round, he paced onward, gazing for a while at the sparkling water; then beyond it, to the distant woods through which the Knight was riding.</content>
<content>Presently he turned, still with his hands behind him, passed to the garden-door, left standing wide, and entered the library.</content>
<content>But not until he kneeled before the shrine of Saint Joseph did he move forward his right hand, and bring into view the rose placed therein by Verity.</content>
<content>It was many years since the Bishop had wept.</content>
<content>He had not thought ever to weep again.</content>
<content>Yet, at sight of the rose, plucked for him by the Angel-child, something gave way within him, and he fell to weeping helplessly.</content>
<content>Saint Joseph, bearded and stalwart, seemed to look down with compassion upon the bowed head with its abundant silvery hair.</content>
<content>Even thus, it may be, had he himself wept when, after his time of hard mental torture, the Angel of the Lord appeared unto him, saying: "Fear not."</content>
<content>After a while the Bishop left the shrine, went over to the deed chest, and laid the rose beside the white stone.</content>
<content>"There, my dear Hugh," he murmured; "thy stone, and my rose.</content>
<content>Truly they look well together.</content>
<content>Each represents the triumph of firm resolve.</content>
<content>Yet mine will shortly fade and pass away; while thine, dear lad, will abide forever."</content>
<content>The Bishop seated himself at his table, and sounded the silver gong.</content>
<content>A lay-brother appeared.</content>
<content>"Benedicite," said the Bishop.</content>
<content>"Request Fra Andrea Filippo at once to come hither.</content>
<content>I must have speech with him, without delay."</content>
<content>CHAPTER LIII

ON THE HOLY MOUNT

On the ninth day since Hugh's departure, the day when fast riding might make his return possible before nightfall, Mora rose early.</content>
<content>At the hour when she had been wont to ring the Convent bell, she was walking swiftly over the moors and climbing the heather-clad hills.</content>
<content>She had remembered a little chapel, high up in the mountains, where dwelt a holy Hermit, held in high repute for his saintliness of life, his wisdom in the giving of spiritual counsel, and his skill in ministering to the sick.</content>
<content>It had come to Mora, as she prayed and pondered during the night, that if she could make full confession to this holy man, he might be able to throw some clear beam of light upon the dark tangle of her perplexity.</content>
<content>This hope was strongly with her as she walked.</content>
<content>"Lighten my darkness!</content>
<content>Lead me in a plain path!"</content>
<content>was the cry of her bewildered soul.</content>
<content>It seemed to her that she had two issues to consider.</content>
<content>First: the question as to whether Hugh, guided by the Bishop, would keep silence; thus making himself a party to her deception.</content>
<content>Secondly: the position in which she was placed by the fact that she had left the Convent, owing to that deception.</content>
<content>But, for the moment the first issue was so infinitely the greater, that she found herself thrusting the second into the background, allowing herself to be conscious of it merely as a question to be faced later on, when the all-important point of Hugh's attitude in the matter should be settled.</content>
<content>She walked forward swiftly, one idea alone possessing her: that she hastened toward possible help.</content>
<content>She did not slacken speed until the chapel came into view, its grey walls glistening in the morning light, a clump of feathery rowan trees beside it; at its back a mighty rock, flung down in bygone centuries from the mountain which towered behind it.</content>
<content>From a deep cleft in this rock sprang a young oak, dipping its fresh green to the roof of the chapel; all around it, in every crack and cranny, parsley fern, hare-bells on delicate, swaying stalks, foxgloves tall and straight, and glorious bunches of purpling heather.</content>
<content>Nearby was the humble dwelling of the Hermit.</content>
<content>The door stood ajar.</content>
<content>Softly approaching, Mora lifted her hand, and knocked.</content>
<content>No voice replied.</content>
<content>The sound of her knock did but make evident the presence of a vast solitude.</content>
<content>Pushing open the door, she ventured to look within.</content>
<content>The Hermit's cell was empty.</content>
<content>The remains of a frugal meal lay upon the rough wooden table.</content>
<content>Also an open breviary, much thumbed and worn.</content>
<content>At the further end of the table, a little pile of medicinal herbs heaped as if shaken hastily from the wallet which lay beside them.</content>
<content>Probably the holy man, even while at an early hour he broke his fast, had been called to some sick bedside.</content>
<content>Mora turned from the doorway and, shading her eyes, scanned the landscape.</content>
<content>At first she could see only sheep, slowly moving from tuft to tuft as they nibbled the short grass; or goats, jumping from rock to rock, and suddenly disappearing in the high bracken.</content>
<content>But soon, on a distant ridge, she perceived two figures and presently made out the brown robe and hood of the Hermit, and a little, barefoot peasant boy, running to keep up with his rapid stride.</content>
<content>They vanished over the crest of the hill, and Mora alone in this wild solitude realised that many hours might elapse ere the Hermit returned.</content>
<content>This check to the fulfilment of her purpose, instead of disappointing her, flooded her heart with a sudden sense of relief.</content>
<content>The interior of the Hermit's cell had recalled, so vividly, the austerities of the cloistered life.</content>
<content>The Hermit's point of view would probably have been so completely from within.</content>
<content>It would have been impossible that he should comprehend the wonder the growing wonder of these days, since she and Hugh rode away from Warwick, culminating in that exquisite hour on the battlements when she had told him of the vision, whispered her full surrender, and yet he faithful and patient even then had touched her only with his glowing eyes.</content>
<content>How could a holy Hermit, dwelling alone among great silent hills, realise the tremendous force of a strong mutual love, the glow, the gladness, the deep, sweet unrest, the call of soul to soul, the throb of hearts, filling the purple night with the soft beat of angels' wings?</content>
<content>How could a holy Hermit understand the shock to Hugh, how fathom the maddening torment of suspense, the abyss of hope deferred, into which the Bishop's letter must have plunged him, coming so soon after he had said: "I ask no higher joy, than to watch the breaking of the day which gives thee to my home"?</content>
<content>But the breaking of the day had brought the stern necessity which took him from her.</content>
<content>Yet why?</content>
<content>How much was in that second letter?</content>
<content>Was it less detailed than the first?</content>
<content>Had Hugh ridden south to learn the entire truth?</content>
<content>Or had he ridden south to arrange with the Bishop for her complete and permanent deception?</content>
<content>Standing on this mountain plateau the morning breeze blowing about her, the sun mounting triumphant in the heavens "as a bridegroom coming out of his chamber," and all around the scent of heather, the hum of bees, the joyful trill of the soaring lark; her own body bounding with life after the swift climb it seemed to Mora impossible that Hugh should withstand the temptation to hold to his happiness, at all costs.</content>
<content>And how could a saintly Hermit judge him as mercifully as she the woman who loved him knew that he should be judged?</content>
<content>She felt thankful for the good man's absence, yet baffled in her need for help.</content>
<content>Looking back toward the humble dwelling, she perceived a rough device of carved lettering on a beam over the doorway.</content>
<content>She made out Latin words, and going nearer she, who for years had worked so continuously at copying and translating, read them without difficulty.</content>
<content>"WITH HIM, IN THE HOLY MOUNT," was inscribed across the doorway of the
Hermit's dwelling.</content>
<content>Mora repeated the words, and again repeated them; and, as she did so there stole over her the sense of an Unseen Presence in this solitude.</content>
<content>"With Him, in the Holy Mount."</content>
<content>She turned to the chapel.</content>
<content>Over that doorway also were carven letters.</content>
<content>Moving closer, she looked up and read them.</content>
<content>"AND WHEN THEY HAD LIFTED UP THEIR EYES, THEY SAW NO MAN, SAVE JESUS ONLY."</content>
<content>Mora opened the door and entered the tiny chapel.</content>
<content>At first, coming in from the outer brightness it seemed dark; but she had left the door standing wide, and light poured in behind her.</content>
<content>Then she lifted up her eyes and saw; and seeing, understood the meaning of the legend above the entrance.</content>
<content>In that little chapel was one Figure, and one Figure only.</content>
<content>No pictured saints were there.</content>
<content>No image of our Lady.</content>
<content>No crucifix hung on the wall.</content>
<content>But, in a niche above the altar, stood a wondrous figure of the Christ; not dying, not dead; not glorified and ascending; but the Christ as very man, walking the earth in human form, yet calmly, unmistakably, triumphantly Divine.</content>
<content>The marble form was carved by the same hand as the Madonna which the Bishop had brought from Rome, and placed in Mora's cell at the Convent.</content>
<content>It had been his gift to his old friend the Hermit.</content>
<content>At first sight of it, Mora remembered hearing it described by the Bishop himself.</content>
<content>Then the beauty of the sculpture took hold upon her, and she forgot all else.</content>
<content>It lived!</content>
<content>The face wore a look of searching tenderness; on the lips, a smile of loving comprehension; in the out-stretched hands, an attitude of infinite compassion.</content>
<content>Mora fell upon her knees.</content>
<content>Instinctively she recalled the earnest injunction of Father Gervaise to his penitents that, when kneeling before the crucifix, they should repeat: "He ever liveth to make intercession for us."</content>
<content>And, strangely enough, there came back with this the remembrance of the wild voice of Mary Seraphine, shrieking, when told to contemplate the dying Redeemer: "I want life not death!"</content>
<content>Here was Life indeed!</content>
<content>Here was the Saviour of the world, in mortal guise, the Word made manifest.</content>
<content>Mora lifted her eyes and read the words, illumined in letters of gold around the arch of the niche, gleaming in the sunlight above the patient head of the Man Divine.</content>
<content>"IN ALL POINTS TEMPTED LIKE AS WE ARE, YET WITHOUT SIN."</content>
<content>And higher still, above the arch:

"A GREAT HIGH PRIEST.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>PASSED INTO THE HEAVENS."</content>
<content>In the silence and stillness of that utter solitude, she who had so lately been Prioress of the White Ladies kneeled and worshipped.</content>
<content>The Unseen Presence drew nearer.</content>
<content>She closed her eyes to the sculptured form.</content>
<content>The touch of her Lord was upon her heart.</content>
<content>She had prayed in her cell that His pierced feet nailed to the wood might become as dear to her as the Baby feet on the Virgin Mother's knees.</content>
<content>In her anguish of cloistered sorrow, that prayer had been granted.</content>
<content>But out in the world of living men and things, she needed more.</content>
<content>She needed Feet that walked and moved, passed in and out of house and home; paused by the hearth; went to the wedding feast; moved to the fresh closed grave; Feet that had sampled the dust of life's highway; Feet that had trod rough places, yet never tripped nor stumbled.</content>
<content>"Tempted in all points."</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>Then here was One Who could understand Hugh's hard temptation; Who could pity, if Hugh fell.</content>
<content>Here was One Who would comprehend the breaking of her poor human heart if, loving Hugh as she now loved, she yet must leave him.</content>
<content>"A great High Priest."</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>.</content>
<content>What need of any other priest, while "with Him in the Holy Mount"?</content>
<content>Passed into the heavens, yet ever living to make intercession for us.</content>
<content>Deep peace stole into her heart, as she knelt in absorbed communion in this sacred place, where, for the first time, in her religious life, she had found herself with "Jesus only."</content>
<content>"Ah, blessed Lord!"</content>
<content>she cried at length, "Thou Who knowest the heart of a man, and canst divine the heart of a woman, grant unto me this day a true vision; a vision which shall make clear to me, without any possibility of doubt, what is Thy will for me."</content>
<content>CHAPTER LIV

THE UNSEEN PRESENCE

The world was a new and a wonderful world as, leaving the chapel, Mora turned her steps homeward.</content>
<content>She had been wont to regard temptation itself as sinful, but now this sacred fact "in all points tempted like as we are" seemed to sanctify the state of being tempted, providing she could add the three triumphant words: "Yet without sin."</content>
<content>As she walked, with springy step, down the grassy paths among the heather, the Unseen Presence moved beside her.</content>
<content>It seemed strange that she should have found in the world this sweet secret of the Perpetual Presence, which had evaded her in the Nunnery.</content>
<content>Often when her duties had taken her elsewhere in the Convent, or during the walk through the underground way on the return from the Cathedral, or even when walking for refreshment in the Convent garden, she would yearn for the holy stillness of the chapel, or to be back in her cell that she might kneel at the shrine of the Virgin and there realise the adorable purity of our blessed Lady's heart; or, prostrating herself before the crucifix, gaze upon those pierced feet, then slowly lift her eyes to the other sacred wounds, and force her mind to realise and her cold heart to receive the mighty fact that the Divine Redeemer thus hung and suffered for her sins.</content>
<content>Transports of realisation had come to her in her cell, or when she kept vigil in the Convent chapel, or when from the height of the Cathedral clerestory she gazed down upon the High Altar, the lighted candles, the swinging censers, and heard the chanting of the monks, and the tinkle of the silver bell.</content>
<content>But these transports had resulted from her own determination to realise and to respond.</content>
<content>The mental effort over, they faded, and her heart had seemed colder than before, her spirit more dead, her mind more prone to apathy.</content>
<content>The greater the effort to force herself to apprehend, the more complete had been the reaction of non-realisation.</content>
<content>But now, in this deep wonder of new experience, there was no effort.</content>
<content>She had but waited with every inlet of her being open to receive.</content>
<content>And now the power was a Real Presence within, revealing an equally Real Presence without.</content>
<content>The Risen Christ moved beside her as she walked.</content>
<content>Her eyes were no longer holden that she should not know Him, for the promised Presence of the Paracletos filled her, unveiling her spiritual vision, whispering within her glowing heart; "It is the Lord!"</content>
<content>"Which Voice we heard," wrote Saint Peter, "when we were with Him in the Holy Mount."</content>
<content>She, too, had first heard it there; but, as she descended, it was with her still.</content>
<content>The songs of the birds, the rush of the stream, the breeze in the pines, the bee on the wing, all Nature seemed to say: "It is the Lord!"</content>
<content>Sorrow, suffering, disillusion might await her on the plain; but, with the Presence beside her, and the Voice within, she felt strong to face them, and to overcome.</content>
<content>Noon found her in her garden, calm and serene; yet wondering, with quickening pulses, whether at nightfall or even at sunset, Hugh would ride in; and what she must say if, giving some other reason for his journey to Worcester, he deceived her as others had deceived; failed her as others had failed.</content>
<content>And wondering thus, she rose and moved with slow step to the terrace.</content>
<content>For a while she stood pondering this hard question, her eyes lifted to the distant hills.</content>
<content>Then something impelled her to turn and glance into the banqueting hall, and there on the spot where he had knelt that she might bless him at parting stood Hugh, his arms folded, his eyes fixed upon her, waiting till she should see him.</content>
<content>CHAPTER LV

THE HEART OF A WOMAN

For a space, through the casement, they looked into one another's eyes; she, standing in the full glory of the summer sunshine, a radiant vision of glowing womanhood; he, in the shade of the banqueting-hall, gaunt and travel-stained, yet in his eyes the light of that love which never faileth.</content>
<content>But, even as she looked, those dark eyes wavered, shifted, turned away, as if he could not bear any longer to gaze upon her in the sunlight.</content>
<content>An immense pity filled Mora's heart.</content>
<content>She knew he was going to fail her; yet the pathos of that failure lay in the fact that it was the very force of his love which rendered the temptation so insuperable.</content>
<content>Swiftly she passed into the banqueting hall, went to him where he stood, put up her arms about his neck, and lifted her lips to his.</content>
<content>"I thank God, my beloved," she said, "that He hath brought thee in safety back to me."</content>
<content>Hugh's arms, flung around her, strained her to him.</content>
<content>But he kept his head erect.</content>
<content>The muscles of his neck were like iron bands under her fingers.</content>
<content>She could see the cleft in his chin, the firm curve of his lips.</content>
<content>His eyes were turned from her.</content>
<content>She longed to say: "Hugh, the Bishop's first letter, lost on its way, hath reached my hands.</content>
<content>Already I know the true story of the vision."</content>
<content>Yet instead she clung to his neck, crying: "Kiss me, Hugh!</content>
<content>Kiss me!"</content>
<content>She could not rob her man of his chance to be faithful.</content>
<content>Also, if he were going to fail her, it were better he should fail and she know it, than that she should forever have the torment of questioning: "Had I not spoken, would he have kept silence?"</content>
<content>Yet, while he was still hers, his honour untarnished, she longed for the touch of his lips.</content>
<content>"Kiss me," she whispered again, not knowing how ten-fold more hard she thus made it for him.</content>
<content>But loosing his arms from around her, he took her face between his hands, looking long into her eyes, with such a yearning of hunger, grief, and regret, that her heart stood still.</content>
<content>Then, just as, rendered dizzy by his nearness, she closed her eyes, she felt his lips upon her own.</content>
<content>For a moment she was conscious of nothing save that she was his.</content>
<content>Then her mind flew back to the last time they had stood, thus.</content>
<content>Again the underground smell of damp earth seemed all about them; again her heart was torn by love and pity; again she seemed to see Hugh, passing up from the darkness into that pearly light which came stealing down from the crypt and she realised that this second kiss held also the anguish of parting, rather than the rapture of reunion.</content>
<content>Before she could question the meaning of this, Hugh released her, gently loosed her hands from about his neck, and led her to a seat.</content>
<content>Then he thrust his hand into his breast, and when he drew it forth she saw that he held something in his palm, which gleamed as the light fell upon it.</content>
<content>Standing before her, his eyes bent upon that which lay in his hand,
Hugh spoke.</content>
<content>"Mora, I have to tell thee a strange tale, which will, I greatly fear, cause thee much sorrow and perplexity.</content>
<content>But first I would give thee this, sent to thee by the Bishop with his most loving greetings; who also bids me say that if, after my tale is told, thy choice should be to return to Worcester, he himself will meet thee, and welcome thee, conduct thee to the Nunnery and there reinstate thee Prioress of the White Ladies, with due pomp and highest honour.</content>
<content>I tell thee this at once to spare thee all I can of shock and anguish in the hearing of that which must follow."</content>
<content>Kneeling before her, Hugh laid her jewelled cross of office on her lap.</content>
